Method of Learning
beginner
6 min
The Ash'ari Methodology of Imam al-Dardir
Describes and defends the Ash'ari methodology of Imam al-Dardir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core features of Imam al-Dardir's Ash'ari methodology and why it is defended as the mainstream Sunni path.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari school is the balanced Sunni mainstream, uniting reason and revelation.

This passage from Imam al-Dardir's commentary presents the methodology of the Ash'ari school, which he follows. It highlights the school's balanced approach, combining reason and transmitted knowledge, and defends it against contemporary claims of innovation. As you read, notice how the passage establishes the Ash'ari position as the path of the vast majority of Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the gist, then follow the English translation. Pay attention to the terminology used.
The passage begins by praising the work and then outlines the methodology.
2
Identify the three attribute categories
The passage mentions a threefold division of divine attributes: essential (nafsiyyah), negative (salbiyyah), and those of meanings (ma'ani). Note how these are defined in the commentary.
The author later explains why he omitted the 'attribute of meanings' type.
3
Understand the balanced methodology
The passage emphasizes that the Ash'ari methodology is a middle path, neither over-relying on reason alone nor on tradition alone. Observe the phrasing 'no excess nor deficiency'.
This balance is key to the school's identity.
4
Reflect on the defense against critics
The passage expresses astonishment at those who accuse the Ash'aris of innovation, citing the hadith about the vast majority. Consider why this defense is important in the context of Islamic theology.
5
Review the footnote on attributes
The footnote explains why al-Dardir did not include the 'attribute of meanings' type. Read it carefully to grasp the subtlety of Ash'ari attribute theology.
This shows the depth and precision of the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشتهر من بين تلك المصنفات، مجموعة من المتون والمنظومات والشروح تلقتها الأمة بالقبول، وكتب الله لها الانتشار والذيوع، كان منها شرح الإمام أحمد الدردير على منظومته المسماة بـ «الخريدة البهية في علم التوحيد وتزكية النفس» والتي جاءت في أسلوب رصين ودقة عالية في معناها ومغزاها، وقوة في صياغتها، وعمق وغزارة في بيانها ومنهجها. وقد سلك العلامة الشيخ الدردير في منظومته هذه طريق السادة الأشاعرة، أهل السنة والجماعة، ونهج منهجهم في تقسيم الصفات إلى نفسية وسلبية ومعاني، وضمنها أقسام هذا العلم من إلهيات ونبوات وسمعيات، ثم ختمها بخاتمة مهمة في التصوف وأهميته في تزكية النفس وإصلاح القلب. السادة الأشاعرة الذي سلكه الإمام الدردير في نظمه وشرحه للخريدة البهية منهج وسط معتدل، لا إفراط فيه ولا تفريط، فهم لا يُغالون ولا يتشددون، لا يعتمدون على النقل ويهملون العقل، ولا يبالغون في الاعتماد على العقل وحده دون النقل، ولكنهم يجعلون منهجهم قائما على العقل والنقل. ومذهبهم هو مذهب السواد الأعظم من أهل الإسلام في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها، وقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لا يجمع الله أمتي على ضلالة أبدا، ويد الله على الجماعة، فاتبعوا السواد الأعظم، فإنه من شذ شذ في النار» رواه الحاكم والترمذي. ومن العجب العجاب أن ظهرت في عصرنا دعوات مغرضة، تبنتها فرق وجماعات وتيارات تدعي أن عقائد السادة الأشاعرة مبتدعة خارجة عن منهج. (1) ولم يذكر العلامة الدردير الصفات المعنوية لأنه يرى أنها ليست بزائدة على المعاني، وإنما هي عبارة عن قيام المعاني بالذات وأن ثبوتها أمر ذهني فقط.
Famous among these works is a group of texts, versified works, and commentaries which the ummah accepted and to which Allah granted spread and circulation, among them is the commentary of Imam Ahmad al-Dardir on his versified work called 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah fi 'ilm al-tawhid wa tazkiyat al-nafs', which came in a solid style, high precision in its meaning and significance, strength in its formulation, and depth and abundance in its explanation and methodology. The scholar Shaykh al-Dardir followed in this versified work the path of the Ash'ari masters, the people of the Sunnah and the community, and adopted their methodology in dividing the attributes into: essential (nafsiyyah), negative (salbiyyah), and meanings (ma'ani), and included the divisions of this knowledge: divine matters (ilahiyyat), prophethood (nubuwwat), and heard matters (sam'iyyat), then concluded it with an important conclusion on Sufism and its importance in purifying the soul and reforming the heart. The Ash'ari masters whom Imam al-Dardir followed in his versified work and his commentary on al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah follow a moderate middle path, no excess nor deficiency; they do not exaggerate nor become strict; they do not rely solely on transmitted knowledge neglecting reason, nor do they exaggerate in relying on reason alone without transmission, but they base their methodology on both reason and transmission. Their school is the school of the vast majority of the people of Islam in the East and West of the earth, and the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Allah will not unite my ummah on error ever, and Allah's hand is with the community, so follow the vast majority, for whoever deviates deviates to the Fire' narrated by al-Hakim and al-Tirmidhi. And it is a great wonder that in our time biased calls have appeared, adopted by groups and factions and movements, claiming that the beliefs of the Ash'ari masters are innovations outside the [proper] methodology. (1) The scholar al-Dardir did not mention the attributes of meanings (ma'nawiyyah) because he holds that they are not additional to the meanings (ma'ani), but rather they are an expression of the subsistence of the meanings in the essence and that their affirmation is only a mental matter.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage introduces Imam al-Dardir's commentary on his own versified work, praising its style and depth. It then describes his methodology as following the Ash'ari school, dividing attributes into essential, negative, and those of meanings, and organizing the work into sections on divine matters, prophethood, and heard matters, with a conclusion on Sufism. The passage emphasizes that the Ash'ari methodology is a balanced middle path, combining reason and revelation without excess or deficiency, and that it is the school of the vast majority of Muslims. A hadith is cited to support following the majority. The passage then expresses astonishment at contemporary movements that accuse the Ash'ari school of being innovative. A footnote explains why al-Dardir did not include the 'attribute of meanings' (ma'nawiyyah) type, stating that he considered them not additional but merely mental affirmations of the attributes of meanings. The passage provides a concise statement of the Ash'ari methodology and defends it against criticism.

Key Terms
السادة الأشاعرة
أهل السنة والجماعة
نفسية
سلبية
معاني
إلهيات
نبوات
سمعيات
السواد الأعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some groups claim that the beliefs of the Ash'ari school are innovations (bid'ah) and outside proper Islamic methodology.
Correction: The passage argues that the Ash'ari school follows the moderate path of Ahl al-Sunnah, is the school of the vast majority of Muslims, and is supported by a hadith commanding adherence to the majority. Thus, it is not an innovation but the orthodox mainstream.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis of the Ash'ari methodology regarding reason and transmitted knowledge?
A) They give precedence to transmitted knowledge over reason.
B) They give precedence to reason over transmitted knowledge.
C) They base their methodology on both reason and transmitted knowledge.
D) They rely solely on transmitted knowledge and ignore reason.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari methodology, as followed by Imam al-Dardir, is the balanced path of Ahl al-Sunnah, combining reason and revelation, and is the position of the vast majority of Muslims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been tempted to rely solely on your intellect when matters of faith seem difficult, rather than submitting to revelation? What specific step can you take this week to balance reason with the transmitted teachings of the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The passage defends the Ash'ari school as the mainstream, but many today dismiss it as innovation. In your own heart, are you influenced by such modern calls? Read the hadith about the vast majority again; does your position align with the mainstream traditional Sunni community, or have you deviated? If you are part of a minority group that claims to be the only saved sect, how do you reconcile this with the Prophet's warning?
?
Imam al-Dardir included a section on tazkiyah al-nafs (soul purification) in his work on tawhid. Are you actively working on purifying your soul, or do you focus only on doctrine? What one habit can you start today to reform your heart, knowing that correct belief must lead to righteous action?

Introduction to al-Kharīdat al-Bahiyyah and the Ashʿarī Methodology
Describes the popularity of the book and the author's Ash'ari methodology in dividing attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope and method of Imam al-Dardīr's commentary, including his Ashʿarī division of divine attributes and the work's tripartite structure.

Core Idea
The foundation of Islamic theology begins with a proper division of Allah's attributes.

This lesson introduces Imam al-Dardīr's celebrated commentary on his theological poem. We will see how the author follows the Ashʿarī school in categorizing divine attributes into essential, negative, and entitative, and how he organizes the work into major theological topics. Understanding this framework is key to navigating the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage to understand the book's context
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the work's popularity and the author's reputation. Notice the emphasis on the ummah's acceptance.
2
Identify the three categories of attributes
Look for the division into nafsīyah (essential), salbīyah (negative), and maʿānī (entitative) attributes. Note that these form the core of Ashʿarī theology.
The author omits the fourth category (maʿnawiyyah) deliberately.
3
Examine the work's structure
Find the three major sections: ilāhiyyāt, nubuwwāt, and samʿiyyāt. Also note the concluding chapter on Sufism and soul purification.
This structure shows the comprehensive scope of the text.
4
Reflect on the omission of maʿnawiyyah
Understand why al-Dardīr did not include maʿnawiyyah attributes: he considered them not additional realities but mere mental affirmations of the maʿānī. This is a nuanced Ashʿarī position.
Avoid the misconception that this was an oversight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واشتهر من بين تلك المصنفات، مجموعة من المتون والمنظومات والشروح تلقتها الأمة بالقبول، وكتب الله لها الانتشار والذيوع، كان منها شرح الإمام أحمد الدردير على منظومته المسماة بـ «الخريدة البهية في علم التوحيد وتزكية النفس والتي جاءت في أسلوب رصين ودقة عالية في معناها ومغزاها، وقوة في صياغتها، وعمق وغزارة في بيانها ومنهجها. وقد سلك العلامة الشيخ الدردير في منظومته هذه طريق السادة الأشاعرة، أهل السنة والجماعة، ونهج منهجهم في تقسيم الصفات إلى نفسية وسلبية ومعاني)، وضمنها أقسام هذا العلم من إلهيات ونبوات وسمعيات، ثم ختمها بخاتمة مهمة في التصوف وأهميته في تزكية النفس وإصلاح القلب. ولم يذكر العلامة الدردير الصفات المعنوية لأنه يرى أنها ليست بزائدة على المعاني، وإنما هي عبارة عن قيام المعاني بالذات وأن ثبوتها أمر ذهني فقط.
It became famous among these works, a collection of texts (mutūn), poems (manẓūmāt), and commentaries (shurūḥ) which the ummah accepted, and Allah decreed for them spread and notoriety. Among them was the commentary of Imam Ahmad al-Dardīr on his poem called 'al-Kharīdat al-Bahiyyah fī ʿIlm al-Tawḥīd wa Tazkiyat al-Nafs,' which came in a solid style, high precision in its meaning and significance, strength in its formulation, and depth and abundance in its explanation and methodology. And the scholar Shaykh al-Dardīr followed in this poem the way of the noble Ashʿarīs, Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jamāʿah, and followed their methodology in dividing the attributes into nafsīyah (essential), salbīyah (negative), and maʿānī (entitative), and included in it the divisions of this science: ilāhiyyāt (divine matters), nubūwāt (prophethood), and samʿiyyāt (audible matters). Then he concluded it with an important conclusion on Sufism and its importance in tazkiyat al-nafs (purification of the soul) and islāḥ al-qalb (reform of the heart). And al-Dardīr did not mention the maʿnawiyyah attributes because he holds that they are not additional to the maʿānī, but rather they are an expression of the subsistence of the maʿānī in the essence and that their affirmation is purely mental.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage introduces the book and the author's methodology. It first notes that the commentary on al-Kharīdat al-Bahiyyah is one of many widely accepted works. The author then states that Imām al-Dardīr followed the Ashʿarī school of Ahl al-Sunnah, specifically in dividing the divine attributes into three categories: nafsīyah (essential attributes that are not negated, like existence), salbīyah (negative attributes that negate imperfection), and maʿānī (entitative attributes that subsist in the essence, like power). He also organized the work into sections on ilāhiyyāt (matters pertaining to God), nubūwāt (prophethood), and samʿiyyāt (matters known only through revelation). The work ends with a chapter on Sufism, emphasizing the purification of the soul and reform of the heart. The footnote explains why al-Dardīr omitted the maʿnawiyyah attributes (like being powerful or willing): he considers them not additional realities but merely mental affirmations of the maʿānī attributes. This is a nuanced Ashʿarī position that avoids multiplying the divine attributes beyond what is necessary.

Key Terms
الخريدة البهية
الصفات النفسية
الصفات السلبية
الصفات المعاني
الصفات المعنوية
الإلهيات
النبوات
السمعيات
تزكية النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author's omission of maʿnawiyyah attributes is an oversight or error.
Correction: Al-Dardīr omitted them deliberately based on the Ashʿarī position that maʿnawiyyah are not additional attributes but mental concepts derived from the maʿānī.

Checkpoint
Why did Imam al-Dardīr not mention the maʿnawiyyah attributes in his poem?
A) He forgot to include them.
B) He considers them not additional to the maʿānī attributes, but merely mental affirmations.
C) He rejected the Ashʿarī division of attributes.
D) He considered them unnecessary for the common believer.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Dardīr's commentary follows the Ashʿarī tradition by dividing attributes into three categories and structuring the work around ilāhiyyāt, nubuwwāt, and samʿiyyāt, with a unique emphasis on spiritual purification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on Allah's attributes, do you ever reduce them to mere concepts? How does al-Dardīr's classification challenge you to deepen your understanding of who Allah is? What one change will you make in your study of tawḥīd to move from intellectual acknowledgment to heartfelt connection?
?
The passage highlights that al-Dardīr included a chapter on Sufism and purification of the soul. In your own pursuit of knowledge, do you prioritize both theology and spiritual purification, or do you neglect one? What step will you take this week to integrate tazkiyah into your study of tawḥīd?
?
Al-Dardīr deliberately omitted the maʿnawiyyah attributes because he saw them as mental affirmations. Have you ever held a belief about Allah that was merely intellectual without it affecting your heart? How can you transform a mental affirmation into a living reality that reshapes your worship and daily life?

The Ash'ari Methodology: A Balanced Middle Path
Defines Ash'ari methodology as moderate and balanced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school is described as a balanced methodology that combines reason and revelation, avoiding extremes.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari path is a middle way between blind traditionalism and pure rationalism.

Imam al-Dardir begins by defining the Ash'ari methodology, which he follows in his poem and commentary. He emphasizes that it is a moderate, balanced approach that neither neglects reason nor over-relies on it, but integrates both with transmitted texts. This introduction sets the stage for understanding the distinctiveness of the Ash'ari school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noticing key terms like 'وسط معتدل' (moderate and balanced), 'إفراط' (excess), and 'تفريط' (negligence). Let the author's words set the tone.
The passage is definitional; every word matters.
2
Identify the two extremes rejected
The passage explicitly rejects two extremes: overreliance on transmitted texts (naql) while neglecting reason, and overreliance on reason alone without transmitted texts. List each in your own words.
This clarifies what the Ash'ari middle path avoids.
3
Reflect on the balanced integration
Consider how the Ash'ari methodology intends to combine both reason and transmitted texts. Why is this balance important in theology?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادة الأشاعرة الذي سلكه الإمام الدردير في نظمه وشرحه للخريدة البهية منهج وسط معتدل، لا إفراط فيه ولا تفريط، فهم لا يُغالون ولا يتشددون، لا يعتمدون على النقل ويهملون العقل، ولا يبالغون في الاعتماد على العقل وحده دون النقل، ولكنهم يجعلون منهجهم قائما على العقل والنقل. ومذهبهم هو مذهب السواد الأعظم من أهل الإسلام في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها، وقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (لا يجمع الله أمتي على ضلالة أبدا، ويد الله على الجماعة، فاتبعوا السواد الأعظم، فإنه من شذ شذ في النار ) . رواه الحاكم في المستدرك بسنده عن ابن عمر رضي الله لهما، ورواه الترمذي في جامعه [كتاب الفتن، باب ما جاء في لزوم الجماعة ] بلفظ : ( إن الله لا يجمع أمتي أو قال أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم على ضلالة ويد الله مع الجماعة، ومن شذ شذ إلى النار).
The noble Ashʿarīs, whom Imam al-Dardīr followed in his poem and commentary on al-Kharīdat al-Bahiyyah, have a moderate and balanced methodology, with no excess nor negligence. They do not exaggerate nor become strict. They do not rely solely on transmitted texts (naql) and neglect reason (ʿaql), nor do they over-rely on reason alone without transmitted texts. Rather, they make their methodology based on both reason and transmitted texts. Their madhhab is the madhhab of the vast majority (al-sawād al-aʿẓam) of the people of Islam in the east and west of the earth. And the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Allah will never unite my ummah upon error, and the hand of Allah is with the congregation. So follow the vast majority, for whoever deviates deviates to the Fire.' It was narrated by al-Ḥākim in al-Mustadrak with his chain from Ibn ʿUmar, may Allah be pleased with them both, and al-Tirmidhī narrated it in his Jāmiʿ [Book of Tribulations, Chapter on Holding Fast to the Congregation] with the wording: 'Allah will not unite my ummah — or he said the ummah of Muḥammad — upon error, and the hand of Allah is with the congregation, and whoever deviates deviates to the Fire.'
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage defines the Ashʿarī methodology as a middle path. It explicitly rejects two extremes: (1) over-reliance on transmitted tradition while neglecting rational inquiry, and (2) overemphasis on reason alone without textual guidance. Instead, Ashʿarīs ground their method in both reason and revelation, avoiding excess (ifrāṭ) and neglect (tafrīṭ). The passage then asserts that this madhhab is followed by the overwhelming majority of Muslims worldwide, citing a hadith that the Prophet assured that the community will never agree on an error, and that one should follow the majority (sawād aʿẓam). The footnote provides the hadith’s sources from al-Ḥākim and al-Tirmidhī, reinforcing its authenticity. This teaching is important for establishing that the Ashʿarī school is not an innovation but the mainstream orthodox position.

Key Terms
منهج وسط معتدل
العقل والنقل
إفراط
تفريط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari school is purely rationalist, relying only on reason.
Correction: This passage shows that Ash'aris base their methodology on both reason and transmitted texts, explicitly rejecting overreliance on reason alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Ash'ari methodology reject?
A) The use of reason entirely
B) The use of transmitted texts entirely
C) Both extremes of neglecting reason or neglecting transmitted texts
D) The combination of reason and transmitted texts

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school is defined by its balanced integration of reason and transmitted texts, avoiding two extremes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult theological question, do you tend to rely more on what scholars have said or on your own reasoning? Which extreme are you closer to, and how can this lesson challenge you to seek a balance?
?
Think of a belief you hold firmly. Is it built on both revealed texts and sound reason, or is it fragile because you have neglected one of these foundations? What will you do this week to strengthen the weaker pillar?
?
The Ash'aris avoided both 'excess' and 'negligence'. In your own practice of Islam, where do you see yourself falling into either extreme—maybe being too rigid in following texts without understanding, or too loose in using reason to justify anything? What is one concrete change you can make today to move toward the middle path?

The Ash'ari School as the Mainstream and the Hadith of Consensus
Claims Ash'ari school is the mainstream and cites consensus hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hadith about the ummah's consensus supports the Ash'ari claim to represent the mainstream Sunni majority.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari school follows the overwhelming majority, protected from error by the Prophet's promise.

Having established the balanced methodology, Imam al-Dardir now connects it to the broader Muslim community. He asserts that the Ash'ari madhhab is that of the vast majority (sawad a'zam) of Muslims worldwide, and cites a well-known hadith in which the Prophet assures that his community will never agree on an error. This provides a powerful argument for the authority of the Ash'ari school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note 'السواد الأعظم'
Read the Arabic passage, paying attention to the phrase 'السواد الأعظم' (the vast majority). This is a key term for the mainstream.
2
Understand the hadith's meaning
The Prophet says Allah will not unite the ummah on error, and that one should follow the majority. Reflect on how this hadith is used to support following the Ash'ari school.
The hadith is cited from al-Hakim and al-Tirmidhi.
3
Consider the argumentative function
Why does the author link the Ash'ari school to the 'sawad a'zam' and then quote the hadith? What does this imply about the school's orthodoxy?
This connects the methodology lesson to broader authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادة الأشاعرة الذي سلكه الإمام الدردير في نظمه وشرحه للخريدة البهية منهج وسط معتدل، لا إفراط فيه ولا تفريط، فهم لا يُغالون ولا يتشددون، لا يعتمدون على النقل ويهملون العقل، ولا يبالغون في الاعتماد على العقل وحده دون النقل، ولكنهم يجعلون منهجهم قائما على العقل والنقل. ومذهبهم هو مذهب السواد الأعظم من أهل الإسلام في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها، وقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (لا يجمع الله أمتي على ضلالة أبدا، ويد الله على الجماعة، فاتبعوا السواد الأعظم، فإنه من شذ شذ في النار ) . رواه الحاكم في المستدرك بسنده عن ابن عمر رضي الله لهما، ورواه الترمذي في جامعه [كتاب الفتن، باب ما جاء في لزوم الجماعة ] بلفظ : ( إن الله لا يجمع أمتي أو قال أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم على ضلالة ويد الله مع الجماعة، ومن شذ شذ إلى النار).
The noble Ashʿarīs, whom Imam al-Dardīr followed in his poem and commentary on al-Kharīdat al-Bahiyyah, have a moderate and balanced methodology, with no excess nor negligence. They do not exaggerate nor become strict. They do not rely solely on transmitted texts (naql) and neglect reason (ʿaql), nor do they over-rely on reason alone without transmitted texts. Rather, they make their methodology based on both reason and transmitted texts. Their madhhab is the madhhab of the vast majority (al-sawād al-aʿẓam) of the people of Islam in the east and west of the earth. And the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Allah will never unite my ummah upon error, and the hand of Allah is with the congregation. So follow the vast majority, for whoever deviates deviates to the Fire.' It was narrated by al-Ḥākim in al-Mustadrak with his chain from Ibn ʿUmar, may Allah be pleased with them both, and al-Tirmidhī narrated it in his Jāmiʿ [Book of Tribulations, Chapter on Holding Fast to the Congregation] with the wording: 'Allah will not unite my ummah — or he said the ummah of Muḥammad — upon error, and the hand of Allah is with the congregation, and whoever deviates deviates to the Fire.'
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage defines the Ashʿarī methodology as a middle path. It explicitly rejects two extremes: (1) over-reliance on transmitted tradition while neglecting rational inquiry, and (2) overemphasis on reason alone without textual guidance. Instead, Ashʿarīs ground their method in both reason and revelation, avoiding excess (ifrāṭ) and neglect (tafrīṭ). The passage then asserts that this madhhab is followed by the overwhelming majority of Muslims worldwide, citing a hadith that the Prophet assured that the community will never agree on an error, and that one should follow the majority (sawād aʿẓam). The footnote provides the hadith’s sources from al-Ḥākim and al-Tirmidhī, reinforcing its authenticity. This teaching is important for establishing that the Ashʿarī school is not an innovation but the mainstream orthodox position.

Key Terms
السواد الأعظم
لا يجمع الله أمتي على ضلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the majority opinion is irrelevant or that following the majority is not required.
Correction: This passage cites a hadith that explicitly commands following the sawad a'zam (vast majority) and warns that deviation from it leads to the Fire.

Checkpoint
What does the hadith quoted in this passage say about the ummah?
A) Allah will always guide it through a small group
B) Allah will never unite it on error
C) The majority will be punished
D) Only the scholars are safe

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school's claim to represent the mainstream is supported by a hadith promising protection from collective error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet ﷺ explicitly warns that whoever deviates from the vast majority deviates to the Fire, how does your current set of beliefs align with the overwhelming mainstream of Muslims across history? Have you ever felt a pull towards a minority view? What would you do if you found yourself outside the sawad al-a'zam?
?
Do you truly trust that the majority of Muslims are protected from error, or do you harbor suspicion that the mainstream has gone astray? Examine your heart: are you attached to the community or to your own opinions?
?
The hadith says 'the hand of Allah is with the congregation.' In your daily life, are you actively seeking unity with the Muslim community, or do you isolate yourself in your own understanding? What practical step can you take today to align yourself more closely with the mainstream of Ahl al-Sunnah?

Introduction to al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah
Al-Dardir introduces himself and his creedal poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the purpose and structure of al-Dardir's creedal poem and its significance as a concise summary of Sunni theology.

Core Idea
A small poem can contain the cream of Islamic creed.

In this opening passage, Ahmad al-Dardir introduces himself and his celebrated poem, al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah. He sets the tone with praise of God and blessings on the Prophet, highlighting his Sunni orientation. Then he explains why this small poem is a powerful tool for learning creed. Let's read the text and unpack its layers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the poet's humble tone and rhythmic prose.
Notice the repeated phrase 'لا سيما' emphasizing the companion in the cave.
2
Identify the four parts of the introduction
Look for: author identification, praise of God (hamd), blessings on the Prophet and his companion (salawat), and description of the poem and its purpose.
Each part serves a distinct rhetorical function.
3
Reflect on the poet's hope
The poem ends with a prayer for acceptance, benefit, and forgiveness. Ask yourself: Why does a scholar start a work of theology with such a personal hope?
This shows the fusion of knowledge and spirituality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الناظم
يقول راجي رحمة القدير » أي أحمد المشهور بالدردير
الحمد لله العلي الواحد * العالم الفرد الغني الماجد
وأفضل الصلاة والتسليم على النبي المصطفى الكريم
الأطهار * لا سيما لا سيما رفيقه في الغار
وآله وصحبه الأطهار
وهذه عقيدة سنية * سميتها الخريدة البهية
لطيفة صغيرة في الحجم * لكنها كبيرة في العلم
تكفيك علما إن ترد أن تكتفي * لأنها بزبدة الفن تفي
والله أرجو في قبول العمل * والنفع منها ثم غفر الزلل
Introduction of the poet: He says, hoping for the mercy of the Almighty, 'I am Ahmad known as al-Dardir.' Praise be to God, the High, the One, the All-Knowing, the Unique, the Self-Sufficient, the Glorious. And the best of prayers and peace be upon the chosen noble Prophet, the pure ones, especially, especially his companion in the cave, and his family and his pure companions. And this is a noble creed; I have named it al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah. Delicate, small in size, but great in knowledge. It suffices you with knowledge if you wish to be content, because it fulfills with the cream of the discipline. And I hope from God for acceptance of the deed, benefit from it, and then forgiveness of slips.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to his poem. It begins with the poet identifying himself as Ahmad al-Dardir, seeking divine mercy. He then praises God with seven attributes: al-Ali (the High), al-Wahid (the One), al-Alim (the All-Knowing), al-Fard (the Unique), al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient), al-Majid (the Glorious). This shows the theological focus on divine attributes. Then he sends blessings on the Prophet Muhammad and specifically mentions his companion Abu Bakr as 'rafiqahu fi al-ghar' (his companion in the cave), indicating the author's Ash'ari/Sunni orientation honoring the Companions. He then states the title of the poem: 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah', meaning 'the splendid virgin pearl'. He describes it as a noble creed, small in volume but rich in knowledge, and expresses hope that it will suffice the seeker of knowledge as it contains the essence of the discipline (zubdat al-fann). Finally, he expresses hope for divine acceptance and forgiveness.

عقيدة سنية
زبدة الفن

D) Al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah; only covers divine attributes

Key Takeaway
Al-Dardir's introduction shows that his poem, though small, is a comprehensive summary of Sunni creed, intended to suffice the seeker and earn divine reward.

?
The poem is called 'the splendid pearl' — how valuable is this creed to you? Will you memorize it and live by it, or will you treat it as just another text to pass? What practical change in your daily life would prove that you treasure it?

Spiritual Foundations: Remembrance, Fear, and Repentance
The author gives initial spiritual advice on dhikr, fear, repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key spiritual practices that form the foundation of the Sufi path: frequent dhikr with adab, balancing fear and hope, and renewing repentance with gratitude and patience.

Core Idea
Remembrance with propriety, fear overcoming hope, and constant repentance lead to spiritual ascent.

This passage opens the conclusion on Sufism, providing essential advice for the spiritual seeker. The author emphasizes that remembrance of Allah (dhikr) must be performed with proper etiquette (adab) to achieve elevation. He then instructs that spiritual journeying requires that fear of Allah outweigh hope, and that one must constantly renew repentance without despairing of Allah's mercy. The passage also commands gratitude for blessings and patience in trials.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the sequence of commands the author gives.
The passage uses imperative verbs (أكثرت, غلب, جدد, كن) to convey guidance.
2
Identify the four main practices
The passage lists four key practices: frequent dhikr with adab, letting fear overcome hope, renewing repentance, and being grateful and patient. Identify each one in the text.
The fourth practice is two-fold: gratitude for favors and patience in trials.
3
Reflect on the balance between fear and hope
The author says 'let fear overcome hope.' Consider how this relates to the Islamic concept of having both fear and hope, and why the author prioritizes fear in this context.
This is a common point of discussion in Sufism; the fear here is reverential awe of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في التصوف
فأكثرت من ذكرها بالأدب * ترقى بهذا الذكر أعلى الرتب
وغلب الخوف على الرجاء * وسر لمولاك بلا تناء
وجدد التوبة للأوزار * لا تيأسن من رحمة الغفار
وكن على آلائه شكورا * وكن على بلائه صبورا
فكل أمر بالقضاء والقدر * وكل مقدور فما عنه مفر
فكن له مسلما كي تسلما * واتبع سبيل الناسكين العلماء
وخلص القلب من الأغيار * بالجد والقيام في الأسحار
والفكر والذكر على الدوام * مجتنبا لسائر الآثام
مراقبا لله في الأحوال * لترتقي معالم الكمال
وقل: رب لا تقطعني * عنك بقاطع ولا تحرمني
من سرك الأبهى المزيل للعمى * واختم بخير يا رحيم الرحما
والحمد لله على الإتمام * وأفضل الصلاة والسلام
على النبي الهاشمي الخاتم * وآله وصحبه الأكارم
Conclusion on Sufism (taṣawwuf). So you have frequented its remembrance with propriety; by this remembrance you ascend to the highest ranks. And let fear overcome hope, and journey to your Lord without distance. And renew repentance for sins; do not despair of the mercy of the Oft-Forgiving. And be grateful for His favors, and be patient with His trials. For every matter is by the decree and predestination (qaḍāʾ wa-l-qadar), and every destined thing has no escape from it. So submit to Him that you may be safe, and follow the path of the devout scholars. And purify the heart from other than Him, with exertion and standing in the pre-dawn hours. And constant thought and remembrance, avoiding all sins. Watching God in all states, so that you may ascend to the stations of perfection. And say: O Lord, do not cut me off from You by any cutter, and do not deprive me of Your most glorious secret that removes blindness; and seal with goodness, O Most Merciful of the merciful. And praise be to God for completion, and the best of prayers and peace be upon the Prophet, the Hashimi seal, and his family and noble companions.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion on Sufism (taṣawwuf) from the poem, providing spiritual counsel and ending with a prayer. The author advises frequenting the remembrance (dhikr) of God with proper adab, which elevates one to the highest ranks. He counsels that fear (khawf) should overcome hope (rajāʾ) in one's spiritual journey, and to travel to one's Lord without separation (tanāʾī). One should renew repentance (tawba) for sins and never despair of God's mercy. Gratitude for blessings and patience in trials are enjoined, since everything occurs by divine decree and predestination (qaḍāʾ wa-l-qadar), and nothing can escape the destined. The seeker is told to submit to God for safety, follow the path of devout scholars (nāsikīn al-ʿulamā), purify the heart from distractions (aghyār) through exertion and standing in the pre-dawn hours (asḥār), and to engage in constant thought (fikr) and dhikr while avoiding all sins. The seeker should be watchful of God in all states to ascend to stations of perfection (kamāl). Finally, a supplication is taught: 'O Lord, do not cut me off from You by any cutter, and do not deprive me of Your most glorious secret that removes blindness; and seal with goodness, O Most Merciful of the merciful.' The passage ends with praise to God and blessings on the Prophet and his family and companions.

الذكر
الأدب
الخوف
الرجاء
التوبة
الأوزار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Sufism teaches despair or excessive fear that negates hope in Allah's mercy.
Correction: The passage balances fear with repentance and hope; it commands renewing repentance and not despairing of mercy, so fear is coupled with optimism.

Checkpoint

A) Lower ranks
B) The highest ranks
C) Worldly success
D) Escape from trials

Key Takeaway
The foundation of the spiritual path lies in proper remembrance, a balanced fear of Allah, constant repentance, and grateful patience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When was the last time you renewed your repentance with a genuine intention to abandon that sin? Are you delaying because you doubt Allah's mercy? The passage commands: 'Do not despair of the mercy of the Oft-Forgiving.'

Accepting Divine Decree and Purifying the Heart
The author discusses divine decree and prescribes spiritual disciplines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author links belief in divine decree (qadr) to active spiritual practices such as submission, following scholars, and purifying the heart through constant remembrance and vigilance.

Core Idea
Submit to Allah's decree, follow scholars, purify your heart through night worship and constant dhikr, and watch Allah in all states.

After grounding the seeker in foundational practices, the author now turns to the theological framework of divine decree (qaḍāʾ wa-l-qadar). He asserts that everything happens by Allah's decree and that nothing can escape it, so the proper response is submission. From there, he instructs the seeker to follow the path of devout scholars, purify the heart from distractions through exertion and pre-dawn worship, engage in constant thought and remembrance, avoid sins, and maintain vigilance over Allah in all states. These are the means to ascend the stations of perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the cause-and-effect logic: because of qadar, submit; then take actions.
2
Identify the components of the spiritual program
The passage lists several actions: submit, follow scholars, purify heart, night worship, constant fikr and dhikr, avoid sins, and watch Allah. List them.
These are all meant to be undertaken while recognizing divine decree.
3
Connect decree to action
Reflect on how belief in qadar does not lead to fatalism but to active effort in spiritual disciplines.
The author commands submission followed by active striving, showing that divine decree and human effort are complementary.

The Seeker's Supplication and Closing Blessings
The author presents a supplication and closing praises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the content and significance of the closing supplication and praises in this conclusion.

Core Idea
The seeker's ultimate plea is to never be cut off from Allah and to be granted His glorious secret, while ending with praise and blessings.

The final portion of the conclusion shifts from instruction to supplication. The author teaches a prayer asking Allah to never let anything sever one's connection with Him and to bestow the most exalted secret that removes spiritual blindness. This is followed by praise to Allah for completing the poem and sending blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad and his family and companions. These lines encapsulate the seeker's ultimate hopes and gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the supplication
Read the Arabic supplication and translation. Understand what the seeker is asking for: not to be cut off, and to be granted the glorious secret.
The phrase 'السر الأبهى' refers to a high spiritual mystery.
2
Reflect on the meaning of the secret
Consider what 'the most glorious secret that removes blindness' might mean—perhaps direct knowledge of Allah that removes ignorance.
This is a deep concept; for now, understand it as a state of close proximity to Allah.
3
Appreciate the closing praises
Notice the author ends with praise to Allah for completion and salawat on the Prophet and his companions. This is a typical way to conclude a work.
The blessings (salat and salam) are a request for mercy and peace.

The seeker's ultimate desire is uninterrupted closeness to Allah and the illumination of His secret, culminating in gratitude and salawat.

Learning Objective

Every scholarly work begins with a sacred invocation and a clear intention.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer invokes Allah, praises Him for guiding him to tawhid, and explains his goal in writing a commentary on his own poem. Let's read and reflect on this foundational introduction.

Commentary
This passage is the author's opening invocation and statement of purpose. It begins with the basmalah, invoking Allah's name and seeking His help. The author then praises Allah for guiding him to the creed of tawhid and freeing the mind from blind imitation. The author then describes his work: it is a refined commentary on a solid summary based on the 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah' (the Brilliant Pearl), which he himself composed on the purposes of tawhid. The commentary aims to clarify meanings and strengthen the structure of the original poem. He states his intention is purely for Allah's sake, seeking reward and protection from unreliable sayings, and that the work is built upon verified proofs with additional material to increase certainty. He concludes by asking Allah that sincere recipients benefit from it and that it be accepted purely for His sake.

Author's Introduction and Purpose of the Commentary
Author's preface: praise, methodology, and prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author frames his commentary—invoking God, praising Him, and defining the work's purpose and methodology.

Core Idea
The commentary is a defense of truth, grounded in proofs, and a break from rationalist speculation.

The author opens his commentary with the basmalah and hamdalah, setting a reverent tone and establishing his theological stance. He describes his work as a means to clarify beliefs, increase certainty, and reject blind imitation. This brief but dense introduction prepares the reader for the rigorous kalam reasoning to come.

Guided Path
1

In Islamic scholarly works, these formulas are often theologically significant.
2
Identify the author's description of his commentary
The author says his commentary clarifies meanings, strengthens structure, and relies on proofs for certainty. Find these phrases in the text.
3
Observe the rejection of rationalist arguments
The passage contrasts his method with 'rational sayings' (التقاويل العقلية). This is a key methodological point.
This reflects the Ash'ari commitment to revelation over pure reason.
4
Note the concluding prayer
The author prays that sincere readers benefit and that the work be accepted by Allah. This shows the religious purpose of scholarship.

The Meaning of 'Ism' and the Basmalah
Linguistic analysis of 'ism' and the basmalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic debate about the derivation of the word 'ism' and the meaning of the basmalah as seeking blessing.

Core Idea
The word 'ism' is derived, and its use in the basmalah indicates seeking blessing through God's name.

After the introduction, the author begins a technical discussion of the basmalah. He explains the definition of 'ism' (noun) and presents two competing grammatical theories of its derivation: the Basran view (from 'wasm' meaning 'mark') and the Kufan view (from 'sūm' meaning 'height'). He then clarifies that the basmalah is a request for blessing through the name of Allah. Though the text is fractured, the core argument is recoverable.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'ism'
Find the definition given: 'noun is what indicates a meaning in itself, not associated with time'. This is the standard grammatical definition.
This definition excludes verbs and particles.
2
Identify the two derivational theories
The Basrans derive 'ism' from 'wasm' (mark), the Kufans from 'sūm' (height, or from 'samiya' meaning 'to be high'). Note how each theory changes the root letters.
These are classical philological debates.
3
Understand the meaning of the basmalah
The author says the basmalah means 'seeking blessing through the name of Allah'. The 'bā' indicates accompaniment or means.

The Commentator's Opening and Explanation of Key Terms
Imam Dardeer opens his commentary with invocations and explains key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the commentator's opening invocations and his clarification of the terms 'ribqa' and 'shawa'ib' as they relate to the goal of attaining true knowledge.

Core Idea
Blind imitation (taqlid) is a 'necklace' that hinders us from reaching the truth.

This lesson explores the opening passage of Imam Dardeer's commentary on 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah'. The commentator begins with praise and blessings, setting a spiritual tone, and then explains two key terms—'ribqa' and 'shawa'ib'—which form a powerful simile about the obstacles to knowledge. Understanding this introduction helps us appreciate the purpose and method of the entire commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening invocations
Read the basmalah, hamdalah, and salawat. Notice how the commentator frames the work as a gift from Allah and links it to freeing the mind from blind imitation.
2
Identify the purpose of the commentary
After 'amma ba'd', the commentator states this is a commentary on 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah'. Understand that 'subtle commentary' implies concise explanation.
3
Analyze the simile of 'ribqa' and 'shawa'ib'
Read the commentator's definition of 'ribqa' (a calf's necklace) and 'shawa'ib' (impurities). See how he likens impurities to a necklace that prevents reaching the goal—knowledge of the truth.
This simile warns against uncritical acceptance of inherited beliefs.
4
Reflect on the intended goal
Note the phrase 'al-maqsūd hunā huwa ma'rifat al-ḥaqq'. The entire work aims at knowledge of the truth, not mere memorization or polemics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
الحمد لله الذي نور قلوبنا بمعرفة عقائدِ التَّوحيد، وحرَّرَ عقولَنَا مِنْ شوائب التقليد، والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا محمد المؤيَّد بالمعجزاتِ الباهرة، وعلى آله وأصحابه أولي المناقب الفاخرة.
أما بعد: فهذا شرح لطيف على المقدِّمةِ المُسَاةِ بالخريدة البهية، التي نظمتها.
الربقة قلادة توضع في عنق العجل الصغير ليمسك بها عند حلاب أمه، والشوائب جمع «شائبة» الأخلاط والمعايب، والمراد تشبيه الشوائب بالربقة في المنع من الوصول إلى المقصود، والمقصود هنا هو معرفة الحق.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah who illuminated our hearts with the knowledge of the creeds of tawhid, and freed our minds from the impurities of taqlid. Blessings and peace be upon our master Muhammad, supported by clear miracles, and upon his family and companions, possessors of excellent merits. To proceed: This is a subtle commentary on the introduction called 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah', which [the author] composed. 'al-Ribqa' is a necklace placed around the neck of a small calf to hold it when milking its mother. 'al-Shawa'ib' is the plural of 'sha'iba', meaning mixtures and defects. The intention is to compare the shawa'ib to the ribqa in preventing reaching the intended goal, and the intended goal here is knowledge of the truth.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's opening invocation and a statement of purpose. It begins with the basmalah and hamdalah, thanking Allah for granting the community knowledge of the principles of tawhid and freeing their minds from the 'shawa'ib' (impurities) of blind imitation or taqlid. Then he sends blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad, who is described as 'al-mu'ayyad bil-mu'jizat al-bahira' (supported by clear miracles), and upon his family and companions. After the 'amma ba'd', he introduces the work: 'This is a subtle commentary on the introduction called al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah, which [the poet] composed.' The commentator then explains two key terms from the opening: 'al-ribqa' (a necklace for a calf) and 'al-shawa'ib' (impurities). He clarifies that 'shawa'ib' are likened to the 'ribqa' because both prevent one from reaching the desired goal—in this case, knowledge of the truth. This explanation shows that the commentator is concerned with precise terminology and setting the stage for the doctrinal content.

Key Terms
عقائد التوحيد
شوائب التقليد
الخريدة البهية
ربقة
شائبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'freeing minds from the impurities of taqlid' means rejecting all forms of following scholars or authority.
Correction: The commentator specifically criticizes 'blind imitation' (taqlid) without evidence, not all forms of following. The goal is to base belief on knowledge, not to abandon all authority.

Checkpoint

A) Memorizing the poem
B) Knowledge of the truth
C) Performing dhikr
D) Writing a commentary

Key Takeaway
The commentator's opening reminds us that the purpose of theological study is to attain knowledge of the truth, free from blind imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The calf's necklace holds it back from its mother's milk—something vital. Is there a 'necklace' of taqlid in your life that is keeping you from the life-giving knowledge of truth? What would it cost you to break free, and are you ready to pay that price?
?
Your goal, according to this passage, is 'knowledge of the truth.' Do you truly seek the truth, or do you seek confirmation of what you already assume? Imagine standing before Allah and explaining why you followed blindly. Would your excuse hold? What will you do after this lesson to change that?

The Author's Introduction: Purpose and Method of the Commentary
Dardeer opens with hamdalah and salawat and describes his method.

Dardeer opens his commentary with praise, prayer, and a clear statement of his balanced methodology.

Imam Dardeer begins by thanking Allah for guiding hearts to tawhid and freeing minds from blind imitation. He then outlines his approach: a middle path between excessive brevity and tedious length, focusing on precise proofs and beneficial insights. This introduction sets the tone for the entire commentary.

This passage is the author's introduction to his commentary. He describes the work as a 'subtle commentary' on his own versified introduction called al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya, which deals with tawhidic beliefs. He explains his methodology: the commentary will clarify meanings and raise the structure of the poem. He avoids two extremes: excessive brevity that omits necessary explanations, and excessive length that bores the reader. Instead, he focuses on 'tahrir al-barāhin' — precise formulations of proofs — along with additional benefits that strengthen certainty. He then prays that Allah makes the work beneficial to those who approach it with a sound heart and accepts it as sincerely for His sake. He concludes the introduction with 'fa aqūl' (I say) and acknowledges that his success is only from Allah, the Exalted and Almighty.

The Grammatical Analysis of the Basmala
Dardeer explains the implied verb in the basmala and its grammatical rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Dardeer interprets the basmala as implying a specific implied verb and the grammatical principles behind this interpretation.

Core Idea
The basmala's implied verb is specific to each action, fronted to indicate exclusive dedication.

Dardeer begins his commentary by explaining the grammatical structure of the basmala. He argues that the phrase 'bismillāh' implies an unstated verb that is not generic but specific to the action at hand. Understanding this grammatical nuance reveals deeper theological implications about the basmala's role in dedicating actions to Allah and seeking His blessing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Dardeer uses a very concise style.
2
Identify the three reasons
The passage gives three reasons for estimating the implied verb as a verb, postponed, and specific. Find each one and note how Dardeer justifies it.
3
Reflect on ikhtiṣāṣ
Consider why fronting the object (the basmala) indicates restriction. How does this relate to dedicating the action exclusively to Allah?
This connects to the concept of ikhtiṣāṣ in Arabic grammar.
4
Understand the specificity of the verb
Ask yourself why the verb must be specific to the action (e.g., 'I compose') and not a generic verb like 'I begin'. What does this imply about the basmala's role in each unique action?
Dardeer emphasizes blessing for all parts of the action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم) أي: أؤلفُ، وإنّما قدَّرْنا المُتَعَلقَ فِعْلًا؛ لأنَّ الأَصْلَ في العَمَلَ للأفْعالِ، ومُتأَخَّرًا؛ لَأَنَّ تقديمَ المعمولِ يُفيدُ الاختصاص، وخاصا؛ لأنَّ كُلَّ شارع في شَيءٍ ينبغي له أنْ يُقَدِّرَ ما جُعِلتِ البسْمَلةُ مبْدَأَ له، ولإفادةِ حُصولِ البَرَكة لجميع أجزاء الفِعْلِ.
('In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful') meaning: I compose. And we have estimated the operative (al-muta‘allaq) to be a verb, because the original principle regarding grammatical operation is for verbs, and [we estimated it] to be postponed, because fronting the object indicates restriction (ikhtiṣāṣ), and [we estimated it to be] specific, because every person commencing something ought to estimate that for which the basmala is made the beginning, and for obtaining blessing for all parts of the action.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological explanation of the basmala. The author interprets the phrase 'bismillāh' as implying an unstated verb: 'I compose' (u’allifu). He justifies this estimation on three counts. First, the verb is the original operator in Arabic grammar; prepositions and nouns derive their operational effect from an implicit verb. Second, the verb is placed after (mutā’akhkhiran) because the basmala is fronted, and fronting the object (the prepositional phrase) indicates restriction (ikhtiṣāṣ) — the action is exclusively initiated with the name of Allah. Third, the verb is specific (khāṣṣan) to the action being undertaken — here composing the commentary — because every author should tailor the basmala to the particular work, and this also brings blessing to every part of the action.

Key Terms
المتعلق
العمل للأفعال
تقديم المعمول
الاختصاص
البركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The implied verb in the basmala is always generic, such as 'I begin'.
Correction: Imam Dardeer insists the verb must be specific to the action, because the basmala is made the beginning of that particular action, and its blessing is tied to the action's parts.

Checkpoint
Why does the author estimate the implied verb in the basmala to be postponed (mutā’akhkhar)?
A) Because verbs are the original operators
B) To indicate restriction (ikhtiṣāṣ)
C) To obtain blessing
D) Because every action needs a specific verb

Key Takeaway
The basmala implies a specific verb for each action, fronted to show exclusive dedication and to bring blessing to the entire action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you begin any action, do you consciously dedicate it to Allah alone, or do you often begin without invoking His name specifically for that action? How might the lesson's emphasis on a specific verb change your practice?
?
The author says the implied verb must be specific to bring blessing to every part of the action. Think of one action you perform frequently—how can you make your basmala for that action more intentional and specific? What would that look like in practice?
?
The fronting of 'bismillah' indicates restriction (ikhtiṣāṣ)—meaning your action is exclusively for Allah. Is there an action you do that you feel is not solely for Allah? How can you purify your intention to make it exclusive to Him starting today?

Imam Dardeer's Methodology in Commenting on al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah
Imam Dardeer explains his approach to writing the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Dardeer's purpose and method in writing this commentary, including his approach to balancing brevity and length, focusing on refined proofs, and the spiritual conditions for benefiting from it.

Core Idea
The commentary balances concise precision and thorough explanation, seeking to increase certainty.

In this opening passage, Imam Dardeer sets forth his methodology for the commentary. He explains that he aims to clarify and strengthen the doctrines of tawhid, avoiding both overly brief and tediously lengthy exposition. Instead, he focuses on refined proofs and mentions benefits that increase certainty, all while asking Allah to make it beneficial for those with sound hearts. This introduction gives us a clear picture of the author's intent and the spirit in which the commentary was written.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author's Aims
Look for the key verbs describing what the commentary does: clarifies, strengthens, etc. Note that it focuses on tawhid doctrines.
The author wants to 'clarify meanings' and 'strengthen foundations'.
2
Understand the Method
Notice the balance between brevity and length. The author says he avoided 'deficient brevity' and 'tedious length', implying a middle path. Also note he confined himself to 'refined proofs'.
3
Recognize the Role of Benefits
The author includes 'benefits by which certainty increases'. This shows that the commentary is not just logical proof but also nourishes faith.
4
Reflect on the Spiritual Dimension
Read the prayer: the author asks that the commentary be beneficial for 'everyone who meets it with a sound heart'. This reminds us that true benefit depends on the reader's sincerity.
The concept of tawfiq (success from Allah) is also highlighted at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على البسملة شرح الخريدة البهية في العقائد التوحيديّة، يوضح معانيها ويُشيّدُ مبانيها (١) يشيد مبانيها أي يرفعها فبعد أن بينها وكشفها هذا الشرح أظهر قيمتها وأعلى قدرها، والمراد بمبانيها ما بنيت عليه الخريدة من الألفاظ.، اجتنبت فيه الاختصار المخل، وأعرَضتُ فيه عن التَّطويل الممل (۲) يريد أن يقول: إنه مختصر غير مخل، ومطول غير ممل، وهما ضدان لا يجتمعان، والجواب عن ذلك أن الاختصار في مواضع، والتطويل في مواضع أخرى على حسب ما يقتضيه المقام.، واقتصرتُ فيه على تحرير البراهين (۳) التحرير في الأصل مطلق التلخيص فالمراد هنا أنه نقحه وهذبه وخلصه من الحشو والتطويل، تحقيق معانيه وتشييد مبانيه.، مع الفوائد التي يزدادُ بها اليقين، والله أسألُ أنْ يَنفَعَ (٤) النفع تابع للحب والاعتقاد، فالنفع بها لا يحصل إلا لمن كان على هذه الصفة. وكذلك الانتفاع بعلوم الأولياء والصالحين من علماء هذه الأمة، فلا يحصل لأحد يعتقد فيهم اعتقادا سيئًا أو يبغضهم أو يقع في أعراضهم أو يقدح في مكانتهم إلخ. بِهِ كُلَّ مَنْ تلقاهُ بِقَلْبٍ سليم، وأن يجعله خالصا لوَجْهِهِ الكريم، إنه المولى الرَّؤوف الرحيم، فأقول، وما توفيقي إلا بالله العلي العظيم: (٥) التوفيق هو خلق القدرة على الطاعة في العبد أو خلق الطاعة من قولك: وفقت بين الشيء والشيء، أي جعلت بينهما موافقة ومناسبة، فمن وفقه الله جعله موافقا لأغراض الشرع، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يُرِدِ اللهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلإِسْلَام وَمَن يُرِدْ أَن يُضِلَّهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيّقاً حَرَجاً﴾ الآية [الأنعام: ١٢٥ ) ، لذلك فالكافر غير موفق، والباء في «الله» إما بمعنى (من) والتقدير: وما توفيقي إلا من الله، أو أنه ضمن التوفيق معنى الإعانة والتقدير، أي: وما أعانني إلا الله.
Discussion on the basmalah. A commentary on the Radiant Pearl (al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah) concerning the doctrines of tawhid, clarifying its meanings and strengthening its foundations (1) — he means elevating it, so after explaining and revealing it, this commentary shows its value and raises its status, and by 'its foundations' he means the expressions upon which the Pearl is built. I avoided therein deficient brevity, and turned away from tedious length (2) — he intends to say: it is concise without deficiency, and lengthy without tedium, and they are two opposites that do not come together; the answer to that is that brevity is in some places and length in others according to what the situation requires. And I confined myself therein to refined proofs (3) — refinement originally means absolute summarization; what is meant here is that he revised, polished, and purified it from filler and length, realizing its meanings and strengthening its foundations. Along with benefits by which certainty increases. And I ask Allah that He make it beneficial (4) — benefit follows love and belief; thus benefit from it only occurs for one who is in this state. Likewise benefit from the sciences of the saints and righteous among the scholars of this nation, so it does not occur for anyone who believes ill of them, hates them, attacks their honor, or disparages their status, etc. — for everyone who meets it with a sound heart, and make it purely for His noble Face. Indeed He is the Patron, the Kind, the Merciful. I say, and my success is only with Allah, the Most High, the Almighty (5) — tawfiq (success) is the creation of ability for obedience in the servant or the creation of obedience; from your saying: I reconciled between one thing and another, i.e., I made agreement and appropriateness between them. So whoever Allah grants tawfiq, He makes him agree with the purposes of the Shari'a. And from it is His statement: {So whoever Allah wants to guide, He expands his breast to Islam, and whoever He wants to misguide, He makes his breast tight and constricted} [al-An'am 125]. Therefore the disbeliever is not given tawfiq. And the ba' in 'Allah' is either with the meaning of 'from' — the estimation: and my success is only from Allah — or [success] has been given the meaning of assistance, and the estimation: and no one assists me except Allah.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an authorial preface in which Imam Dardeer outlines his methodology for the commentary. He begins by identifying the work: it is a commentary on al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah, a poem on tawhid. He states three key features: (1) he clarifies its meanings and strengthens its foundations; (2) he avoids both deficient brevity and tedious length, achieving a middle ground where brevity and prolixity are used as appropriate; (3) he focuses on refined proofs (tahrir al-barāhin) and includes beneficial points that increase certainty. He then prays that Allah make it beneficial specifically for those with a sound heart and for His sake alone. Finally, he acknowledges that his success (tawfiq) comes solely from Allah, and explains tawfiq as the creation of ability for obedience in the servant. This preface establishes the tone of the work: a balanced, precise, and spiritually sincere exposition.

Key Terms
الخريدة البهية
التوحيد
الاختصار
التطويل
تحرير البراهين
اليقين
التوفيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a commentary cannot be both concise and thorough, or that brevity and length are mutually exclusive.
Correction: Imam Dardeer explains that he uses brevity in some places and length in others as the situation requires, thus achieving a balanced work that is neither deficiently brief nor tediously long.

Checkpoint
What does the author say he avoided in this commentary?
A) Deficient brevity and tedious length
B) Only deficient brevity
C) Only tedious length
D) Both brevity and length entirely

Key Takeaway
The commentary is carefully crafted to avoid extremes, centered on refined proofs and beneficial insights, and intended for those pure of heart, relying solely on Allah's aid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you seek knowledge, do you examine your heart for any grudge or ill feeling towards the scholars through whom this knowledge comes? Will you repent and clear your heart so that you may truly benefit?
?
Do you attribute your progress in understanding faith to your own intelligence and effort, or do you acknowledge that every bit of success is from Allah alone? How would your prayer change if you truly believed this?
?
Imam Dardeer balanced brevity and length in his commentary. In your own learning, do you rush through to get to the end (deficient brevity) or get bogged down in unnecessary details (tedious length)? What is one concrete change you will make to seek a balanced approach?

Unpacking the Opening Line
Dardeer explains the first line of his poem and its grammatical and theological details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and theological nuances in Imam Dardeer's opening line of his poem.

Core Idea
Every word in a classical text is chosen with precision, reflecting deep grammar and theology.

Imam Dardeer begins his poem with a humble and theologically rich phrase. In this lesson, we unpack each word of that opening line to see how grammar and theology intertwine in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the full English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the structure: the author first identifies himself, then explains the verb, then the construct, then the divine name.
2
Analyze the verb 'yaqulu'
Look at how Dardeer explains the original form of 'yaqulu' and the lightening process. This shows attention to linguistic precision.
The original was 'yaqūlu' with a long ū, lightened to 'yaqulu' by moving the vowel.
3
Understand 'raji rahmat' as a construct
See how 'raji' (hoping) is added to 'rahmat' (mercy) in an iḍāfa construction, indicating the one who hopes for mercy.
The common misunderstanding is that 'raji' implies passive hoping; here it includes taking means.
4
Explore the two meanings of 'al-Qadir'
Dardeer gives two interpretations: permanently powerful (ṣifa mushabbaha) or abundantly powerful (ṣīghat mubālagha). Both emphasize Allah's perfect power.
This shows how a single divine name can carry multiple layers of meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول راجي رحمة القدير * أي: أحمد المشهور بالدردير (يقول) هو من باب نصر فأصله يقول بسكون فائه وضم عينه فخفف بنقل حركة العين إلى الفاء راجي رحمة بإضافة الوصف إلى معموله أي المؤمل المنتظر إنعام (القدير) أي دائم القدرة فهو صفة مشبهة أو الكثير القدرة بمعنى الاقتدار فيكون صيغة مبالغة.
He says: 'The one hoping for the mercy of the All-Powerful' * i.e., Ahmad known as al-Dardīr. ('Yaqūlu') is from the paradigm 'naṣara'; its original form is 'yaqūlu' with sukun on its fā' and ḍamma on its 'ayn, then it was lightened by moving the vowel of the 'ayn to the fā'. 'Rājī raḥmat' is a construct of the descriptive word to its object, i.e., the one who hopes and expects the favor of ('al-Qadīr'), i.e., the permanently powerful, so it is a descriptive adjective (ṣifa mushabbaha), or the one of abundant power meaning omnipotence, so it becomes an intensive form (ṣīghat mubālagha).
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is an introductory explanation of the first line of Imam Dardeer's poem. He begins the poem with 'Yaqulu raji rahmat al-qadir', which means 'He says: the one hoping for the mercy of the All-Powerful'. He identifies himself as the speaker, Ahmad al-Dardir. Then he provides a grammatical and theological analysis of each word. 'Yaqulu' is a verb from the paradigm 'nasara' (to help), originally with a sukun on the first radical (fā') and a ḍamma on the second (ʿayn). The lightened form is achieved by moving the vowel of the ʿayn to the fā', so it becomes 'yaqulu' instead of 'yaqūlu'? Actually, the explanation says the original had sukun on fā' and ḍamma on ʿayn, so it was 'yaqūlu' (with long ū), then lightened to 'yaqulu' (short u). The phrase 'rājī raḥmat' is a construct (iḍāfa) where 'rājī' (hoping) is the descriptive word governing its object 'raḥmat' (mercy). 'Rājī' means the one who hopes and expects. 'Al-Qadīr' is one of Allah's names meaning the one who is always powerful (dā'im al-qudra) – thus a descriptive adjective indicating permanence – or it can mean the one of abundant power (al-kathīr al-qudra), which would be an intensive form. This shows the author's careful attention to linguistic precision and theological nuance even in his opening line.

Key Terms
يقول
راجي
رجاء
القدير
صفة مشبهة
صيغة مبالغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'raji' means simply 'hoping' without the nuance of taking means.
Correction: In this context, 'raji' implies expecting while taking means (al-rajā' ma'a al-akhdh bil-asbāb), as Dardeer explains it as 'the one who hopes and expects the favor'.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'al-Qadīr' as explained in this passage?
A) The one who is able to do anything only once
B) The permanently powerful or abundant in power
C) The one who creates power in others
D) The one who has power limited to certain things

Key Takeaway
The opening line of the poem reveals the author's humility and theological precision, showing that even the first words are carefully chosen to convey multiple layers of meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam Dardeer begins his poem calling himself 'rājī raḥmat al-Qadīr'—one hoping for mercy while taking means. When you start a task or write something, do you begin with that same posture of humble reliance, or do you lean on your own ability? What would change if you consciously began every project with 'I am the one hoping for the mercy of the All-Powerful'?
?
Dardeer shows that even a single divine name like 'al-Qadīr' can be understood in two profound ways: as a permanent attribute or an intensive abundance. Which of these meanings evokes a stronger sense of awe in your heart? Are you content with a shallow understanding of Allah's names, or will you, starting today, ponder each one more deeply?
?
The grammatical analysis here is not just academic—it shapes theology. Have you ever dismissed detailed linguistic study as 'too technical' and missed the spiritual depth it offers? Will you now approach Arabic grammar as a pathway to knowing Allah more intimately, beginning with this lesson?

The Grammar and Theology of 'Al-Hamdu Lillah'
Dardeer explains the grammatical status and types of 'al' in 'al-hamdu lillah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical function of 'al' in 'al-hamdu lillah' and the theological implications of its possible interpretations.

Core Idea
The 'al' in 'al-hamdu' is not just a definite article; it shapes the meaning of praise.

In the opening phrase of his poem, Imam Dardeer provides a grammatical analysis that reveals layers of meaning. Before diving into theological arguments, he clarifies the precise linguistic scope of the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah'. This passage teaches us that even the definite article can carry significant doctrinal weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: الحمد, أل, لله.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the grammatical context
Identify that Dardeer states the entire text is 'maqul al-qawl' in the accusative. Then note his analysis of 'al' in 'al-hamdu'.
The 'al' can be either jinsiyyah (genus) or istighraqiyyah (totality).
3
Distinguish the two meanings of 'al'
Explore the difference: jinsiyyah refers to the genus of praise without specifying instances; istighraqiyyah encompasses every instance of praise.
This distinction affects whether praise is universal or categorical.
4
Interpret the 'lam' in 'lillah'
The 'lam' indicates istihqaq (entitlement) – praise is due to Allah alone.
This underlines that all praise belongs to Allah, not just some.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this grammatical point matters: it establishes that all praise is rightfully Allah's, setting the tone for the entire creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمدُ للهِ العَلِيِّ الواحدِ * العالم الفَردِ الغنيّ الماجدِ (الحمد لله) هو وما بعده إلى آخرِ الكِتابِ مقولُ القَولِ في محلّ نصب، و«أل» فيه جنسيّة، أو استغراقيّةُ، ولامُ «لله) للاستحقاق.
Praise be to Allah, the High, the One, the All-Knowing, the Unique, the Self-Sufficient, the Glorious. (Al-hamdu lillah) is, along with what follows it to the end of the book, the reported speech in the accusative case, and the definite article 'al' in it is either for genus or for totality, and the 'lam' of 'lillah' indicates entitlement.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological note on the opening phrase 'al-hamdu lillah'. The author states that the entire text from this phrase to the end of the book is the content of the author's speech (maqul al-qawl) and is in the accusative case as the object of 'yaqulu' (he says). Then he analyzes the definite article 'al' in 'al-hamdu': it can be either for genus (jinsiyyah), meaning the genus of praise, or for totality (istighraqiyyah), meaning all praise. The distinction: if it is for genus, it refers to the category of praise without specifying individuals; if for totality, it means every individual instance of praise. The 'lam' in 'lillah' indicates entitlement (istihqaq), meaning that praise is due to Allah alone. This establishes that all praise belongs exclusively to Allah.

The lesson distinguishes between genus and totality of praise. Reflect: Are there aspects of your life where you praise Allah in a general sense but neglect to attribute specific events to Him?
