The Two Types of 'Al' in 'Alhamdulillah'
Dardir explains the grammatical status of 'al-hamdu lillah' and the meaning of its 'al' and lam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of the definite article 'al' in the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' and how to distinguish between them.

Core Idea
The 'al' in 'al-hamd' can mean either the genus of praise or every praise.

When we say 'Alhamdulillah', it is not just a fixed expression; its grammatical structure carries deep meaning. In this passage, Imam Dardir explains two possible interpretations of the definite article 'al' in 'al-hamd' and how to tell them apart. Understanding this will sharpen your appreciation of a phrase we repeat daily.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical framework
Read the passage and note that Dardir first sets the grammatical status of the entire phrase: it is 'maqool al-qawl' in the accusative case. Then he turns to the word 'al-hamd' itself.
The phrase is the object of speech, a common construction in Arabic.
2
Learn the two types of 'al'
Dardir explains that the 'al' in 'al-hamd' can be either for genus (jinsiyya) or for totality (istighraqiyya). Understand the difference: genus focuses on the concept, totality on every instance.
3
Examine the signs of totality
Look at the two signs given: (1) the validity of exception after the noun, and (2) describing the definite noun with a plural. These signs help determine when 'al' means 'all'.
The Quranic verses provided are key evidence.
4
Apply to 'al-hamd'
Consider the meaning of 'al-hamd' under each interpretation. If 'al' is for genus, it means praise in general; if for totality, it means every praise belongs to Allah.
Both are doctrinally sound, but they convey different nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله هو وما بعده إلى آخر الكتاب مقول القول في محل نصب، و«أل» فيه جنسية، أو استغراقية، ولام «لله» للاستحقاق. الفرق بينهما أن الجنسية ينظر منها إلى جنس الحمد بقطع النظر عن الأفراد، أما الاستغراقية فعلامتها صحة الاستثناء بعدها، مثل قوله تعالى: {إنَّ الإِنسَانَ لَفِي خُسْرٍ إِلَّا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا} [العصر: ٢-٣] وكذلك وصف المحلى بها بالجمع كما في قوله تعالى: {أَوِ الطِّفْلِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يَظْهَرُوا عَلَى عَوْرَاتِ النِّسَاءِ} [النور: 31]، فإذا كانت «أل» في الحمد للجنس فيكون معناها جنس المحامد بقطع النظر عن البعض أو الكل، أما إذا كانت استغراقية فيكون معناها كل حمد فهو مستحق لله تعالى.
"Alhamdulillah and what comes after it to the end of the book is the quoted speech in the accusative case. The 'al' in it is either for genus or for totality, and the lam in 'lillah' is for entitlement. The difference between them is that from the genus one looks at the genus of praise regardless of individuals, while the sign of the totality is the validity of exception after it, like His saying: 'Indeed mankind is in loss, except those who believe' [al-Asr: 2-3], and likewise the description of the definite noun with a plural as in His saying: 'or children who have not yet revealed the private parts of women' [an-Nur: 31]. So if the 'al' in 'al-hamd' is for genus, its meaning is the genus of praises regardless of some or all; but if it is for totality, its meaning is every praise is entitled to Allah."
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage explains the grammatical construction of the phrase "Alhamdulillah" (Praise be to Allah). It states that the entire phrase from "al-hamdu" onward is the maqool al-qawl (the content of the saying) and is in the accusative case as the direct speech. Then it discusses the definite article "al" in "al-hamd". It can be either for genus (jinsiyya) or for totality (istighraqiyya). The lam in "lillah" indicates exclusive entitlement (istihqaq) – only Allah deserves praise. The passage then elaborates on the difference between the two types of "al". For genus, it looks at the concept of praise in general without considering individual instances. For totality, the sign is that an exception can follow, as in the Quranic verse "Indeed mankind is in loss, except those who believe". Also, if the noun with "al" is described by a plural, it indicates totality, as in "children who have not revealed". So if "al" is for genus, "al-hamd" means the general concept of praise; if for totality, it means all praises are for Allah.

مقول القول
أل الجنسية
أل الاستغراقية
لام الاستحقاق

Correction: Dardir shows it can have a specific semantic range: either indicating the genus (type) of praise or encompassing all individual praises (totality).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The definite article 'al' in 'al-hamd' has two possible meanings—genus or totality—and the context or signs help determine which is intended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is there any area of your life where you subtly claim praise for yourself rather than directing it to Him? What will you change?
?
The difference between jinsiyya and istighraqiyya is not just grammar; it reflects the depth of your tawheed. Does your heart truly believe that all praise—past, present, future—belongs to Allah, or do you sometimes think others deserve praise? How can you train your heart to see everything as a reason to praise Allah alone?

Why the Poem is Called 'Great'
The author explains the meaning of 'great' and poses an implied question about hyperbole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author describes the poem as 'great' and how he addresses the potential objection of hyperbole.

Core Idea
The poem is 'great' not in size but in the depth of theological knowledge it contains.

The author of the commentary explains that the poem's greatness lies in its comprehensive coverage of essential theological topics. He then anticipates a question: is such praise hyperbolic? This passage sets up the answer by detailing what the poem actually contains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the statement of greatness to the list of contents and finally the implied question.
2
Identify the meaning of 'great'
The author clarifies that 'great' refers to value in knowledge, not physical size. Find the phrase that makes this distinction.
Look for 'في العلم' and 'المعاني المدلولة لها'.
3
List the contents of the poem
The passage enumerates six areas the poem covers. List them: what is wajib/mustahil/ja'iz for Allah and messengers, proofs, refutation, sam'iyyat, tasawwuf.
This shows why the poem is great.
4
Understand the implied question
The author then poses an implied question: is this description of the poem hyperbole? Note that he does not answer directly here; the answer comes in the following text.
The question is 'أو هذا من باب المبالغة؟'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(لكنها كبيرة) أي: عظيمة (في العلم) :أي: المعاني المدلولة لها، وذلك لأنها اشتملت على بيان ما يحب الله تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وعلى مثل ذلك في من رسله عليهم الصلاة والسلام، وعلى البراهين القطعية التي يخرج بها المكلف من ربة التقليد (۳) إلى نور التحقيق (۳) ، حتى لا يكون في إيمانه خلافٌ لا يكون في إيمانِه خِلاف - وسيأتي بيان الخلاف في إيمان المقلد إن شاء الله تعالى وعلى الرَّدَّ على أهل الضلال تصريح تارةً وتلوي أخرى)، وعلى السمعيات)، وعلى شَيْءٍ مِنَ التصوُّف الذي هو (۲) حياة النفوس، كما سترى ذلك كلَّه إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ تعالى مُفَصَّلًا. ولذا قال مُستأنفا في جواب سؤالٍ مُقَدَّرٍ نَشَأَ ما قبْلَه، تقديرُه: هل تكفى هذه العقيدة المكلف في دينه، كما يدلُّ عليه هذا الوَصْفُ الذي قدَّمته؟ أو هذا مِنْ بابِ المبالغة ؟ ثم ذكرت الحواشي: (١) كان مقتضى الظاهر أن يقول عزيزة أو كثيرة، وذلك لأن الكبر يرجع للكمية أي العدد، وهو لا يكون إلا فيما له أجزاء، والعلم معنى من المعاني، والجواب أنه إنما عبر بذلك لتحصل المقابلة مع قوله: «صغيرة» ففي كلامه الجناس المطابق. (۲) حياة النفوس. (۳) أي ظلمة التقليد وقيوده المانعة من الاهتداء. (۳) التحقيق هو إثبات العقائد بأدلتها. (٤) العبارة إذا دلت على المعنى المراد منها يقال له: تصريح، وإن أشارت له يقال له: تلويح، فإن كانت الإشارة خفية كان تلميحًا. (٥) السمعيات ما دليله سمعي كالبعث والحشر والنشر والجنة والنار. (٦) المبالغة هي إعطاء الشئ أكثر مما يستحق، وتنحصر في ثلاثة: التبليغ والإغراق والغلو، وذلك أن المدعى إن كان ممكنا عقلا وعادة فتبليغ، وإن كان ممكنا عقلا لا عادة فإغراق مثل: ونُكْرِمُ جارنا ما دام فينا * وتتبعه الكرامة حيثُ مالا، فهذا ممكن عقلا لا عادةً، وإن لم يكن ممكنا عقلا ولا عادةً فغلو مثل: وأخَفْتَ أَهْلَ الشِّرْكَ حتّى إِنَّهُ * لَتَخَافُكَ النُّطَفُ التي لم تُخلق، وذلك لأن خوف النطف غير المخلوقة ممتنع عقلا وعادة والمقبول من الغلو ما أدخل عليه لفظ يقربه من الصحة نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿يَكَادُ زَيْتُهَا يُضِيءُ وَلَوْ لَمْ تَمْسَسْهُ نَارٌ ﴾ [النور: ٣٥)، فإذا عرف هذا فالمبالغة منها ما هو مقبول ومنها ما هو مردود.
“(But it is great) meaning: valuable (in knowledge) i.e., in the meanings it indicates. This is because it includes an explanation of what is obligatory, impossible, and permissible for Allah, and similarly for His messengers, upon them peace and blessings, and the definitive proofs by which the responsible person emerges from the darkness of blind imitation to the light of verification, so that there is no doubt in his faith – and the discussion of the faith of the blind follower will come later, God willing – and it includes refutation of the people of misguidance explicitly sometimes and allusively others, and the sam'iyyat (audible matters), and something of tasawwuf which is (2) the life of souls, as you will see all of that in detail, God willing. Then he said, resuming an answer to an implied question that arose from what preceded: its estimation: Does this creed suffice for the responsible person in his religion, as indicated by this description I have presented? Or is this from the category of hyperbole? Then the footnotes were mentioned: (1) The apparent requirement was that he say ‘dear’ or ‘abundant’, because greatness pertains to quantity i.e., number, and that only applies to what has parts, while knowledge is a meaning. The answer is that he used that word to achieve a contrast with his saying ‘small’, so in his words there is assonant paronomasia. (2) The life of souls. (3) i.e., the darkness of blind imitation and its restraints that prevent guidance. (3) Verification is establishing beliefs with their proofs. (4) If the expression indicates the intended meaning directly, it is called explicit; if it alludes to it, it is called allusive; if the allusion is subtle, it is called suggestive. (5) The sam'iyyat are matters whose proof is transmitted, like resurrection, gathering, dispersal, paradise, and hellfire. (6) Hyperbole is giving something more than it deserves. It is limited to three: balāgh (exaggeration that is possible both rationally and customarily), ighrāq (exaggeration possible rationally but not customarily), and ghalū (exaggeration not possible rationally or customarily). For example, the boast: ‘We honor our neighbor as long as he is among us, and honor follows him wherever he goes’ – this is possible rationally but not customarily. And another: ‘You have frightened the people of shirk so much that even the sperm that have not been created fear you’ – this is impossible rationally and customarily. The acceptable form of ghalū is when a word is introduced that brings it close to truth, like the verse: ‘…its oil would almost shine, even if no fire touched it’ (an-Nur 35). So, hyperbole includes what is acceptable and what is rejected.”
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of the poem 'Al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah', where the author explains why the poem is called 'great' (kabīra). He clarifies that 'great' does not refer to physical size but to the richness of its meanings. The poem covers: what is wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz for Allah and His messengers; definitive proofs that elevate the believer from taqlid to tahqīq (verification); refutations of heretics; sam'iyyat (transmitted matters like resurrection); and some tasawwuf. The author then introduces an imagined objection: is this description a form of hyperbole (mubālagha)? The answer is implicit: the description is literal, not hyperbolic. The footnotes explain: (1) why 'kabīra' is used rather than 'azīza' or 'kathīra' – to create jinas (paronomasia) with the poem's title 'small'. (2) 'Tasawwuf' is the life of souls. (3) 'Taqlid' is darkness; 'tahqīq' is establishing beliefs by proofs. (4) 'Tasrīh' vs 'talwīh' vs 'talmi' – degrees of explicitness. (5) 'Sam'iyyat' are matters based on scripture. (6) A full classification of mubālagha into three types – tablīgh, ighrāq, ghalū – with examples, and the distinction between acceptable and unacceptable forms.

Key Terms
التقليد
التحقيق
السمعيات
التصوّف
التصريح
التلويح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'great' refers to the poem's length or volume.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'great' refers to the value of its knowledge, not its physical size.

Checkpoint

?

What would it be like to prioritize depth over breadth in your next learning session?

Honestly examine your own conviction: are the proofs outlined here enough to ground your faith firmly, or do you still harbor doubts? What would it take for you to reach the certainty described by 'noor al-tahqeeq'?

The Three Types of Hyperbole
The footnote defines hyperbole and divides it into three types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of hyperbole (mubālagha) in Arabic rhetoric and how they are assessed in kalam.

Core Idea
Hyperbole has three types, and only the extreme form is problematic unless qualified.

In response to the question of whether the description of the poem is hyperbole, the footnote provides a detailed classification of hyperbole. This lesson explores the three types and their examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of hyperbole
Start by reading the definition: hyperbole is giving something more than it deserves. Then see how it is divided into three types based on rational and customary possibility.
2
Identify the three types
The three types are: tablīgh (possible both rationally and customarily), ighrāq (possible only rationally), and ghalū (impossible both). Match each type to its condition.
3
Study the examples
The passage gives examples: a line of poetry for ighrāq, and another for ghalū. Understand why each fits its category.
4
Note the exception for ghalū
The passage mentions that ghalū can be acceptable if a word brings it close to truth, citing the Quranic verse. Consider how this applies to the poem's description.

Source Text

(لكنها كبيرة) أي: عظيمة (في العلم) :أي: المعاني المدلولة لها، وذلك لأنها اشتملت على بيان ما يحب الله تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وعلى مثل ذلك في من رسله عليهم الصلاة والسلام، وعلى البراهين القطعية التي يخرج بها المكلف من ربة التقليد (۳) إلى نور التحقيق (۳) ، حتى لا يكون في إيمانه خلافٌ لا يكون في إيمانِه خِلاف - وسيأتي بيان الخلاف في إيمان المقلد إن شاء الله تعالى وعلى الرَّدَّ على أهل الضلال تصريح تارةً وتلوي أخرى)، وعلى السمعيات)، وعلى شَيْءٍ مِنَ التصوُّف الذي هو (۲) حياة النفوس، كما سترى ذلك كلَّه إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ تعالى مُفَصَّلًا. ولذا قال مُستأنفا في جواب سؤالٍ مُقَدَّرٍ نَشَأَ ما قبْلَه، تقديرُه: هل تكفى هذه العقيدة المكلف في دينه، كما يدلُّ عليه هذا الوَصْفُ الذي قدَّمته؟ أو هذا مِنْ بابِ المبالغة ؟ ثم ذكرت الحواشي: (١) كان مقتضى الظاهر أن يقول عزيزة أو كثيرة، وذلك لأن الكبر يرجع للكمية أي العدد، وهو لا يكون إلا فيما له أجزاء، والعلم معنى من المعاني، والجواب أنه إنما عبر بذلك لتحصل المقابلة مع قوله: «صغيرة» ففي كلامه الجناس المطابق. (۲) حياة النفوس. (۳) أي ظلمة التقليد وقيوده المانعة من الاهتداء. (۳) التحقيق هو إثبات العقائد بأدلتها. (٤) العبارة إذا دلت على المعنى المراد منها يقال له: تصريح، وإن أشارت له يقال له: تلويح، فإن كانت الإشارة خفية كان تلميحًا. (٥) السمعيات ما دليله سمعي كالبعث والحشر والنشر والجنة والنار. (٦) المبالغة هي إعطاء الشئ أكثر مما يستحق، وتنحصر في ثلاثة: التبليغ والإغراق والغلو، وذلك أن المدعى إن كان ممكنا عقلا وعادة فتبليغ، وإن كان ممكنا عقلا لا عادة فإغراق مثل: ونُكْرِمُ جارنا ما دام فينا * وتتبعه الكرامة حيثُ مالا، فهذا ممكن عقلا لا عادةً، وإن لم يكن ممكنا عقلا ولا عادةً فغلو مثل: وأخَفْتَ أَهْلَ الشِّرْكَ حتّى إِنَّهُ * لَتَخَافُكَ النُّطَفُ التي لم تُخلق، وذلك لأن خوف النطف غير المخلوقة ممتنع عقلا وعادة والمقبول من الغلو ما أدخل عليه لفظ يقربه من الصحة نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿يَكَادُ زَيْتُهَا يُضِيءُ وَلَوْ لَمْ تَمْسَسْهُ نَارٌ ﴾ [النور: ٣٥)، فإذا عرف هذا فالمبالغة منها ما هو مقبول ومنها ما هو مردود.
“(But it is great) meaning: valuable (in knowledge) i.e., in the meanings it indicates. This is because it includes an explanation of what is obligatory, impossible, and permissible for Allah, and similarly for His messengers, upon them peace and blessings, and the definitive proofs by which the responsible person emerges from the darkness of blind imitation to the light of verification, so that there is no doubt in his faith – and the discussion of the faith of the blind follower will come later, God willing – and it includes refutation of the people of misguidance explicitly sometimes and allusively others, and the sam'iyyat (audible matters), and something of tasawwuf which is (2) the life of souls, as you will see all of that in detail, God willing. Then he said, resuming an answer to an implied question that arose from what preceded: its estimation: Does this creed suffice for the responsible person in his religion, as indicated by this description I have presented? Or is this from the category of hyperbole? Then the footnotes were mentioned: (1) The apparent requirement was that he say ‘dear’ or ‘abundant’, because greatness pertains to quantity i.e., number, and that only applies to what has parts, while knowledge is a meaning. The answer is that he used that word to achieve a contrast with his saying ‘small’, so in his words there is assonant paronomasia. (2) The life of souls. (3) i.e., the darkness of blind imitation and its restraints that prevent guidance. (3) Verification is establishing beliefs with their proofs. (4) If the expression indicates the intended meaning directly, it is called explicit; if it alludes to it, it is called allusive; if the allusion is subtle, it is called suggestive. (5) The sam'iyyat are matters whose proof is transmitted, like resurrection, gathering, dispersal, paradise, and hellfire. (6) Hyperbole is giving something more than it deserves. It is limited to three: balāgh (exaggeration that is possible both rationally and customarily), ighrāq (exaggeration possible rationally but not customarily), and ghalū (exaggeration not possible rationally or customarily). For example, the boast: ‘We honor our neighbor as long as he is among us, and honor follows him wherever he goes’ – this is possible rationally but not customarily. And another: ‘You have frightened the people of shirk so much that even the sperm that have not been created fear you’ – this is impossible rationally and customarily. The acceptable form of ghalū is when a word is introduced that brings it close to truth, like the verse: ‘…its oil would almost shine, even if no fire touched it’ (an-Nur 35). So, hyperbole includes what is acceptable and what is rejected.”
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of the poem 'Al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah', where the author explains why the poem is called 'great' (kabīra). He clarifies that 'great' does not refer to physical size but to the richness of its meanings. The poem covers: what is wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz for Allah and His messengers; definitive proofs that elevate the believer from taqlid to tahqīq (verification); refutations of heretics; sam'iyyat (transmitted matters like resurrection); and some tasawwuf. The author then introduces an imagined objection: is this description a form of hyperbole (mubālagha)? The answer is implicit: the description is literal, not hyperbolic. The footnotes explain: (1) why 'kabīra' is used rather than 'azīza' or 'kathīra' – to create jinas (paronomasia) with the poem's title 'small'. (2) 'Tasawwuf' is the life of souls. (3) 'Taqlid' is darkness; 'tahqīq' is establishing beliefs by proofs. (4) 'Tasrīh' vs 'talwīh' vs 'talmi' – degrees of explicitness. (5) 'Sam'iyyat' are matters based on scripture. (6) A full classification of mubālagha into three types – tablīgh, ighrāq, ghalū – with examples, and the distinction between acceptable and unacceptable forms.

Key Terms

التبليغ
الإغراق
الغلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think all hyperbole is false or unacceptable.
Correction: The passage shows that tablīgh and ighrāq are acceptable, and even ghalū can be acceptable with a qualifying expression.

Checkpoint
Which type of hyperbole is impossible both rationally and customarily?
A) Tablīgh
B) Ighrāq
C) Ghalū
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Hyperbole is divided into three types based on possibility, and only ghalū requires a mitigating expression to be acceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent conversation where you exaggerated to emphasize a point. Using the three categories from this lesson, classify your exaggeration. Was it acceptable? What might you say differently to keep the truth intact while still being impactful? Commit to one specific change.
?
The Qur'an uses 'almost' to transform an impossible statement into acceptable hyperbole. Reflect on your own speech: do you have a habit of speaking in absolutes or extreme terms? What 'almost' word can you insert this week to bring your language closer to truth? Name a concrete situation.

Why Tawhid and Its Fivefold Honor
The commentator enumerates five reasons for the honor of this science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the science of tawhid is named after divine unity despite covering prophethood and afterlife, and the five aspects that demonstrate its supreme honor.

Core Idea
The honor of tawhid comes from its subject, need, objects, goal, and certainty of its proofs.

This passage from al-Siba'i's hashia answers a common question: why is this science called 'tawhid' (divine unity) when it also discusses prophethood and the afterlife? The commentator offers two explanations and then lists five compelling reasons for the science's inherent honor. These reasons will deepen your appreciation for the study of Islamic creed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the naming question
Read the passage and note the question: why call it tawhid if it includes more? The answer is either that tawhid is the most important part or that it is a condition for worship validity.
This shows the science's scope is broader than its name suggests.
2
Memorise the five aspects
The passage lists five aspects of honor. Identify each: (1) the foundation (knowledge of divine rulings), (2) the need (it is chief of religious sciences), (3) its objects (Essence, attributes, acts), (4) its goal (most honorable), (5) its proofs (certain and reliable).
These aspects are often cited in Islamic theology.
3
Note the alternative interpretation
The passage ends with an alternative view: that 'tawhid' here means faith (iman), which includes all three categories (divinity, prophethood, afterlife).
This resolves the naming issue differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) العقائد تشمل الإلهيات والنبوات والسمعيات، فلماذا سمي بعلم التوحيد؟ أو فلماذا اقتصر على التوحيد ؟ والجواب أنه الجزء الأهم أو أنه شرط صحة العبادات، ويبين شرفه خمس جهات: ۱ - شرف ما يبنى عليه وهو العلم بأحكام الألوهية، ۲ - شدة الحاجة إليه لكونه رئيس العلوم الدينية، ۳- جهة شرف معلوماته التي هي مباحث الذات والصفات والأفعال، ٤ - جهة شرف الغاية فإن غايته أشرف الغايات -٥ شرف أدلته فإنها أوثق الأدلة لأنها قطعية، أو يقال: إن المراد بالتوحيد الإيمان فيشمل الثلاثة. اه بتصرف [ حاشية السباعي ص ١٣٦]
(1) The beliefs include the divine matters, prophethoods, and the sam'iyyat. So why is it called the science of tawhid? Or why is it limited to tawhid? The answer is that it is the most important part, or that it is a condition for the validity of acts of worship. And it shows its honor in five aspects: 1. The honor of what it is based upon, which is the knowledge of the rulings of divinity. 2. The intense need for it because it is the chief of the religious sciences. 3. The aspect of the honor of its objects of knowledge, which are the investigations of the Essence, attributes, and acts. 4. The aspect of the honor of its goal, for its goal is the most honorable of goals. 5. The honor of its proofs, for they are the most reliable proofs because they are certain. Or it is said: by tawhid is meant faith, so it includes the three. End, with adaptation [Hashiyat al-Siba'i, p. 136].
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note (hashia) from the commentator al-Siba'i, explaining why the science is called tawhid despite encompassing divinity, prophethood, and sam'iyyat. The answer given is either that tawhid is the most important part or that it is a condition for worship validity. Then five aspects of its honor are enumerated: (1) It is built upon knowledge of divine rulings. (2) It is intensely needed as the chief of religious sciences. (3) Its objects of knowledge (Essence, attributes, acts) are honorable. (4) Its goal (eternal happiness) is the most honorable. (5) Its proofs are the most reliable because they are certain. An alternative view is that 'tawhid' here means faith, encompassing all three categories. The note ends with a source reference.

Key Terms
توحيد
شرف العلم
قطعيات
علم الألوهية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The science of tawhid only discusses God's unity and excludes prophethood and afterlife.
Correction: In fact, it includes these topics, but is called tawhid because it is the most important part or a condition for worship, or because 'tawhid' here means faith (iman) which encompasses all.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Hope, Means, and Three Supplications
Dardeer explains the verse seeking acceptance, benefit, and forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic concept of hope (rajā') as distinct from wishful thinking, and the three specific supplications Dardeer makes in his poem: acceptance, benefit, and forgiveness.

Core Idea
True hope in Allah requires taking means; wishful thinking is blameworthy.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer unpacks a line from his creed poem, clarifying that authentic hope in Allah is not mere wishful thinking but a state of the heart accompanied by sincere effort. He also explains three specific requests he makes: acceptance of his work, benefit from the creed, and forgiveness of slips. This lesson explores each element and its spiritual significance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the exclusivity of hope in Allah
Note how the fronting of 'Allah' in the poem emphasizes that hope is directed solely to Him. This teaches tawhid in reliance.
Dardeer highlights that putting the object first indicates exclusivity.
2
Distinguish hope from wishful thinking
Dardeer defines hope as heart's attachment with taking means; without means it is blameworthy wishfulness. Reflect on how this applies to your own practice.
Hope is praiseworthy only when accompanied by effort.
3
Examine the three supplications
Dardeer hopes for acceptance of the work, benefit from the creed, and forgiveness of slips. Each has a specific meaning; consider why he includes all three.
Acceptance means being pleased with the work; benefit is the opposite of harm; forgiveness covers sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله أرجو في قبولِ العَمَلِ * والنفْعَ منها ثُمَّ عَفْرَ الزلل (والله أرجو) قدم الاسم الاعظم لإفادة الاختصاص، إذ تقديم المعمول يُفيد ذلك، أي: لا أرجو إلا الله تعالى. والرجاءُ: تعلُّقُ القَلْبِ بحصولِ مرغوب فيه في المستقبلِ معَ الأَخْذِ في الأسباب، وهو ممدوح شَرْعًا، فإن لم يأخُذ في الأسبابِ فطمع، وهو مذمومٌ شَرْعًا. (في قبول العمل الذي منه تأليف هذه العقيدة، وقبولُ الشَّيء: الرّضا به وعدَمُ ردِّهِ. (و) أرجوه تعالى (النَّفْعَ) هو ضِدَّ الضَّر (۲)، (منها) أي : مِنْ هذه العقيدة، أي: بها، أي: أرجوه تعالى أنْ ينْفَعَ بها كلَّ مَنْ  َقرَأَها أو طالعها وحصَّلَها أو كتبها. ويصحُ أنْ تكونَ (مِنْ) ابتدائية، هي ومجرورُها حالٌ مِنَ النفع، أَيْ: حالَ كونِ النفع حاصلا وناشئًا منها. (ثُمَّ) أي: وأَرْجوه (غَفْرَ) أَيْ: سَتْرَ (الزِّلَلِ) جمع «زَلّةٍ»، بالفتْحِ مَصْدَرِ «زِلَّ» بنج الزاي أيضًا (يزل) بكَسْرِها ، يعني المعاصي.
And from Allah I hope for acceptance of the work * and benefit from it, then forgiveness of slips. (And from Allah I hope) He presented the greatest name to indicate exclusivity, for putting the object first indicates that, i.e., I hope from none but Allah Most High. Hope (rajā'): the heart's attachment to the obtaining of something desired in the future, along with taking means, and it is praiseworthy legally. If one does not take means, then it is wishfulness (tama'), and it is blameworthy legally. (for acceptance of the work) from which the composition of this creed comes, and acceptance of a thing: being pleased with it and not rejecting it. (And) I hope from Him Most High (the benefit) which is the opposite of harm (2), (from it) i.e., from this creed, i.e., by it, i.e., I hope from Him Most High that He benefit by it everyone who reads it or studies it, obtains it, or writes it. It is also valid that (min) be initiatory, with its governed noun as a circumstantial qualifier of benefit, i.e., the benefit occurring and arising from it. (Then) i.e., and I hope (for forgiveness) i.e., covering (of slips) plural of 'zalla' (slip), meaning sins.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the poem. Dardeer explains the line 'And from Allah I hope for acceptance of the work, and benefit from it, then forgiveness of slips.' He first notes that the object 'Allah' is fronted to indicate exclusivity: 'I hope only from Allah.' Then he defines hope (rajā') as the heart's attachment to a future desired outcome, but with the condition of taking the necessary means. This type of hope is praiseworthy; if one hopes without taking means, it is vain wish (tama'), which is blameworthy. He then explains 'acceptance of the work'—acceptance means being pleased with it and not rejecting it. The 'benefit' is the opposite of harm, and he hopes that Allah benefits by this creed anyone who reads, studies, obtains, or writes it. The preposition 'min' can be initiatory, meaning that the benefit originates from the creed. Finally, 'then forgiveness of slips'—slips meaning sins—he hopes for covering and forgiveness of sins.

رجاء
طمع
قبول العمل
نفع
غفر
زلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hope in Allah is simply wishing for good without effort.
Correction: True hope (rajā') requires taking the necessary means; without means it is vain wish (tama') and blameworthy.

Checkpoint
According to Dardeer, what distinguishes praiseworthy hope from blameworthy wishfulness?
A) The intensity of desire
B) Taking the necessary means
C) The number of good deeds
D) Public declaration

Key Takeaway
Authentic hope in Allah is active, involving sincere effort; Dardeer's supplications teach us to seek acceptance, benefit, and forgiveness through our works.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Dardeer asks for forgiveness of slips — all of them. Are you hiding from admitting your sins, or do you bring them to Allah with the confidence that He covers and forgives? What keeps you from that candidness?

Hope, Acceptance, Benefit, and Forgiveness
Dardir defines hope and wishful thinking, and enumerates three objects of hope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic concept of hope (rajā'), its distinction from wishful thinking (ṭama'), and the three specific hopes expressed by the poet: acceptance of deeds, benefit from the creed, and forgiveness of slips.

Core Idea
True hope is accompanied by action; wishful thinking is blameworthy.

Imam Dardir begins his commentary by analyzing the poet's declaration of hope in Allah. He defines hope as a praiseworthy attachment of the heart that requires taking the necessary means, warning against empty wishful thinking. He then elaborates on three objects of hope: that Allah accepts the work, benefits others through it, and forgives the author's slips. This lesson unpacks these foundational concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Dardir emphasizes exclusivity by placing 'Allah' first.
The name of Allah is fronted to indicate that hope is directed to Him alone.
2
Understand the definition of rajā' and ṭama'
Focus on the condition of taking means. Hope is praiseworthy when accompanied by effort; without it, it becomes blameworthy wishful thinking.
3
Examine the three hoped-for things
Identify each hope: acceptance of the work, benefit from it, and forgiveness of slips. Note how Dardir defines acceptance and benefit, and how forgiveness relates to concealment of sins.

(۱) القبول هو الإثابة على العمل الصحيح وعدم رده، والرضا يستلزم عدم الرد. (۲) الضر بالفتح هو المصدر، وبالضم الاسم وهو أثر الفعل، والأول نفس الفعل، يقال: ضَرَّه ضراً فقام به ضر. (وسترها صادقٌ بِمَحْوِها مِنَ الصُّحُفِ ) وبِعَدَمِ المؤاخذة بها، وإِنْ كَانَتْ مَوجودةً فيها، وورَدَ في السُّنَّةِ ما يدل لكل، والمرجو مِنْ سَعَةِ كرَمِه تعالى الأوَّلُ (٢). (١) أي إزالتها بالكلية. (۲) ولما جمع بين رجاء قبولها والنفع منها، كان ذلك إشارة إلى أن العمل لله مع إرادة الثواب جائز، وإن كان غيره أكمل منه، لأن درجات الإخلاص ثلاث: عليا ووسطى ودنيا، - فالعليا أن يعمل لله وحده امتثالا للأمر وقيامًا بحق العبودية وإن أعلمه الله أنه معاقب والوسطى أن يعمل للثواب الآخرة - والمرتبة الدنيا أن يعمل للإكرام في الدنيا والسلامة من آفاتها، وما عدا هذه الثلاث فهو من الرياء.
(And Allah I hope) — He placed the greatest name first to indicate exclusivity, for advancing the object of the action indicates that, i.e., I hope in none except Allah Almighty. And hope (ar-rajā') is the heart's attachment to obtaining something desirable in the future while taking the means; it is praiseworthy legally. If one does not take the means, it is wishful thinking (at-ṭama'), which is blameworthy legally. (for acceptance of the work from which is the composition of this creed [al-ʿaqīdah], and acceptance of a thing is being pleased with it and not rejecting it. (And) I hope from Him Almighty (benefit) — it is the opposite of harm, (from it) i.e., from this creed, meaning by it: I hope from Him Almighty that He benefit by it everyone who reads it, studies it, memorizes it, or writes it. And it is valid that (min) be partitive, and it with its object is a circumstantial qualifier of benefit, i.e., while benefit is produced and arising from it. (Then) i.e., and I hope (forgiveness) i.e., concealment (of slips) — the plural of 'zillah', with fatḥ, verbal noun of 'zalla' with fatḥ of the zāy also (yazillu) with kasr, meaning sins. (1) Acceptance (al-qabūl) is rewarding for the correct deed and not rejecting it, and being pleased entails not rejecting. (2) Harm (ad-ḍarr) with fatḥ is the source, and with ḍamm is the noun, which is the effect of the act; the first is the act itself. One says: 'ḍarrahū ḍarran' so harm arose in him. (And its concealment is truly by erasing it from the scrolls) and by not holding one to account for it, even if it exists in them; and in the Sunnah is what indicates each, and what is hoped from the vastness of His generosity is the first. (1) i.e., removing it entirely. (2) And since he combined hoping for its acceptance and benefit from it, that is an indication that acting for Allah with desire for reward is permissible, though other than it is more complete. For the degrees of sincerity (al-ikhlāṣ) are three: high, middle, and low. The high is to act for Allah alone, in compliance with the command and fulfilling the right of servitude even if Allah informs him that he will be punished; the middle is to act for the reward of the Hereafter; and the low degree is to act for honor in this world and safety from its afflictions; and any than these three is hypocrisy (ar-riyā').
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the opening couplet of the poem 'Al-Kharīdat al-Bahiyyah'. The author, Imam Dardir, explains the meaning of the poet's words, which express hope in Allah for three things: acceptance of the work (the creed itself), benefit from it, and forgiveness of slips. First, Dardir analyzes the phrasing 'Wa-Allāhu arjū' – by placing the name of Allah first, the poet indicates exclusivity: he hopes in none but Allah. Then he defines 'ar-rajā'' (hope) linguistically and legally, distinguishing it from 'at-ṭama'' (wishful thinking). The key is that hope is praiseworthy when accompanied by taking the means; without means, it becomes blameworthy wishful thinking. Next, he discusses acceptance ('qabūl'): acceptance of a thing means being pleased with it and not rejecting it; in the context of deeds, acceptance is rewarding the correct deed and not rejecting it. Then he explains the second hope: benefit (naf') – the poet hopes that Allah will benefit others through this work, whether they read, study, memorize, or write it. The preposition 'min' can indicate that the benefit arises directly from the creed. Then the third hope: forgiveness of slips (zalal), i.e., sins. Concealment can be by erasing them from the record or by not taking to account while they remain; but from Allah's generosity, the first is hoped for. Finally, a long footnote adds that hoping for acceptance and benefit together indicates that acting for Allah with a desire for reward is permissible, though higher degrees of sincerity (ikhlāṣ) exist. Dardir then enumerates three degrees: 1) the highest – acting solely for Allah, in obedience and servitude, even if punishment is expected; 2) the middle – acting for the reward of the Hereafter; 3) the lowest – acting for worldly honor and safety. Anything beyond these three is hypocrisy. This classification teaches that sincerity has levels, and it is permissible to aim for reward, but the best is pure devotion.

Key Terms

قبول
نفع
غفر
زلل
طمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hope (rajā') is merely wishful thinking without any action.
Correction: Islamic hope requires taking the necessary means; otherwise, it is blameworthy wishful thinking (ṭama').

Checkpoint
What distinguishes praiseworthy hope (rajā') from blameworthy wishful thinking (ṭama') according to Imam Dardir?
A) Praiseworthy hope is for worldly matters, blameworthy for religious matters
B) Praiseworthy hope is accompanied by taking the necessary means, blameworthy is without action
C) Praiseworthy hope is for acceptance of deeds, blameworthy is for forgiveness
D) Praiseworthy hope is for the Hereafter, blameworthy is for this world

Key Takeaway
True hope in Allah is always coupled with effort; merely wishing without action is blameworthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hope for something from Allah, do you actually take the means, or do you merely wish? Examine your heart: are you mixing rajā' with ṭama'? What will you change today to act on your hope?
?
You have recited this creed or studied it; do you truly hope for its acceptance? What does that require of you in terms of sincerity and action?
?
Reflect on the forgiveness you hope for. Are you taking the means to avoid sins, or are you relying on wishful thinking? What step can you take right now to act on your hope?

The Three Degrees of Sincerity
Dardir classifies sincerity into three degrees and relates them to hope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three levels of sincerity (ikhlāṣ) as classified by Imam Dardir: the highest (acting for Allah alone), the middle (acting for reward), and the lowest (acting for worldly benefit), and how hoping for reward relates to these degrees.

Core Idea
Sincerity has levels; the highest is pure servitude regardless of reward or punishment.

After discussing the poet's hope for acceptance and benefit, Dardir adds an important clarification: hoping for reward is permissible, but it is not the highest form of sincerity. He then categorizes sincerity into three degrees—high, middle, and low—and warns that anything beyond these is hypocrisy. This lesson explains these degrees and their relationship to hope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sincerity
Read the Arabic footnote and its translation. Notice that Dardir introduces the degrees after linking them to the earlier discussion of hope.
The passage begins with 'And since he combined hoping for its acceptance...'
2
Identify the three levels
List the high, middle, and low degrees, noting the motivation and goal for each.
The high: acting for Allah even if punishment is expected; middle: acting for reward; low: acting for worldly honor.
3
Understand the connection to hope
Dardir says hoping for reward is permissible (jā'iz) but falling into a lower degree. Recognize that the highest sincerity transcends hope for reward.
This complements the earlier lesson on hope.

The Status of the Classification of Rational Judgments
The commentator explains why the division is a muqaddimat al-kitab not muqaddimat al-ilm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the division of rational judgments is a 'preliminary of the book' unique to kalam, not a universal preliminary of science.

Core Idea
This classification is specific to kalam, not a universal science foundation.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the technical status of the division of rational judgments. He distinguishes between a preliminary of the science (needed in every science) and a preliminary of the book (specific to a particular book's subject). This distinction prevents confusion about the scope of this classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author defines the two types of preliminaries.
The passage uses technical terms like 'muqaddimat al-ilm' and 'muqaddimat al-kitab'.
2
Identify the two types of preliminaries
Find the definitions for 'muqaddimat al-ilm' and 'muqaddimat al-kitab' as given in the passage. Note what each one includes.
Muqaddimat al-ilm must be needed in every science; muqaddimat al-kitab is specific to a subject.
3
Understand the instrumental attribution
Reflect on the final sentence: the relation of judgment to intellect is like an instrument. How does this connect to the classification?
The intellect is the tool through which rational judgments are made.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this distinction matters for the study of kalam. How does it prevent misunderstanding?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن تقسيم الحكم العقلي إلى هذه الأقسام الثلاثة لا يقال له مقدمة علم، لأنه لا يحتاج له إلا في هذا العلم بخصوصه، ولا يقال له مقدمة علم إلا ما يحتاج له في كل علم، وهو الحد والموضوع والغاية. وإنما تسمى مقدمة كتاب وهى ما قدمت أمام المقصود بالذات لارتباط له بها وانتفاع بما فيه، لأن أقسام الحكم العقلي مخصوصة بالكتب المؤلفة في هذا الفن. نسبته للعقل من نسبة الشيء لآلته؛ أي فالحكم آلته العقل.
And know that the division of rational judgment into these three divisions is not called a 'preliminary of the science' (muqaddimat al-ilm), because it is needed only in this particular science, and nothing is called a preliminary of the science except what is needed in every science, which is definition, subject, and goal. Rather, it is called a 'preliminary of the book' (muqaddimat al-kitab), which is what is placed before the intended subject due to its connection to it and benefit from it, because the divisions of rational judgment are specific to books authored in this art. Its attribution to the intellect is the attribution of a thing to its instrument; i.e., the intellect is the instrument of judgment.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage clarifies the technical status of the classification of rational judgments. The commentator states that this division is not a muqaddimat al-ilm (preliminary of the science) because a muqaddimat al-ilm is only something needed in every science, such as definitions, subject matter, and goal. Since this classification is only needed in kalam, it is not a universal prerequisite. Instead, it is called a muqaddimat al-kitab (preliminary of the book), meaning something placed at the beginning of a book because it is connected to and beneficial for the main subject. The commentator also explains the attribution of the judgment to the intellect: it is an attribution of a thing to its instrument, so the judgment is made by the intellect as an instrument. This nuanced distinction prevents misunderstanding about the scope of these rational judgments.

Key Terms
مقدمة علم
مقدمة كتاب
الحكم العقلي
آلة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the classification of rational judgments is a universal prerequisite for all sciences.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is only a preliminary of the book, specific to kalam, not a preliminary of every science.

Key Takeaway

Classification of Judgment: Legal, Customary, and Rational
Dardeer classifies judgment into sharʿī and non-sharʿī, defining sharʿī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between legal (sharʿī) and non-legal judgment, and the roles of reason and custom in non-legal judgments.

Core Idea
Judgment is either from Allah's address or from reason and custom.

Imam Dardeer begins by clarifying the different types of judgment. Not every judgment we make comes from divine law; some are based on reason or custom. This foundational classification helps us avoid conflating religious rulings with rational or conventional assessments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the complete passage from the matn along with the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the framework.
Pay attention to the terms used for each category.
2
Identify the two broad categories of judgment
Notice that judgment (ḥukm) is first divided into sharʿī and non-sharʿī. The sharʿī is defined as Allah's address; non-sharʿī is whatever else.
3
Understand the definition of legal judgment (sharʿī)
Sharʿī judgment is the address of Allah relating to actions of the obligated (mukallafūn). It comes in three forms: command (ṭalab), permission (ibāḥa), or stipulation (waḍʿ) for these two.
The mention of 'customary and rational' in this part may be a scribal insertion; the main point is the three forms.
4
Recognize the components of non-legal judgment
Non-sharʿī judgment consists of affirming or negating something of something. The source (ḥākim) for such judgments is either reason (ʿaql) or custom (ʿāda).
This separates rational and customary verdicts from divine law.
5
Note the common misunderstanding
Some might think that customary judgments (e.g., 'fire burns') are part of Islamic law. The passage clearly places them outside sharʿī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و«الحكم»: إمّا شرعيٌّ، وهو: خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين بالطلب أو الإباحة والعادي والعقلي أو الوضع لهما. وإمّا غَيرُه، وهو: إثبات أمرٍ لأمرٍ أو نفيه عنه، والحاكم به إمّا العقل وإمّا العادة.
And 'judgment' (al-ḥukm) is either legal (sharʿī), which is: the address of Allah Most High relating to the actions of the obligated ones, by command or permission, and the customary and the rational, or the stipulation (al-waḍʿ) for them both. And it may be other than that, which is: affirming something of something or negating it from it, and the judge (al-ḥākim) is either reason or custom.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of judgment (ḥukm) as understood in uṣūl al-fiqh and ʿilm al-kalām. The author states that judgment (ḥukm) is either sharʿī (legal), which is defined as the address of Allah concerning the actions of the morally responsible (mukallafūn). This address is by way of command (ṭalab), permission (ibāḥa), or stipulation (waḍʿ). The mention of 'al-ʿādī wa al-ʿaqlī' (customary and rational) in this context is ambiguous; it may be a heading for the second category or an insertion. The passage then clarifies that other than sharʿī, there is a non-sharʿī judgment which consists of affirming or denying something of something, and the source of this judgment is either reason (ʿaql) or custom (ʿāda). Thus, the non-sharʿī judgments are divided into rational and customary. This classification is fundamental in kalam for distinguishing between revealed law and natural reasoning.

Key Terms
حكم
شرعي
طلب
إباحة
وضع
عادي
عقلي
حاكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that customary judgments (like 'fire burns') are part of Islamic law (sharʿī).
Correction: The passage classifies customary judgments as non-sharʿī, distinct from divine commands and permissions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is included in the definition of legal judgment (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī)?
A) Only command and prohibition
B) Command, permission, and stipulation
C) Only rational judgments
D) Customary linkages

Key Takeaway
The classification of judgment into sharʿī (divine address) and non-sharʿī (reason/custom) is fundamental for distinguishing theological and legal reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision in your life, how often do you check whether it aligns with Allah's address (sharʿī) versus your own reasoning or societal custom? Can you identify a specific area where you have been following custom rather than divine command? What will you change about that starting today?
?
The passage distinguishes between judgments from Allah and those from reason or custom. Is there a belief you hold that you have assumed is from Allah but actually comes from your culture? How would you verify its source using the criterion given here?
?
The rational judgment is described as affirming or negating something. In what specific aspect of your faith have you relied on your own rational affirmation or negation without grounding it in divine address? Commit to one action this week to seek the sharʿī ruling on it.

Summary and Detailed Evidence, and the Ruling on Taqlid
Dardeer distinguishes summary from detailed evidence and discusses taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between summary and detailed evidence in Islamic theology, and the debated status of taqlid.

Core Idea
Summary proof suffices for knowledge by consensus; detailed proof is a collective duty.

Dardeer begins by distinguishing between two types of evidence used to establish theological knowledge. The first, summary evidence, is sufficient for belief by scholarly consensus. The second, detailed evidence, is required only as a collective obligation. He then touches on taqlid, noting disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
The passage covers three distinct points.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
Look for the definitions of summary and detailed evidence and note their different rulings (consensus vs collective obligation).
Consider the example given for summary proof.
3
Understand taqlid and its disagreement
Read the definition of taqlid and observe that there is disagreement among scholars about its validity.
This passage does not resolve the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يكفي في المعرفة الدليلُ الجُمْلي) اتفاقا، وهو «المعجوزُ عَنْ تفصيله وحل الشَّبَه عنه»، كأَنْ يُعرَفَ وُجوده تعالى بكونه خالقا للعالم. وأما التفصيلي وهو المقدورُ فيه على ما ذُكِرَ فلا يجبُ عَينيًّا، بلْ وُجوبًا كفائيا لصونِ الدين بدفع الخصوم. وأما التقليد، وهو الأخُذُ بِقَولِ الغَيرِ مِنْ غَيرِ حُجّةٍ، أي: الاعتقاد الجازمُ الْمُتَمَسَّلُ فيه بمُجَرَّدِ قَولِ الغَيرِ، فقد اختُلِفَ فيه
And that which suffices for knowledge is the summary proof by consensus, and it is 'that which one is unable to detail and resolve the doubts about it', such as knowing His existence the Most High by His being the Creator of the world. As for the detailed proof, which is that which one is able to do what was mentioned, it is not individually obligatory, but rather a collective obligation for the preservation of the religion by repelling opponents. As for taqlid, which is taking the statement of another without proof, i.e., the firm belief adopted solely by the statement of another, there is disagreement concerning it.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage discusses the types of evidence sufficient for knowledge and the ruling on taqlid. The author first states that the summary proof (al-dalil al-ijmali) suffices for knowledge by consensus. This is defined as evidence that one cannot elaborate in detail or resolve doubts about. An example is knowing God's existence by the fact that He is the Creator of the world. Then he contrasts it with the detailed proof (al-dalil al-tafsili), which is the evidence that one can elaborate as mentioned. This is not wajib 'ayni (individually obligatory), but wajib kifa'i (collectively obligatory) to preserve the religion by repelling opponents. Finally, he discusses taqlid, defined as accepting another's statement without proof, i.e., a firm belief based solely on another's statement. He notes that there is disagreement about its validity; he does not resolve it here but implies it is debated.

الدليل التفصيلي

وجوب كفائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid is always invalid and unacceptable.
Correction: The passage shows that there is disagreement about taqlid, implying it is not universally condemned.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Summary and Detailed Evidence in Theology
Dardeer distinguishes between summary and detailed evidence and their respective obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between summary and detailed evidence and why each has a different obligation in theological knowledge.

Core Idea
Summary evidence suffices for personal belief; detailed evidence protects the faith communally.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer addresses a practical question: how much evidence does a Muslim need to know fundamental truths? He distinguishes between two levels of proof: a general summary evidence that is enough for personal conviction, and a detailed evidence that equips one to debate and defend. Understanding this distinction clarifies the communal nature of theological scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two categories: summary evidence (dalīl ijmālī) and detailed evidence (dalīl tafṣīlī).
The passage uses technical terms; don't rush.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms 'الدليل الجملي' and 'الدليل التفصيلي' and understand their definitions given in the text.
The definitions are embedded: summary evidence is what one cannot detail; detailed evidence is what one can.
3
Understand the obligation types
Notice that summary evidence is sufficient by consensus (individual obligation), while detailed evidence is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya). Ask: why would detailed evidence not be required of every individual?
Dardeer says it is for 'repelling opponents' – a communal defense.
4
Reflect on the example
The example of knowing God as Creator illustrates summary evidence. Think of other simple proofs that would fall under this category.
Example: the order of the universe points to a Creator.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
This passage shows the balance between personal faith and scholarly responsibility. How does this apply today?
Not all are required to be theologians, but some must be.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يكفي في المعرفة الدليل الجُمْلي اتفاقا، وهو «المعجوز عن تفصيله وحل الشبه عنه»، كأن يُعْرَفَ وجوده تعالى بكونه خالقا للعالم. وأما التفصيلي وهو المقدور فيه على ما ذُكِرَ فلا يجب عينيًّا، بل وجوبا كفائيا لصون الدين بدفع الخصوم.
And what suffices for knowledge is the summary evidence (al-dalīl al-ijmālī) by consensus, and it is 'that which one is incapable of detailing and resolving doubts about,' such as knowing His existence by His being the Creator of the world. As for the detailed evidence (al-tafṣīlī), which is that which one is capable of as mentioned, it is not individually obligatory, but rather a communal obligation to protect the religion by repelling opponents.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage explains two types of evidence in theology. The first is summary evidence (dalīl ijmālī), which provides a general proof without detailed elaboration. This is considered sufficient for knowledge by consensus. An example is knowing God's existence simply by recognizing that He created the world. The second is detailed evidence (dalīl tafṣīlī), which involves precise argumentation and the ability to resolve doubts. This type is not individually obligatory (farḍ ʿayn) but is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya), because it serves the purpose of defending the religion against opponents.

الدليل التفصيلي

الوجوب الكفائي

Correction: The passage clarifies that detailed evidence is a communal obligation, not an individual one; summary evidence suffices for personal conviction.

Key Takeaway
Summary evidence is enough for personal faith; detailed evidence is a communal duty to protect the religion.

The Correct View on Taqlid and Nazar
Dardeer states the correct view and discusses the obligation of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct view that taqlid suffices for valid faith but speculation (nazar) is still obligatory for knowledge.

Core Idea
Taqlid saves faith but not obedience; nazar is a separate duty.

Imam Dardeer begins by stating the predominant view that imitation (taqlid) in the foundational beliefs of faith is sufficient for the validity of faith. But he immediately raises a crucial question: if faith by imitation is valid, does that exempt one from the duty of rational speculation (al-nazar)? This passage establishes the nuanced position that while the imitator's faith is valid, he remains sinful if he neglects the obligatory means to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how Dardeer presents the correct view first, then raises the question about nazar.
2
Identify the two sub-opinions on nazar
Look for the two possibilities: one that nazar is obligatory (and its neglect is sinful) and another that it is merely a condition of perfection. Note which one Dardeer indicates as correct.
The correct view is that nazar is obligatory, as understood from the phrase 'knowledge of Allah'.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on how this view balances the validity of the imitator's faith with the duty to seek knowledge. Why is speculation obligatory even when faith is already valid?
Speculation is the means to knowledge, and knowledge itself is obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقيل: إنه يكفي في عقائد الإيمان وهو الصحيح، فإيمانُ المُقَلِّدِ صحيحٌ. وعليه، فهل يحِبُّ النَّظَرُ؟ فيكونُ معَ صِحَةِ إيمانِه عاصيا بتَرْكِ النَّظَرِ المُوَصِّل للمَعْرِفِةِ - وهو الصحيحُ كما يُفهَم مِنْ قولنا معْرِفةُ اللهِ»- أوْ لا، بل هو شَرطُ كمال؟ وقيل: لا يكفي، فالمقلد كافر. وقيل: يكفي إِنْ قلَّدَ القُرآنَ والسُّنَّةَ القَطْعِيةَ، وفيه نظر. وذهَبَ بعضُهم إلى تحريم النَّظَرِ ، لأنه مظنّةُ الوقوع في الشُّبَهِ والضلال، وليسَ بشَيء.
It was said: It (imitation) suffices for the beliefs of faith, and that is the correct view, so the faith of the imitator is valid. Upon this, is speculation required? So he is, while his faith is valid, disobedient in refraining from speculation that leads to knowledge - and that is the correct view as understood from our statement 'knowledge of Allah' - or not, rather it is a condition of perfection? And it was said: It does not suffice, so the imitator is an unbeliever. And it was said: It suffices if he imitates the Qur'an and the certain Sunnah, and there is consideration in this. And some held the prohibition of speculation, because it is a likely cause of falling into doubts and misguidance, but it is nothing.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents and evaluates different opinions on the sufficiency of imitation (taqlid) in the fundamentals of faith and the ruling on speculative reasoning (al-nazar). The author begins by stating the correct view: imitation suffices, so the imitator's faith is valid. Then he raises the question: if faith is valid by imitation, is speculation still required? He presents the correct view: the imitator is disobedient in refraining from speculation that leads to knowledge, because speculation is a means to the obligatory knowledge. Then he mentions the alternative view that speculation is merely a condition of perfection, not obligatory. Then he presents the view that imitation does not suffice, making the imitator an unbeliever. Then a moderate view: imitation suffices if it is of the Qur'an and certain Sunnah, but he notes this view is subject to consideration. Finally, he refutes the view that speculation is prohibited, arguing it is baseless because it would entail contradicting the Qur'an and Sunnah which enjoin speculation, and would imply that all scholars who engaged in speculation were disobedient.

Key Terms
التقليد
النظر
المقلد
عقائد الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view presented in this passage, what is the ruling on a person who has valid faith through imitation but does not engage in speculation?
A) His faith is invalid.
B) His faith is valid and he is not sinful.
C) His faith is valid but he is sinful for leaving speculation.
D) He is an unbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid gives valid faith, but nazar remains an obligation to attain knowledge; neglecting it is sinful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that taqlid gives valid faith, but nazar is an obligation—neglecting it is disobedience. When you think about your own core beliefs about Allah, do you rely on what you were raised with, or have you personally examined them through nazar? If you haven't, what real step will you take today to begin that inquiry, knowing that postponement is a sin?
?
The phrase 'knowledge of Allah' is the goal of nazar. Ask yourself: Do you truly know Allah, or do you only know about Him from others? If your connection to Him lacks personal, investigative depth, what fears or excuses prevent you from seeking that knowledge—and are you willing to set them aside starting now?
?
This lesson separates the validity of your faith from your duty to pursue knowledge. Even if your faith is correct, are you comfortable with the thought that you might be sinning daily by ignoring nazar? How does it affect your relationship with Allah to know that He requires you to use your intellect to know Him, and that neglecting that is not just a missed opportunity but an act of disobedience? What will you do about it this week?

Other Opinions on Taqlid and Refutation of Prohibition
Dardeer presents and evaluates three alternative opinions on taqlid and nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative opinions on taqlid—including that the imitator is an unbeliever or that only certain imitation suffices—and why the view that prohibits speculation is baseless.

Core Idea
Not all opinions on taqlid are equal; the view that prohibits nazar is critically invalid.

After presenting the correct view, Imam Dardeer turns to other opinions held by scholars. He mentions a strict position that deems the imitator an unbeliever, a moderate one that allows imitation only from the Qur'an and Sunnah, and finally an extreme view that prohibits speculation altogether. He dismisses the last as baseless, showing that the path of knowledge cannot be closed.

Guided Path
1
Read the remainder of the passage
Read the Arabic for the three remaining opinions and their evaluation. Notice how Dardeer categorizes them.
The first opinion is the strictest, the second has some merit but is questionable, the third is rejected.
2
Analyze each opinion
For each opinion, note the ruling on the imitator and the evidence used. Consider why Dardeer says of the second 'fihi nazar' (it is subject to consideration).
3
Understand the refutation
What is the basis for rejecting the prohibition of nazar? Dardeer says it is 'laysa bi shay' (nothing). Why would it be invalid?
Prohibiting nazar contradicts the Qur'an and Sunnah that command reflection.

Knowledge: The First Obligation and Its Basis in Sharia
Dardeer states that ma'rifa is the first obligation and refutes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of Allah is the first obligation and why it is imposed by the Sharia, not by reason alone.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah is the door to all obligations, and it is required by divine command, not human reason.

This passage from Imam Dardeer's Sharh al-Kharidah al-Bahiyyah establishes a foundational principle: the very first thing required of every accountable person is to know Allah. Dardeer clarifies that this duty comes from the Sharia, not from reason, and he defends this view against the Mu'tazila objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words sink in before interpreting.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the two-part claim
Dardeer asserts that knowledge is the first obligation and that it is by Sharia, not reason. Find where he states each part.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila argue that if knowledge is not obligatory by reason, messengers could be silenced. Understand their reasoning step by step.
The phrase 'silencing of the messengers' is a key concept.
4
Understand Dardeer's answer
Dardeer replies that the obligation to comply depends on the actual reality of the obligation, not on the person's knowledge of it. See how this refutes the Mu'tazila.
This answer preserves the role of Sharia without epistemic preconditions.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it grounds religious duty in divine command rather than human reason, shaping the entire framework of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلَمْ أَنَّ المعرفة هي أوَّلُ واجب على المُكَلِّفِ، إذ جميع الواجبات مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عليها. وقولُه (فاعرِفِ) أَيْ: اعرف أنها واجبةٌ بالشرع لا بالعقل، خلافًا للمُعْتَزِلة. المعتزلة قالوا: لو لم تجب المعرفة بالعقل للزم إفحام الرسل، لأن المرسل إليه يقول لا أنظر إلا إذا ثبت عندي وجوب النظر ، ولا يثبت مالم أنظر فيما تدعوني إليه فأنا لا أنظر أصلا، والجواب عن هذه الشبهة أن وجوب الامتثال لا يتوقف على العلم بوجوب النظر بل على ثبوته في الواقع.
And know that knowledge (ma'rifa) is the first obligation upon the legally responsible person, since all obligations are dependent upon it. And His statement (so know [fa'rif]) means: know that it is obligatory by the Law (shar'), not by the intellect, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila said: If knowledge were not obligatory by the intellect, that would necessitate the silencing of the messengers, because the one to whom the message is sent would say: I will not speculate unless it is established for me that speculation is obligatory, and it is not established unless I speculate about what you call me to, so I will not speculate at all. The answer to this specious argument is that the obligation of compliance is not dependent on the knowledge of the obligation of speculation, but on its actual reality.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage states that knowledge (ma'rifa) of Allah is the very first obligation upon every person who is legally responsible (mukallaf), because all other obligations depend on it. The author then explains the meaning of the Quranic imperative 'so know' (fa'rif): it means to know that this obligation is imposed by the Law (shar'), not by reason, contrary to the Mu'tazila who held it is obligatory by reason. The author then presents and answers the Mu'tazila's objection: if knowledge were not obligatory by reason, the messengers could be silenced because the recipient could refuse to speculate until reason proves the obligation. The answer is that the obligation to comply with the messenger does not depend on the recipient's knowledge of the obligation of speculation, but on the fact that the obligation actually exists in reality; once the message is delivered, the duty to respond is in place, regardless of the recipient's epistemic state about the obligation of speculation.

Key Terms
المعرفة
الواجب
الشرع
العقل
المعتزلة
وجوب النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the obligation of knowledge is by reason alone, but the author insists it is by sharia.
Correction: The obligation to know Allah is imposed by the Sharia, not by unaided reason.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the Mu'tazila's objection that if knowledge were not obligatory by reason, it would silence the messengers?
A) He says the messengers have miracles.
B) He says obligation to comply depends on the actual reality of the obligation, not on the person's knowledge of it.
C) He says reason alone suffices.
D) He says the objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of Allah is the first obligation, and it is required by divine revelation, not by unaided reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before reading this lesson, did you assume that the obligation to seek knowledge of Allah was obvious by reason, or did you rely on revelation? How does Dardeer's position challenge your own assumption about the source of your most basic religious duty?
?
Reflect on a time when you delayed an Islamic obligation because you felt you needed a rational justification first. How does Dardeer's argument that compliance depends on actual reality (not your knowledge of the obligation) call you to action now—without waiting for full intellectual certainty?
?
The Mu'tazila's vicious circle is an intellectual trap: they demand proof before submitting. In what area of your life are you still demanding a proof that Allah has already given you, refusing to submit until your own mind consents? What would it cost you to submit based on the message itself?

Tafwid and Ta'wil: Two Valid Approaches to Ambiguous Texts
Explanation of poem line on tafwid and ta'wil for ambiguous divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methods of interpreting ambiguous divine attributes—ta'wil tafsili and tafwid—and the scholarly preference between them.

Core Idea
Both tafwid and ta'wil are valid; the difference is one of preference, not permissibility.

When we encounter Qur'anic or hadith texts that seem to imply anthropomorphism, scholars have outlined two valid responses. The poet al-Laqani succinctly presents both options in his poem, and the commentary clarifies their meanings and the debate over which is superior. This lesson unpacks that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to see the original wording and its literal meaning. Notice the structure of the poem line and the footnote.
Pay attention to the words 'أوله' and 'فوض'.
2
Identify the two methods
The poem gives two commands: 'interpret it' (ta'wil tafsili) and 'or delegate' (tafwid). Understand what each method entails: ta'wil assigns a specific interpretation that negates unworthy meanings; tafwid refrains from specifying, leaving the true meaning to Allah.
The particle 'or' indicates choice, not contradiction.
3
Understand the reasoning behind preference
The footnote explains that the Khalaf prefer ta'wil for its clarity and refutation of opponents, while the Salaf prefer tafwid for its safety from assigning an unintended meaning. Grasp this difference in priorities.
Both methods uphold divine transcendence; the disagreement is about which is better (afdaliyya).
4
Appreciate the unity
Recognize that both methods are valid and necessary. The passage emphasizes that committing either is sufficient, and the disagreement is only about preference, not permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقَولُ العلامة اللقاني: وكل نص أوهم التشبيها (۱) * أوله» أي: تفصيلا، وقوله: أو فَوِّضُ أَي: بأن تؤوّله إجمالا على معنى أنك لا تُعينُ له عَحْمَلًا، بدليل قوله بعده: ورُمْ تنزيها» و «أو» في كلامه - رحمه الله - للتخيير . (1) أي باعتبار ظاهر دلالته والمراد بـ «أوهم التشبيها» أي: أوقع في الوهم معنى غير لائق ومذهب أهل الحق من السلف والخلف تأويل الظاهر لوجوب تنزيهه تعالى عنه، وقدم التأويا على التفويض لأرجحيته لقول أهل هذا العلم طريقة الخلف أعلم وأحكم لما فيها من مزيد الإيضاح والرد على الخصوم، وطريقة السلف ومنهم الأئمة الأربعة أسلم للسلامة من تعيين معنى يكون غير مراد له تعالى، والخلاف إنما هو في الأولوية وإلا فارتكاب كل كاف. [الصاوي على الجوهرة ص ٢١٣]
So the saying of the scholar al-Laqani: 'And every text that suggests anthropomorphism (1), interpret it' i.e., in detail, and his saying: 'or delegate' i.e., that you interpret it summarily in the sense that you do not assign to it a [specific] meaning, as evidence by his saying after it: 'and intend transcendence,' and 'or' in his speech - may Allah have mercy on him - is for choice. (1) That is, considering its apparent indication, and the intended meaning of 'suggests anthropomorphism' is: it places in the mind an unseemly meaning, and the school of the people of truth among the Salaf and Khalaf is to interpret the apparent [meaning] due to the obligation of declaring Him transcendent above it, and they gave precedence to interpretation over delegation because of its superiority, according to the saying of the people of this knowledge: 'the way of the Khalaf is more knowledgeable and wiser because it contains more clarification and refutation of opponents, and the way of the Salaf, including the four Imams, is safer for safety from assigning a meaning that is not intended for Him, Exalted is He, and the disagreement is only about preference, otherwise committing [tafwid] is sufficient.' [al-Sawi on al-Jawhara, p. 213]
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage explains the poem line by al-Laqani that addresses how to handle Qur'anic or hadith texts that seem to imply anthropomorphism (tashbih). The author first quotes the poet's line: 'And every text that suggests anthropomorphism, interpret it' — meaning provide a detailed interpretation (ta'wil tafsili) that explains the intended meaning in a way that negates any unworthy attribute. The alternative given in the poem is 'or delegate' (fawwid), which means to refrain from specifying a particular interpretation, acknowledging that the apparent meaning is not intended while leaving the true meaning to Allah. The author notes that the particle 'or' here indicates a choice between two valid methods. The footnote clarifies that the 'suggestion of anthropomorphism' refers to the apparent wording placing an unworthy meaning in the mind. The correct approach, according to both Salaf and Khalaf, is to interpret such texts in a manner befitting Allah's transcendence. However, the Khalaf (later scholars) prefer detailed ta'wil because it provides clearer explanation and refutes opponents, while the Salaf (and the four Imams) prefer tafwid as it is safer, avoiding the risk of assigning a meaning that Allah did not intend. The disagreement is only about which method is better (afdaliyya), not about permissibility — both are permissible and necessary for upholding divine transcendence.

Key Terms
أوهم التشبيها
أوله
فوض
التفويض
التأويل
السلف
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ta'wil and tafwid are mutually exclusive or that one is invalid.
Correction: The passage presents both as valid methods, with the disagreement only about which is preferable. Both are necessary for upholding divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason for the preference of ta'wil over tafwid?
A) Ta'wil is the only method accepted by the Salaf
B) Ta'wil provides more clarification and helps refute opponents
C) Tafwid is forbidden because it neglects understanding
D) Both methods are equally preferred with no distinction

Key Takeaway
Both ta'wil and tafwid are permissible methods to uphold divine transcendence when interpreting ambiguous texts, with preference depending on whether one prioritizes clarity (Khalaf) or safety (Salaf).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse in the Quran that seems to describe Allah in human-like terms, does your heart tremble with the fear of falling into misbelief? Which of the two paths—seeking a detailed interpretation or entrusting the meaning to Allah—truly brings you peace and certainty?
?
Are you more concerned with having a clear intellectual explanation for every divine attribute, or with safeguarding your heart from assigning any meaning that Allah did not intend? Where does your current practice lean, and are you willing to examine whether it is driven by humility or by pride in your own understanding?
?
The Salaf preferred tafwid because of its safety, fearing they might assign a meaning not intended by Allah. When you read ambiguous texts, do you feel that same profound awe and caution, or do you rush to interpret them without pausing to consider that you might be overstepping? How can you cultivate that reverent hesitation in your daily relationship with Allah's revelation?
