The Danger of Philosophy and the Purpose of Knowledge
Dardeer warns against philosophy and clarifies the goal of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Dardeer warns against studying philosophy and how he defines the true purpose of knowledge as worship of Allah.

Core Idea
Philosophy leads to doubt and ruin; knowledge profits only when paired with action for Allah's sake.

Imam Dardeer begins with a stark warning: occupying oneself with the sciences of the philosophers rarely leaves one's creed untouched. At best, it breeds skepticism; at worst, it leads to disbelief. He then redirects the reader to the real purpose of knowledge: to worship Allah and gain salvation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's tone come through.
Notice the strong warning language.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has a clear warning against philosophy followed by the positive purpose of knowledge. Find the transition point.
3
Reflect on knowledge and action
Dardeer says knowledge does not save unless acted upon. Ask yourself why he connects this to the validity of worship.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology.
4
Consider the implications
Think about how this warning applies to a student of theology. What kind of knowledge should one prioritize?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: الإلهيات
واعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَنِ اشْتَغَلَ بِعِلْمِ الفلاسفة قل أن تنجو عقيدَتُه مِنْ ظُلْمَةٍ، أقلها كثرة التشكيك والوسوسة التي تجره إلى الابتداع أو إلى الكُفْرِ – والعياذ بالله تعالى- فالحذَرَ من الاشتغال بخُرافاتهم.
على أنَّ المطلوبَ مِنَ العَبدِ إنما هو عِبادة الله، اعتقادًا وعملا، لينجو مِنَ النارِ في الآخرة.
والعِلْمُ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ عِلْمٌ لا يَنَجِّي مِنْ عَذَابِ اللهِ مَا لَمْ يُعْمَلْ به، والعِبادةُ المطلوبة شَرْطُ صحتها العِلْمُ فينبغي للعاقل أنْ يَقْتَصِرَ مِنَ العِلم على ما به العمل، وهو العِلْمُ الشرعيٌّ.
وهو ثلاثة أنواع: عِلْمُ أصول الدين، وعِلْمُ الفِقْهِ، وعِلْمُ التفسير، وما يَتَّصِلُ بذلك مِنْ آلاتها، كعلم النحو والمعاني والبيان، بخلافِ عُلوم الفلاسفة فإنها بطّالةٌ إنْ سَلِمَ صاحبُها مِنَ الضلالِ، وإلا فهي عينُ الوَبالِ.
نعم عِلمُ الطَّب، وما يَوَصِّلُ إلى معْرِفِةِ الوقت والجهاتِ مِنْ عِلْمِ النُّجوم فذلك جائز، على أنا لا نُسَلِّمُ أنَّ هذا مِنْ عِلْم الفلاسفة، بل هو مِنَ الشَّرعيّ، بدليل ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي جَعَلَ لَكُمُ النُّجُومَ لِتَهْتَدُوا بِهَا فِي ظُلُمَاتِ الْبَرِّ وَالْبَحْرِ﴾، والإذن بالطَّب مشهور فى السُّنّة.
The First Section: Divine Matters. And know that whoever occupies himself with the science of the philosophers, rarely will his creed escape from darkness, the least of which is excessive skepticism and whispering that drag him to innovation or to disbelief – and may Allah protect us – so beware of occupying yourself with their nonsense. However, what is required of the servant is only the worship of Allah, in belief and action, to be saved from the Fire in the Hereafter. And knowledge, inasmuch as it is knowledge, does not save from the punishment of Allah unless it is acted upon, and the required worship has as a condition of its validity knowledge, so it is incumbent upon the intelligent person to restrict himself to that knowledge by which action is done, and that is the religious knowledge. And it is of three types: the science of the foundations of religion (usul al-din), the science of jurisprudence (fiqh), and the science of exegesis (tafsir), and what is connected to that of their tools, like the science of grammar (nahw), rhetoric (ma'ani), and eloquence (bayan). Contrary to the sciences of the philosophers, for they are idle, if their possessor is safe from misguidance, otherwise they are the essence of burden. Yes, the science of medicine, and what leads to knowledge of time and directions from the science of astronomy – that is permissible. However, we do not concede that this is from the science of the philosophers; rather it is from the religious, by the evidence: 'And it is He who made for you the stars that you may be guided by them through the darknesses of the land and sea.' And the permission for medicine is well-known in the Sunnah.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a classification and evaluation of different sciences. The author begins with a warning: whoever occupies himself with the science of the philosophers will rarely keep his creed safe from darkness, and at the very least it leads to excessive skepticism and whispering that drag him to innovation or disbelief. Therefore, one must beware of occupying oneself with their nonsense. Then the author explains the true purpose of the servant: only the worship of Allah, in belief and action, to be saved from the Fire. Knowledge itself does not save unless it is acted upon. Worship requires knowledge as a condition of its validity, so the intelligent person should restrict himself to that knowledge by which action is done, which is religious knowledge (العلم الشرعي). Religious knowledge is of three types: (1) the science of the foundations of religion (علم أصول الدين), i.e., theology; (2) the science of jurisprudence (علم الفقه), i.e., Islamic law; and (3) the science of exegesis (علم التفسير), i.e., Quranic interpretation. Also included are the tools that serve these, such as grammar (النحو), rhetoric (المعاني), and eloquence (البيان). In contrast, the sciences of the philosophers are idle (بطالة): if a person is safe from misguidance, they are a waste of time; otherwise, they lead to ruin. Then the author makes an exception: the science of medicine and the science of astronomy that helps determine time and directions are permissible. However, he argues that these are not really from the science of the philosophers; rather they are religious sciences because they are supported by Quranic evidence (the stars for guidance) and the Sunnah (permission for medicine). Thus, the author outlines a hierarchy of knowledge: religious sciences are obligatory or commendable, their tools are necessary for understanding them, while philosophy is generally harmful and forbidden, except for practical sciences like medicine and astronomy that have religious utility.

Key Terms
علم الفلاسفة
ظُلْمَة
تشكيك
وسوسة
ابتداع
كفر
عبادة
عمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Studying philosophy is harmless and does not affect one's faith.
Correction: Dardeer states that philosophy rarely leaves one's creed untouched; it brings darkness, skepticism, and can lead to innovation or disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the least harmful effect of studying philosophy?
A) Immediate disbelief
B) Excessive skepticism and whispering
C) Loss of knowledge
D) Physical illness

Key Takeaway
Knowledge without action does not save; the only knowledge that benefits is that which leads to worship of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever sought Islamic knowledge for intellectual pride or to win debates, rather than to change how you live and worship? If so, what will you do today to repent and reorient your intention?
?
When you learn a new command from Allah, do you immediately ask yourself how to implement it, or do you treat it as head knowledge? What is one piece of knowledge you have learned recently that you have not acted upon, and what is preventing you?
?
Are there any topics—philosophical or otherwise—that you indulge in out of curiosity, even though they stir doubts in your heart? Are you willing to abandon them completely to protect your faith, as Dardeer warns?

Classification of Sciences: Beneficial, Harmful, and Permissible
Dardeer classifies sciences into beneficial religious sciences, harmful philosophical sciences, and permissible practical sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of religious sciences, the role of auxiliary tools, and why medicine and astronomy are exceptions to the condemnation of philosophy.

Core Idea
True knowledge is that by which Allah is worshipped; only religious sciences and their tools, plus medicine and astronomy, are beneficial.

Having established the purpose of knowledge, Dardeer now classifies sciences. He distinguishes the religious sciences (usul, fiqh, tafsir) and their auxiliary tools from the idle sciences of philosophers. He then makes exceptions for medicine and astronomy, showing they are actually rooted in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: classification, condemnation, exception.
2
Identify the three religious sciences
Find 'ثلاثة أنواع' and list them. Also note the auxiliary tools mentioned.
These are the core Islamic sciences.
3
Understand the condemnation of philosophy
Dardeer says philosophical sciences are either idle (بطالة) if safe, or burdensome (وبال) if leading to misguidance.
4
Examine the exceptions
Medicine and astronomy (for time and direction) are permissible. Note the scriptural proofs: Quran for stars, Sunnah for medicine.
Dardeer argues these are actually religious sciences.

?

?

?
You have access to the same Qur'an and Sunnah that justify medicine and astronomy as religious knowledge. How can you transform your study of the natural world—whether biology, physics, or any field—into an act of worship and ihsan, rather than a mere secular pursuit?

Defining Tasawwuf: The Science of Spiritual Purification
Dardeer defines tasawwuf and states its subject and purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Dardeer defines tasawwuf as a science, its subject matter (the heart and senses), and its goal (spiritual purification built on correct creed).

Core Idea
Tasawwuf is the science of purifying the heart and senses, rooted in sound ʿaqīdah.

In this concluding section, Imam Dardeer turns from theology to the inner dimension of Islam. He introduces tasawwuf not as a separate discipline but as a science that perfects the soul, built upon the beliefs just established. The passage provides both a poetic motivation and a formal definition, clarifying its subject and purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line and its commentary
Start with the verse 'fa-akthiran min dhikrihā bi-l-adab' and Dardeer's explanation. Notice the emphasis on dhikr (remembrance) and adab (etiquette) as foundational.
The light nūn of emphasis underscores the command.
2
Identify the formal definition of tasawwuf
Find the sentence where tasawwuf is defined as a science. Note the phrase 'ʿilm bi-uṣūl yuʿrafu bihi ṣalāḥ al-qalb wa-sāʾir al-ḥawās'.
This definition highlights both heart and senses.
3
Connect subject, goal, and placement
Understand that the subject is the heart and senses, the goal is purification (the 'life of hearts'), and it is placed after creed because correct belief is necessary for the spiritual journey.

Source Text

إذا علمت ذلك (فأكثرَن (۱)) - بنونِ التَّوكيد الخفيفة (مِنْ ذكرها) أي: كلمة الإسلام، (بالأدب) أي: معَ الآداب التي ذكَرَها القومُ).

رتبه على معرفة عقائدِ الإيمان، لأنه لا يُمكِنُ السَّيرُ إلى الله تعالى إلا بعد معْرِفتِها (). وحد التصوف علما هو علم بأصول يُعرَفُ به صلاح القلب وسائر الحواس.
Conclusion: On Sufism (al-taṣawwuf)
-59: So multiply its remembrance with etiquette, you will rise by this remembrance to the highest ranks.
If you know that, then 'multiply' (fa-akthiran) with the light nūn of emphasis, (from its remembrance) i.e. the word of Islam, (with etiquette) i.e. with the etiquettes that the people (of Sufism) have mentioned.
Definition of Sufism, its goal, and its subject matter.
And this is a beginning from him—may Allah forgive him—in the art of Sufism, which is the life of hearts, and its subject matter is [?] he placed it after knowledge of the beliefs of faith, because journeying to Allah Most High is not possible except after knowing them. And the definition of Sufism as a science is: a science of principles by which the soundness of the heart and the rest of the senses is known.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage introduces the concluding section on Tasawwuf (Sufism). It begins with a line of poetry from the original poem: 'So multiply its remembrance with etiquette, you will rise by this remembrance to the highest ranks.' Then Dardeer provides a grammatical and terminological commentary: 'if you know that, then multiply (fa-akthiran) with the light nūn of emphasis, (from its remembrance) i.e. the word of Islam, (with etiquette) i.e. with the etiquettes that the people (of Sufism) have mentioned.' The phrase 'definition of Sufism, its goal, and its subject matter' serves as a heading. Dardeer then states that this is a beginning from him (the author) in the art of Sufism, which is the life of hearts. He notes that its subject matter is placed after knowledge of the beliefs of faith, because journeying to Allah is impossible without that knowledge. Finally, he gives a formal definition: 'Sufism as a science is a science of principles by which the soundness of the heart and the rest of the senses is known.' This defines tasawwuf as a practical science aimed at spiritual purification.

Key Terms
تصوف (taṣawwuf)
ذكر (dhikr)
أدب (adab)
قلب (qalb)
حواس (ḥawās)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tasawwuf is a separate, esoteric practice independent of Islamic theology.
Correction: Dardeer explicitly states that tasawwuf is placed after creed and that journeying to Allah is impossible without correct beliefs.

Checkpoint

A) A science of principles by which the heart and senses are purified
B) The life of hearts
C) The journey to Allah
D) Knowledge of the beliefs of faith

Key Takeaway
Tasawwuf is a science of principles for purifying the heart and senses, and it must be built upon sound ʿaqīdah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The text calls tasawwuf 'the life of hearts.' Honestly, is your heart truly alive? What would change if you approached this science with the same discipline as your other Islamic studies?

The Seven Pre-Remembrance Etiquettes
Lists the etiquettes to observe before beginning dhikr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven etiquettes to observe before beginning dhikr, as taught by Imam Dardir.

Core Idea
Proper dhikr begins with preparation — repentance, purity, and presence.

Imam Dardir outlines the essential etiquettes that should be observed before engaging in the remembrance of Allah. These practices, both inward and outward, prepare the heart and body for a focused and spiritually fruitful session of dhikr. The following passage enumerates seven key preparations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: each etiquette is introduced with 'أن' (that) after the general term 'القبلية'.
The passage begins with 'فالقبلية' which means 'as for the pre-remembrance etiquettes'.
2
Identify each of the seven etiquettes
List them in order as they appear. There are seven distinct actions mentioned.
The seventh one might be easy to miss: bringing to mind the shaykh.
3
Understand the purpose behind each
For each etiquette, ask: why is this important for dhikr? The commentary will help clarify the spiritual benefits.
For example, purification is for external cleanliness, while repentance cleanses the heart.
4
Reflect on personal application
Think about how you can incorporate these etiquettes into your own practice of dhikr. Which ones are already part of your routine? Which need more attention?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقبلية: أن يُجدِّدَ التَّوبةَ لِما وقع فيه مِنَ المخالفات، أو الخواطر الرديئة، وأنْ يتطَهَّرَ مِنَ الحَدَثِ والخبث، وأنْ يتوجه إلى الله تعالى برغبة ليحصل له الجمعية في الذكر، وأن يستغفِرَ الله تعالى بما تيسر، بأي صيغةٍ كانتْ، وأَنْ يُصلّي على النبي في السيرِ، ثُمَّ يَشْرَعَ فِي الذِّكْرِ. كذلك، وأن يستقبل القبلة؛ لأنها أفضل الجهات، وأن يستحضر شَيخه ليكون رفيقه.
As for the pre-remembrance etiquettes: that he renew repentance for what occurred from him of violations or evil thoughts, and that he purify himself from ritual impurity and filth, and that he turn to Allah Almighty with desire to obtain presence in the remembrance, and that he seek forgiveness from Allah Almighty with what is easy, in whatever formula, and that he send blessings upon the Prophet in the journey, then he begins the remembrance. Also, that he face the qibla, because it is the best of directions, and that he bring to mind his shaykh to be his companion.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage enumerates the etiquettes that should be observed before engaging in dhikr (remembrance of Allah). The author lists seven specific practices: 1) renewing repentance for past sins and bad thoughts; 2) performing ritual purification (wudu or ghusl) and removing physical impurities; 3) turning to Allah with sincere desire to attain spiritual presence (jamʿiyya) during dhikr; 4) seeking forgiveness in any easy manner; 5) sending blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad; 6) facing the qibla (direction of prayer) as the best direction; and 7) bringing to mind one's spiritual guide (shaykh) to be a companion. These preparations aim to create the right inner and outer state for effective remembrance.

شيخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dhikr is only about repeating words; no special preparation is needed.
Correction: This passage shows that dhikr requires prior spiritual and physical preparation, including repentance, purification, and focused intention.

Checkpoint

A) Renewing repentance
B) Purifying from ritual impurity
C) Facing the qibla
D) Raising the hands in supplication

Key Takeaway
The seven pre-remembrance etiquettes ensure that dhikr is performed with spiritual and physical readiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sit to remember Allah, do you take any time to prepare? How often do you rush into dhikr without repentance or purification? Commit today to practicing at least one of these etiquettes before your next dhikr session.
?
The passage says to bring to mind your shaykh to be your companion. Who is the person of knowledge you rely on in your spiritual journey? If you don't have one, what steps can you take to find a guide, so that your dhikr is not done in isolation?
?
Turning with desire (رغبة) to Allah is essential. Ask yourself: Do you truly desire Allah's presence when you remember Him, or is your dhikr a habit? If it is the latter, what can you do this week to rekindle that desire?

Etiquettes of Dhikr
Details the etiquettes to observe while performing dhikr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific etiquettes to observe during the dhikr of 'La ilaha illa Allah', including pronunciation, posture, intention, and head movement.

Core Idea
Dhikr is not just words; it is a disciplined act of worship with precise etiquettes.

The author now turns to the practical aspects of dhikr. While the previous lessons established the virtue and meaning of dhikr, this passage details the outward and inward etiquettes that transform mere repetition into a focused spiritual practice. These guidelines ensure the dhikr is performed with presence of heart and in accordance with tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of etiquettes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a list of instructions, each introduced by 'أن' (that he...).
The passage covers pronunciation, intention, posture, and movement.
2
Identify the categories
Group the etiquettes into categories: pronunciation rules, inner states (earnestness, sincerity, negation of creations), physical posture (sitting, eyes, head movement), and conclusion.
This helps organize the many details.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why each etiquette is prescribed. For example, why close the eyes? The passage says it illuminates the heart. Why negate creations? Because they cut off from Allah.
The etiquettes are means to deepen focus and sincerity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآداب المصاحبة له: فأنْ يستَحضِرَ معناها إجمالًا، وأن يُحقِّقَ الهمزة، ويمُدَّ ألفَ (لا) مدًا مُتَوَسَطًا، ويفتح ها «إله» فتحةً خفيفةً، ويمُدَّ أَلفَ «الله» وألفَ «إله) مدًا طبيعيا، ويأتي بالهاءِ مِنَ «الله»، ويقف عليها، وأنْ يَذكُرَ بِهِمَةٍ وقُوَّةٍ، وأنْ يكونَ ذكره رغبةً في مرضاة الله ومحبته وامتثالا لأمره، لا لرياء ولا لسُمْعَةٍ، ولا لأمْرِ دُنيَوي أو أُخروي، وأن ينفي الأكوانَ مِنْ قَلبه؛ لأنَّ مُلاحَظةَ شَيْءٍ مِنها قاطع عن اللَّهِ، ولولا أنَّ للشَّيخ مَدخَلًا في السَّيرِ ما سَوَّغوا له مُلاحظته في حالِ البِدايةِ، وأَنْ يَجْلِسَ كجُلوسه في التشهد إلا لتعب فيجوزُ التربُّعُ ، وأَنْ يُغْمِضَ عَينَيهِ؛ لأنَّ له تأثيرًا في تنوير القلب، وأن يبتدئ بـ «لا» جهة اليمين، ويرجع بـ «إله»، ويختم بـ «الله» جهةً اليسارِ مُشيرًا إلى قلبه، فإذا أرادَ َختَمَ الذَّكْر ختمه بـ «المُحمَّدٌ رسولُ اللهِ
And as for the accompanying etiquettes: that he bring to mind its meaning in summary, and that he articulate the hamza precisely, and extend the alif of 'la' with a moderate lengthening, and open the ha of 'ilaha' with a light fatha, and extend the alif of 'Allah' and the alif of 'ilaha' with a natural lengthening, and pronounce the ha of 'Allah' and stop on it, and that he remember with earnestness and strength, and that his remembrance be out of desire for Allah's pleasure and love and obedience to His command, not out of hypocrisy or reputation-seeking, nor for any worldly or otherworldly matter, and that he negate the existences from his heart, because observing anything from them cuts off from Allah, and were it not that the shaykh has an entry in the journey, they would not have permitted his observation in the beginning stage, and that he sit as he sits in the tashahhud, except due to fatigue in which case sitting cross-legged is permissible, and that he close his eyes, because it has an effect in illuminating the heart, and that he begin with 'la' from the right side, and return with 'ilaha', and conclude with 'Allah' from the left side, pointing to his heart. And if he wishes to conclude the remembrance, he concludes it with 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage lists the etiquettes to observe during dhikr. The author begins by stating that the practitioner should have a general understanding of the meaning of the words. Then he gives detailed pronunciation rules for the phrase 'Lā ilāha illa Allāh': articulate the hamza of 'ilāha', extend the 'lā' moderately, pronounce the 'hā' of 'ilāha' with a light fatha, extend the alifs naturally, and stop on the 'hā' of 'Allāh'. The dhikr should be done with earnestness and strength. The intention must be purely for Allah's pleasure and love, not for show or worldly gain. The practitioner should negate all created things (akwān) from his heart, as noticing them cuts one off from Allah. The shaykh's role is permitted only initially. The sitting posture is like in tashahhud, but cross-legged if tired. Eyes should be closed to help illuminate the heart. The movement of the head is specified: begin 'lā' from the right, move to the left on 'ilāha', and end 'Allāh' on the left, pointing to the heart. If concluding, add 'Muḥammadun Rasūlu Allāh'.

Post-Remembrance Etiquettes and Spiritual States
Describes etiquettes after dhikr and the arriving spiritual states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of post-dhikr stillness and how different spiritual states (asceticism, reliance, patience) manifest after remembrance.

Core Idea
The real work of dhikr happens in the silence after it.

The passage from Imam Dardir explains the etiquette after completing dhikr. It emphasizes that the spiritual benefits of remembrance are not fully realized without a period of silent stillness. During this time, various spiritual states (called wāridāt) arrive and settle in the heart, transforming the practitioner's relationship with the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source and its translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the structure: first a general principle, then three examples, then additional etiquettes from Imam al-Ghazali.
2
Identify the three types of arrivals
The author lists three specific arrivals: asceticism (zuhd), reliance (tawakkul), and patience (ṣabr). For each, note the effect they have on the heart after settling.
The effects are: indifference to the world, total delegation to Allah, and unshaken calm in calamity.
3
Understand the role of stillness
Reflect on why silence and humility (khushūʿ) are necessary. The passage says the arrival cannot settle without them. Why might stillness be essential for internal transformation?
Stillness allows the heart to absorb the state without distraction.
4
Examine Ghazali's additional etiquettes
Imam al-Ghazali adds four etiquettes for the silence period: vigilance of Allah, maintaining the meaning of dhikr in the heart, negating all thoughts, and total focus. Consider how these deepen the stillness.
5
Connect to practice
Think about how you can apply this teaching to your own dhikr practice. After your next session, try sitting in silence for a few minutes with these etiquettes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآدابُ البعدية: فإنه يسكُتُ ويسكُنُ بخُشوع، فإنَّ للذكر واردات ترد على قلب الذاكر، ولا يتمكَّنُ الوارد مِنَ القلب إلا بذلك، فإذا كانَ الوارد وارد زُهْدِ وجَبَ التمهل حتى يتم ويتمكَّنَ مِنَ القلب، فتستوي عِندَه الدُّنيا، أقبلتْ أمْ أدبرت، وإذا كان وارد توَكَّل صارَ بعد ذلكَ مُفوِّضًا أمره إلى ربِّه في كُلِّ شَيْءٍ، وإذا كان وارد صبر صار بعد ذلك لا ينزَعِجُ مِنْ تفاقم الأهوالِ، وهكذا مِنَ الوارداتِ. قال الإمام الغزالي رَضَ الله عنه : ولهذه السكتة آداب: مراقبة الله تعالى، وإجراء معنى الذكر على قلبه، ونفي الخواطر كُلها، وجمع حواله كُلها بحيث لا تتحرك منه شعرة
And as for the post-remembrance etiquettes: he remains silent and still with humility, because remembrance has arrivals that come to the heart of the rememberer, and the arrival cannot settle in the heart except by that. So if the arrival is an arrival of asceticism, he must wait until it completes and settles in the heart, so the world becomes equal to him, whether it comes or goes. And if it is an arrival of reliance, he becomes thereafter one who delegates his affair to his Lord in everything. And if it is an arrival of patience, he thereafter does not become agitated by the escalation of terrors. And so on from the arrivals. Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him, said: And for this silence there are etiquettes: vigilance of Allah Almighty, conducting the meaning of the remembrance on his heart, negating all thoughts, and gathering all his faculties such that not a hair of him moves.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage explains the etiquettes after completing dhikr. The practitioner should remain silent and still with humility, because the dhikr brings spiritual states (wāridāt) to the heart. These states need silence and stillness to settle. The author gives examples of three types of arrivals: if it is an arrival of asceticism (zuhd), one should wait until it takes root, after which the world becomes equal in his eyes regardless of circumstances. If it is an arrival of reliance (tawakkul), he becomes one who delegates all his affairs to Allah. If it is an arrival of patience (ṣabr), he becomes unshaken by severe trials. The passage then quotes Imam al-Ghazali, who says that this silence has its own etiquettes: being watchful of Allah, keeping the meaning of the dhikr present in the heart, negating all distracting thoughts, and gathering all one's focus so completely that not even a hair moves.

The Method of Mujahada and Definitions of Suluk and Sayr
Describes the method to attain maskanah and defines suluk and sayr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical method of mujahada to attain spiritual station (maskanah) and the distinction between suluk and sayr in the spiritual path.

Core Idea
Mujahada is the path; suluk is the journey; sayr is the initial turning of the heart.

This passage from Imam Dardee's sharh outlines the essential practices that lead to spiritual station (maskanah). It defines the concepts of mujahada (striving), suluk (journeying), and sayr (traveling), clarifying their interrelation. Let's explore the method and these key terms step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the author's flow and technical terms.
Notice the Quranic citation and the colon at the end—this passage continues.
2
Identify the practices of mujahada
The passage lists several practices that together constitute mujahada: constant dhikr, attachment of heart to Allah, hunger, vigil, seclusion, silence except for dhikr, and other pillars. Make a mental list.
These are the means to attain maskanah.
3
Understand the distinction between suluk and sayr
The author defines sayr as the turning of the heart to Allah while opposing the soul's desires, even lawful ones, for Allah's pleasure. He then says sayr is the cause of suluk, and sometimes suluk is used for both. Grasp this causal relationship.
Suluk is the actual journey; sayr is the initial movement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسهل الوصول إليها عادةً إلا بمُداومة ذكرِ «لا إله إلا الله» ليلا ونهارًا، معَ تعلق القلب بالله وحده، والجوع والسهر والاعتزال عن الناس، والصمت إلا عن ذكر الله تعالى، ومُلاحظة بقيّة أركان الطريق التي سيأتي بيانها إن شاء الله تعالى)، وهو المسمّى بـ «المجاهدة»، قال تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ جَاهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا) (٢)، وهذا الترقي هو المُسَمّى بـ السُّلوكِ إلى مَلِكِ المُلوكِ عِندَ الطائفة. وأما السَّيرُ إلى الله تعالى فهو تَوجُهُ القَلْبِ إلى الربِّ مَعَ مُخالفة النفْسِ في شَهَواتِها - ولو مُباحةً - طلبًا لمرضاة الله تعالى، وإيثارا له على ما سواه، فالسَّيرُ كالسبب في السلوكِ، وقد يُطلَقُ السُّلوك على المعنى الثاني أيضًا. والسُّلوك إلى الله تعالى طريقةً النبيين والصدّيقين والعُلَماء العاملين إلا أنه مُختلفٌ :
And it is not easy to reach it (i.e., maskanah) usually except by constant remembrance of 'La ilaha illa Allah' night and day, with attachment of the heart to God alone, and hunger, vigil, seclusion from people, silence except for remembrance of God Most High, and observing the remaining pillars of the path that will be explained later, God willing. And this is called 'mujahada' (striving). The Most High said: 'And those who strive in Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways' [Al-Ankabut 29:69]. And this ascension is called 'suluk' (journeying) to the King of kings, according to the sect. As for the 'sayr' (traveling) to God Most High, it is the turning of the heart to the Lord while opposing the soul in its desires—even if permissible—seeking the pleasure of God Most High, and preferring Him over all else. So sayr is like the cause of suluk, and suluk is sometimes also applied to the second meaning. And suluk to God Most High is the way of the prophets, the truthful ones, and the acting scholars, but it varies.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage explains the method to attain maskanah: continuous dhikr, attachment to God, hunger, vigil, seclusion, silence, and observing the other pillars. This is called mujahada. The author cites the Quranic verse. The resulting ascension is called suluk. Then he defines sayr as the turning of the heart to God while opposing the soul's desires, even lawful ones, for God's pleasure. Sayr is the cause or prerequisite for suluk. Finally, he states that suluk is the way of the prophets and scholars, but it differs (in degrees or methods). The colon at the end anticipates a detailed explanation that follows.

Key Terms
مجاهدة

سير
ذكر
صمت
عزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think suluk and sayr are the same thing or unrelated.
Correction: The passage clarifies that sayr is the cause of suluk and is a distinct concept. Sayr is the initial turning and opposition to the soul, while suluk is the ongoing journey.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of sayr (traveling) in relation to suluk (journeying)?
A) Sayr is the opposite of suluk
B) Sayr is the cause of suluk
C) Sayr and suluk are identical
D) Sayr is the result of suluk

Key Takeaway
Mujahada through specific practices leads to suluk, with sayr being the initial turning of the heart that causes the journey.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the path to Allah requires hunger, vigil, seclusion, and silence. Which of these is most difficult for you today? Will you commit to practicing one of them this week, even if only for a short time?
?
The author says sayr is turning the heart while opposing desires, even permissible ones. What is one permissible pleasure you cling to that comes between you and Allah? Can you give it up for one day, purely for His sake?
?
The passage says suluk is the way of the prophets. You claim to follow them. But where is your suluk? List the practices from the passage. Which one have you been neglecting, and what concrete step will you take tonight to begin?

The Spiritual Paths of Prophets and Saints
Introduction to the spiritual paths and the principle of beginnings and ends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different spiritual paths of prophets and saints and the principle that the end reflects the beginning.

Core Idea
The spiritual journey begins where it ends—the end is the beginning illuminated.

This lesson explores the concluding section on Sufism, where the author contrasts the path of prophets—starting from perfection—with the path of seekers—starting from darkness. It also presents the key principle that the start of a journey determines the brilliance of its destination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on prophetic paths
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Note how the prophets' path is described as ascending from purified souls to infinite stations.
Notice the hierarchy among prophets.
2
Contrast with the path of others
Observe the difference: non-prophets start from dark souls (ammara or lawwama) and progress to a complete truthful soul (siddiqiyya).
3
Reflect on the principle of beginnings
Understand the statement that 'by burning the beginning comes the illumination of the end' in the context of spiritual struggle. Ask yourself how this applies to your own journey.
This principle links beginnings directly to outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة: في التصوف. فسلوك الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام مبدؤه الترقي من نفوس مطهرة كمالية إلى ما لا نهاية له من المقامات الإحسانية، وهو في نفسه متفاوت، فسلوك أولي العزم منه أعلى وأجل من سلوك غيرهم، وسلوك سيد أولي العزم عليه وعليهم أفضل الصلاة والسلام أعلى من غيره، إذ مبدؤه نهاية غيره. وأما سلوك غيرهم فمن نفوس أمارة أو لوامة ظلمانية، إلى نفس كاملة صديقية. والنهايات تختلف في الإشراق حسب اختلاف البدايات، فبإحراق البداية يكون إشراق النهاية. والنفوس سبعة بحسب أوصافها، وإلا فهي واحدة:
Conclusion: On Sufism. So the path of the prophets, peace be upon them, its beginning is the ascent from purified perfect souls to infinite levels of excellence, and it is in itself varied. So the path of the determined messengers among them is higher and greater than the path of others, and the path of the master of the determined messengers, peace and blessings be upon him and them, is higher than others, for its beginning is the end of others. As for the path of others, it is from commanding or blameworthy dark souls to a complete truthful soul. The ends differ in illumination according to the difference of beginnings; by burning the beginning comes the illumination of the end. The souls are seven according to their descriptions, otherwise they are one:
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage introduces the conclusion on Sufism. The author first describes the spiritual journeys of prophets, noting that their path begins from purified and perfected souls and ascends to infinite divine stations. The path is not uniform; the messengers of strong resolve (ulul-azm) have a higher path than others, and the Prophet Muhammad's path is the highest, as his starting point is the endpoint of others. For non-prophets, the path begins from dark, commanding (al-ammara) or blameworthy (al-lawwama) souls and progresses to a complete truthful (siddiqiyya) soul. He then states the general principle that the illumination of the end depends on the burning of the beginning through struggle. Finally, he declares that souls are seven by their descriptions, but in reality they are one. This sets the stage for listing the seven souls.

لوامة
صديقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All spiritual paths are the same.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes prophetic paths (beginning from perfection) from non-prophetic paths (beginning from imperfection). They differ in starting point and ultimate illumination.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the starting point of the path of non-prophets?
A) Purified souls
B) Commanding or blameworthy dark souls
C) Siddiqiyya souls
D) Infinite stations

Key Takeaway
The spiritual journey's beginning determines its luminous outcome—prophets start from perfection, others from struggle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the principle: 'by burning the beginning comes the illumination of the end.' What is the 'beginning' of your own soul right now? Are you willing to burn your attachments, habits, or comforts to achieve a luminous end? What would that burning actually look like in your daily life starting tomorrow?
?
The Prophet's beginning was the end of others. Where do you stand—are you content to remain in the lower path of the commanding soul, or do you yearn for the truthful soul? What is the one thing you are holding onto that prevents you from ascending even one step?
?
Your end depends on your beginning. If your current state is a beginning, what kind of end is it leading to? Imagine standing before Allah at the end—will your spiritual struggle today be worthy of the illumination you seek? If not, what will you change today?

The Seven Souls: One Essence, Many Descriptions
Concluding statement on the seven souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sufi concept of the seven souls as states of a single soul.

Core Idea
The soul is one in essence, seven in description.

This brief but profound statement concludes the section on Sufism, asserting that the multiplicity of souls refers to their qualities, not their underlying reality. It sets the stage for deeper exploration of the seven stages of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Note the exact phrasing: the souls are seven by description, otherwise they are one.
The colon at the end indicates a list is to follow in the next passage.
2
Reflect on the meaning of 'description'
Understand that the soul takes on different characteristics or states (e.g., commanding, blaming, serene), but it remains one and the same entity.
3
Connect to the previous discussion
Recall that the path of non-prophets involves transformation from ammara/lawwama to siddiqiyya. These are specific descriptions among the seven.
This shows how the seven states correspond to spiritual stages.

The Balance of Fear and Hope in Health and Illness
Dardeer explains the situational dominance of fear and hope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spiritual aspirants should prioritize fear over hope in health and hope over fear in illness, according to Imam Dardeer's commentary.

Core Idea
Fear and hope are like two wings — you need both, but the active wing changes with your state.

This lesson begins with a poetic instruction to make fear dominant over hope on the journey to Allah. Dardeer explains that while both fear and hope are essential, their emphasis shifts depending on one's physical and spiritual condition. He presents the metaphor of the bird's wings and contrasts the Maliki and Shafi'i positions on this balance.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line
Start with the verse: 'Make fear dominant over hope, and travel to your Lord without distance.' This sets the theme.
Notice the command: the path requires both direction (travel) and intensity (fear).
2
Understand the bird wings analogy
Dardeer explains that fear and hope are like two wings of a bird. Losing one leads to a fall. This shows both are necessary, but their proportions vary.
The analogy emphasizes mutual dependence, not equality.
3
Learn the Maliki position
In health, fear should dominate to drive worship and purify the heart. In sickness nearing death, hope should dominate as one approaches the Generous Lord.
This is the recommended practice according to Imam Malik.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that fear and hope should be balanced equally in both health and sickness, like two equal wings.
This difference shows variance within Sunni scholarship on the same principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغلّب الخَوفَ على الرجاءِ * وسَرْ مَولاكَ بِلا تَناءِ (وغلّب) في حال اشتغالِكَ بالذَّكر المذكور (الخَوفَ) مِنَ اللهِ تعالى ما دُمتَ في حال الصحة (على الرجاء) في رحمته وعفوه). يُريد أنه لا بُدَّ للعَبدِ مِنَ الخَوفِ والرجاء معا؛ لأنهما كجَناحَي الطائر، متى فَقَدَ أحدهما سقَطَ، إلا أنه في حال الصحة والسلامة ينبغي تغليب جانب الخوف على جانب الرجاء؛ لأنه كالسوط ينساقُ به إلى الاعتناء بالعبادة، وبه تزولُ الرعوناتُ النفسية عن القلب إنْ شاءَ اللهُ تعالى. فإذا نزل به المَرَضُ وأَشْرَفَ على الموتِ فينبغي تغليب جانبِ الرجاء على الخَوفِ؛ لأنه حالُ القُدومِ على الكريم. (۱) هذا هو مذهب مالك، وعند الشافعي يجعلها كجناحي الطائر مستويين صحة ومرضا. [حاشية قال الغزالي في الإحياء في بيان حقيقة الخوف والرجاء بيانه أن كل ما يلاقيك من مكروه ومحبوب الصاوي، ص ٨١]. ينقسم إلى موجود في الحال وإلى موجود فيما مضى وإلى منتظر في الاستقبال، فإذا خطر ببالك موجود فيما مضى سمي ذكرا وتذكراً، وإن كان ما خطر بقلبك موجوداً في الحال سمي وجداً وذوقاً وإدراكاً، وإن كان قد خطر وجود شيء في المستقبل وغلب على قلبك سمي خوفا وإشفاقا، وإن كان محبوبا حصل من انتظاره تعلق القلب به ويسمى رجاء، فالرجاء هو ارتياح القلب لانتظار ما هو محبوب عنده. الخوف والرجاء الطريق إلى الله وأصولها شرح الخريدة البهية والخَوفُ: هَم وقلقٌ لِما هو آتِ والحُزنُ: هَمَّ لِما فات والرجاء: تعلُّق القلب بمَرْغوبِ يحصل في المستقبل مع الأخذ في الأسباب، فإِن لَّمْ يَأْخُذْ فَطَمَع، وهو مذموم شرعا.
And make fear dominant over hope, and travel to your Lord without distance. (And make dominant) during your occupation with the aforementioned remembrance (fear) of Allah Almighty as long as you are in a state of health (over hope) in His mercy and pardon. He intends that the servant must have fear and hope together; because they are like the two wings of a bird, when one of them is lost it falls. However, in the state of health and safety, the side of fear should be made dominant over the side of hope, because it is like a whip by which one is driven to attend to worship, and by it the psychological vanities are removed from the heart, if Allah wills. Then when illness befalls him and he approaches death, then the side of hope should be made dominant over fear, because that is the state of arriving before the Generous. (1) This is the school of Malik, and according to al-Shafi'i he makes them like the two wings of the bird, equal in health and sickness. [Footnote: al-Ghazali said in al-Ihya' in explaining the reality of fear and hope, his explanation is that everything that meets you of disliked and beloved things is divided into what is present in the moment, what is in the past, and what is awaited in the future. So if there occurs to your mind something existing in the past, it is called remembrance and recollection; and if what occurs to your heart is existing in the present, it is called finding (wajd), tasting, and perception; and if there occurs the existence of something in the future and it overwhelms your heart, it is called fear and apprehension; and if it is beloved, then from its waiting occurs the heart's attachment to it and it is called hope. So hope is the heart's delight in waiting for what is beloved to it. Fear and hope. The path to Allah and its principles, commentary of al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah. And fear: is anxiety and worry for what is coming. And grief: is worry for what has passed. And hope: is the heart's attachment to a desired thing that will occur in the future while taking the means; if he does not take the means, then it is greed (tama'), and it is blameworthy in the Law.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between fear (khawf) and hope (raja') in the spiritual path. The author begins by commenting on the poetic line: 'Make fear dominant over hope, and travel to your Lord without distance.' He states that while engaging in remembrance (dhikr), one should prioritize fear of Allah in health over hope in His mercy. However, both fear and hope are necessary together, like the two wings of a bird; if one is missing, the bird falls. In health, fear should dominate because it drives one to worship and purifies the heart from vain desires. When one falls ill and nears death, hope should dominate because one is approaching the Generous Lord. The marginal note clarifies that this is the school of Malik, while al-Shafi'i holds that fear and hope should be balanced like equal wings in both health and sickness. The text then provides a broader classification: everything that enters the mind can be divided into past, present, or future. Past occurrences give remembrance (dhikr), present occurrences give spiritual finding (wajd), and future occurrences produce fear (if disliked) or hope (if beloved). Hope is defined as the heart's delight in awaiting something beloved. Finally, the author gives precise definitions: fear is anxiety for what is coming, grief is worry for what has passed, and hope is the heart's attachment to a desired future outcome while taking the means; without taking means, it becomes blameworthy greed (tama').

رجاء

Misconception: Some think the spiritual path requires only fear or only hope at all times.
Correction: The author shows both are necessary, but their emphasis changes with state.

Checkpoint

A) In health
B) In illness nearing death
C) Always
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The wise seeker adjusts the emphasis of fear and hope according to their spiritual and physical state, keeping both wings intact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your current state of health, does the fear of Allah drive you to worship, or has comfort made you complacent? How will you strengthen the wing of fear without losing hope?
?
When you face a trial or illness, do you feel hope in Allah’s mercy or does despair creep in? What specific teaching from this lesson can you hold onto to tip the balance toward hope?
?
You have two wings: fear and hope. Which one has been weaker in your life lately? What practical step will you take today to strengthen it, following the guidance of this lesson?

The Reality of Fear, Hope, and Grief according to al-Ghazali and Dardeer
Al-Ghazali and Dardeer define fear, hope, grief, and greed using a time-based classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of fear, hope, grief, and greed, and how they relate to past, present, and future mental experiences according to al-Ghazali and Dardeer.

Core Idea
Fear is worry for the future, grief is worry for the past, and hope is the heart's delight in a desired future while taking means.

This lesson dives into the technical definitions of spiritual emotions. After laying out the practical balance, the text, via a marginal note from al-Ghazali, provides a systematic classification of all mental experiences into three time zones: past, present, and future. From this comes a clear distinction between fear, grief, hope, and greed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ghazali classification
Examine the division of everything that meets you into present, past, and future. This framework underlies the definitions.
Notice how ‘wajd’ is for present experiences, ‘dhikr’ for past.
2
Define fear and hope
Fear is anxiety for something disliked in the future; hope is the heart's delight in waiting for something beloved in the future, while taking the means.
Hope requires action; without it, it becomes greed (tama’).
3
Distinguish grief from fear
Grief is worry for what has passed, while fear is for what is coming. Both are forms of worry but oriented differently.
This is a subtle but important distinction for spiritual introspection.
4
Understand the blameworthy greed
Hope that does not involve taking means is called greed (tama’), and it is blameworthy in the Sharia.
This teaches that hope must be active, not passive wishful thinking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغلّب الخَوفَ على الرجاءِ * وسَرْ مَولاكَ بِلا تَناءِ (وغلّب) في حال اشتغالِكَ بالذَّكر المذكور (الخَوفَ) مِنَ اللهِ تعالى ما دُمتَ في حال الصحة (على الرجاء) في رحمته وعفوه). يُريد أنه لا بُدَّ للعَبدِ مِنَ الخَوفِ والرجاء معا؛ لأنهما كجَناحَي الطائر، متى فَقَدَ أحدهما سقَطَ، إلا أنه في حال الصحة والسلامة ينبغي تغليب جانب الخوف على جانب الرجاء؛ لأنه كالسوط ينساقُ به إلى الاعتناء بالعبادة، وبه تزولُ الرعوناتُ النفسية عن القلب إنْ شاءَ اللهُ تعالى. فإذا نزل به المَرَضُ وأَشْرَفَ على الموتِ فينبغي تغليب جانبِ الرجاء على الخَوفِ؛ لأنه حالُ القُدومِ على الكريم. (۱) هذا هو مذهب مالك، وعند الشافعي يجعلها كجناحي الطائر مستويين صحة ومرضا. [حاشية قال الغزالي في الإحياء في بيان حقيقة الخوف والرجاء بيانه أن كل ما يلاقيك من مكروه ومحبوب الصاوي، ص ٨١]. ينقسم إلى موجود في الحال وإلى موجود فيما مضى وإلى منتظر في الاستقبال، فإذا خطر ببالك موجود فيما مضى سمي ذكرا وتذكراً، وإن كان ما خطر بقلبك موجوداً في الحال سمي وجداً وذوقاً وإدراكاً، وإن كان قد خطر وجود شيء في المستقبل وغلب على قلبك سمي خوفا وإشفاقا، وإن كان محبوبا حصل من انتظاره تعلق القلب به ويسمى رجاء، فالرجاء هو ارتياح القلب لانتظار ما هو محبوب عنده. الخوف والرجاء الطريق إلى الله وأصولها شرح الخريدة البهية والخَوفُ: هَم وقلقٌ لِما هو آتِ والحُزنُ: هَمَّ لِما فات والرجاء: تعلُّق القلب بمَرْغوبِ يحصل في المستقبل مع الأخذ في الأسباب، فإِن لَّمْ يَأْخُذْ فَطَمَع، وهو مذموم شرعا.
And make fear dominant over hope, and travel to your Lord without distance. (And make dominant) during your occupation with the aforementioned remembrance (fear) of Allah Almighty as long as you are in a state of health (over hope) in His mercy and pardon. He intends that the servant must have fear and hope together; because they are like the two wings of a bird, when one of them is lost it falls. However, in the state of health and safety, the side of fear should be made dominant over the side of hope, because it is like a whip by which one is driven to attend to worship, and by it the psychological vanities are removed from the heart, if Allah wills. Then when illness befalls him and he approaches death, then the side of hope should be made dominant over fear, because that is the state of arriving before the Generous. (1) This is the school of Malik, and according to al-Shafi'i he makes them like the two wings of the bird, equal in health and sickness. [Footnote: al-Ghazali said in al-Ihya' in explaining the reality of fear and hope, his explanation is that everything that meets you of disliked and beloved things is divided into what is present in the moment, what is in the past, and what is awaited in the future. So if there occurs to your mind something existing in the past, it is called remembrance and recollection; and if what occurs to your heart is existing in the present, it is called finding (wajd), tasting, and perception; and if there occurs the existence of something in the future and it overwhelms your heart, it is called fear and apprehension; and if it is beloved, then from its waiting occurs the heart's attachment to it and it is called hope. So hope is the heart's delight in waiting for what is beloved to it. Fear and hope. The path to Allah and its principles, commentary of al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah. And fear: is anxiety and worry for what is coming. And grief: is worry for what has passed. And hope: is the heart's attachment to a desired thing that will occur in the future while taking the means; if he does not take the means, then it is greed (tama'), and it is blameworthy in the Law.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between fear (khawf) and hope (raja') in the spiritual path. The author begins by commenting on the poetic line: 'Make fear dominant over hope, and travel to your Lord without distance.' He states that while engaging in remembrance (dhikr), one should prioritize fear of Allah in health over hope in His mercy. However, both fear and hope are necessary together, like the two wings of a bird; if one is missing, the bird falls. In health, fear should dominate because it drives one to worship and purifies the heart from vain desires. When one falls ill and nears death, hope should dominate because one is approaching the Generous Lord. The marginal note clarifies that this is the school of Malik, while al-Shafi'i holds that fear and hope should be balanced like equal wings in both health and sickness. The text then provides a broader classification: everything that enters the mind can be divided into past, present, or future. Past occurrences give remembrance (dhikr), present occurrences give spiritual finding (wajd), and future occurrences produce fear (if disliked) or hope (if beloved). Hope is defined as the heart's delight in awaiting something beloved. Finally, the author gives precise definitions: fear is anxiety for what is coming, grief is worry for what has passed, and hope is the heart's attachment to a desired future outcome while taking the means; without taking means, it becomes blameworthy greed (tama').

Key Terms
خوف
رجاء
حزن
ذكر
وجد
طمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hope is simply wishing for something good from Allah without effort.
Correction: The text defines hope as requiring taking the means; without that, it becomes blameworthy greed.

Checkpoint
According to the definitions, what is the heart's attachment to a desired future outcome while taking the means called?
A) Fear
B) Hope
C) Greed
D) Grief

Key Takeaway
True hope is active, not passive; it combines anticipation with effort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your past sins, do you remain stuck in grief (huzn) that paralyzes you, or does that grief drive you to seek forgiveness and change? What one past event do you need to turn into a catalyst for action today?
?
Is your hope in Allah's mercy truly active—accompanied by taking the means—or is it mere wishful thinking? Identify one area where you have hope but no effort, and commit to one concrete step you will take this week.
?
Fear of the future can either push you closer to Allah or away from Him. What is a specific fear you have, and how can you transform that fear into a proactive hope that motivates righteous action? Write down one action you will take today to address that fear.

Spiritual Travel: Attaching the Heart to Allah
Dardeer defines sayr and identifies the straight path

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that spiritual travel (sayr) in Sufi terminology means attaching the heart to Allah while opposing the soul's desires, and that this is the straight path to Allah.

Core Idea
The journey to Allah is an inner transformation, not a physical movement.

In this lesson, we explore Dardeer's explanation of the key term 'sayr' (spiritual travel). He clarifies that it is not about moving from place to place but about turning the heart exclusively towards Allah while resisting the lower self. This is the straight path that leads to Allah, followed by the 'shuttar'—the swift ones among the lovers of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the definition of sayr
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay close attention to how Dardeer defines 'sayr' as the heart's attachment to Allah.
The definition is repeated for emphasis.
2
Contrast with physical travel
Reflect on the difference between physical travel and inner spiritual travel. Dardeer explicitly distinguishes the two.
This helps grasp the interior nature of the path.
3
Understand who the shuttar are
The path is described as the way of the shuttar. Note that they are characterized by love and longing for Allah.
'Shuttar' comes from 'shata' meaning swiftness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسر سيرا حثيثا المولاك أي : سيّدك وخالقك ، بلا تناء أي: بلا تباعد عن الطريق المستقيم المُوَصلِ إلى الله تعالى، بأَنْ تُعَلِّقَ قلبَكَ بِغَيْرِهِ تعالى. وتقَدَّمَ أَنَّ السَّيرَ عِبارةٌ عَنْ تعلَّقِ القَلْبِ بِاللهِ تعالى معَ مُخالَفَةِ النَّفْسِ في شهواتها إيثارا له تعالى على غيره، وهذا هو الطريق المستقيمُ الموصلُ إلى الله تعالى، وهي طريق الشُّطَارِ مِنْ أهل المحبّة والشّوقِ إلى بارئ النسم. ومبناها على الموتِ بالإرادة؛ لَخَبَرِ (موتوا قبل أن تموتوا) ولذا قال سيّدي عُمَرُ بنُ الفارض ونفسي كانتْ قبل لوّامة، متى * أُطِعْهَا عَصَتْ، أَو أَعْصِ كَانتْ مُطيعتي فَحَمَّلْتُها ما الموتُ أيسَر بعضه * وأتعبتُها كيما تكونَ مُريحتي فعادتْ ومهما حُمَلَتْه تحمَّلتْ * هُ مِنِّي، وإِنْ خَفَّفْتُ عنها تأذَّتِ وأصولها عشرة: (۱) الشاطر في الاصطلاح الصوفي هو السابق المسرع إلى الله . [المعجم الوسيط ٥٠١/٢] (۲) قال ابن حجر: هو غير ثابت، وقال القاري: هو من كلام الصوفية، ومعناه: موتوا اختيارا بترك الشهوات قبل أن تموتوا اضطرارا بالموت الحقيقي. [ كشف الخفا ٢٩١/٢، الأسرار المرفوعة في الأحاديث الموضوعة ٣٦٣] (۳) عمر بن الفارض أبو الحسن علي بن المرشد العارف المحب صاحب الأحوال والشعر الرائق وصاحب الديوان المعروف، توفي بمصر ودفن بالمقطم. ميزان الاعتدال ٣٦٢/٢، طبقات الأولياء ٤٦٤]. (٤) أي أصول الطريق الموصلة إلى الله.
And travel quickly to your Lord, i.e. your Master and Creator, without distance, i.e. without remoteness from the straight path that leads to Allah Almighty, by attaching your heart to Him Almighty. It has preceded that travel (sayr) is an expression of the heart's attachment to Allah Almighty while opposing the soul in its desires, preferring Him Almighty over other than Him. And this is the straight path that leads to Allah Almighty, and it is the path of the shuttar (the swift ones) among the people of love and longing for the Creator of souls. Its foundation is death by will, because of the hadith 'Die before you die'. Hence my master Umar ibn al-Farid said: 'My soul was previously blameworthy; whenever I obeyed it, it disobeyed, and whenever I disobeyed it, it obeyed me. So I burdened it with something whose smallest part is easier than death, and I tired it so that it would become my rest. Then it became such that whatever I loaded upon it, it bore from me; and if I lightened it, it was hurt.' And its principles are ten: (1) al-shātir in the Sufi terminology is the one who outstrips and hurries to Allah. (2) Ibn Hajar said: it is not established, and al-Qari said: it is from the speech of the Sufis, and its meaning: die voluntarily by leaving desires before you die involuntarily by real death. (3) Umar ibn al-Farid: Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Murshid, the arif, the lover, possessor of states and elegant poetry, author of the famous diwan, died in Egypt and was buried in al-Muqattam. (4) That is, the principles of the path that leads to Allah.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage explains the second part of the poetic line: 'travel to your Lord without distance.' The author interprets 'travel quickly' (sir) as moving swiftly towards Allah by attaching the heart to Him while opposing the soul's desires. This is the straight path, the way of the 'shuttar' (the swift ones) who are lovers of Allah. The foundation of this path is 'death by will', based on the saying 'Die before you die.' To illustrate this struggle against the lower self, the author quotes a poem by Umar ibn al-Farid, describing how he burdened his soul until it became compliant. Then the author states that the principles (usul) of this path are ten, but he does not list them in this passage; instead, the text provides footnotes explaining key terms. The footnote defines 'shātir' as the one who hurries to Allah, and discusses the authenticity of the hadith 'Die before you die', noting that Ibn Hajar considers it not established, while al-Qari says it is from Sufi speech meaning voluntrary death by abandoning desires. Another footnote provides a brief biography of Umar ibn al-Farid. The final note clarifies that 'its principles' refers to the principles of the path leading to Allah.

Key Terms
سير
طريق مستقيم
شطار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dardeer, what is the meaning of 'sayr' (travel) in the spiritual context?
A) Moving from one physical place to another
B) The heart's attachment to Allah while opposing the soul's desires
C) Performing pilgrimages frequently
D) Reciting the names of Allah

Key Takeaway
Spiritual travel is the heart's journey away from desires and towards Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that sayr is attachment to Allah while opposing your soul’s desires. Think of the last desire you indulged—what did your heart attach to in that moment? If you knew that each desire chosen over Allah distances you from the straight path, how will you prepare yourself tomorrow when that same desire calls?
?
The passage says the shuttar are the people of love and longing. When was the last time you actually longed for Allah—not just recited prayers out of habit, but truly missed His presence? What is the one worldly attachment that chokes that longing? Will you starve it today?
?
You are commanded to travel quickly to your Lord, but your heart often feels sluggish or distracted. If you could name the single biggest obstacle that slows your spiritual travel, would you be willing to remove it—even if it means losing something you hold dear? What is that thing, and what is your first step to letting it go?

Death by Will: The Foundation of the Path
Dardeer establishes voluntary death as the foundation and cites a hadith and a poem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'death by will' as the foundational principle of the spiritual path, illustrated by the hadith 'Die before you die' and the poetry of Umar ibn al-Farid.

Core Idea
The spiritual path is built on voluntarily dying to your desires.

Dardeer now states that the straight path is based on 'death by will'—a voluntary death to one's ego and worldly attachments. He supports this with the well-known prophetic saying 'Die before you die' and illustrates the struggle against the lower self with a powerful poem by the Sufi poet Umar ibn al-Farid. The lesson also notes that the path has ten principles, though they are not detailed here.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the basis of the path
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Dardeer explicitly states that the path's foundation is death by will.
The phrase 'مبناها على الموت بالإرادة' is key.
2
Reflect on the hadith
Consider the meaning of 'Die before you die'. How does this relate to opposing the soul's desires?
This hadith is widely cited in Sufi literature.
3
Examine the poem
Read the poem by Umar ibn al-Farid. It describes the process of disciplining the soul. How does it reinforce the concept of voluntary death?
The poet describes burdening the soul until it becomes obedient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[المعجم الوسيط ٥٠١/٢] (۲) قال ابن حجر: هو غير ثابت، وقال القاري: هو من كلام الصوفية، ومعناه: موتوا اختيارا بترك الشهوات قبل أن تموتوا اضطرارا بالموت الحقيقي. And travel quickly to your Lord, i.e. your Master and Creator, without distance, i.e. without remoteness from the straight path that leads to Allah Almighty, by attaching your heart to Him Almighty. It has preceded that travel (sayr) is an expression of the heart's attachment to Allah Almighty while opposing the soul in its desires, preferring Him Almighty over other than Him. And this is the straight path that leads to Allah Almighty, and it is the path of the shuttar (the swift ones) among the people of love and longing for the Creator of souls. Its foundation is death by will, because of the hadith 'Die before you die'. Hence my master Umar ibn al-Farid said: 'My soul was previously blameworthy; whenever I obeyed it, it disobeyed, and whenever I disobeyed it, it obeyed me. So I burdened it with something whose smallest part is easier than death, and I tired it so that it would become my rest. Then it became such that whatever I loaded upon it, it bore from me; and if I lightened it, it was hurt.' And its principles are ten: (1) al-shātir in the Sufi terminology is the one who outstrips and hurries to Allah. (2) Ibn Hajar said: it is not established, and al-Qari said: it is from the speech of the Sufis, and its meaning: die voluntarily by leaving desires before you die involuntarily by real death. (3) Umar ibn al-Farid: Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Murshid, the arif, the lover, possessor of states and elegant poetry, author of the famous diwan, died in Egypt and was buried in al-Muqattam. (4) That is, the principles of the path that leads to Allah.

Misconception & Correction

What does Dardeer mean by 'death by will' (al-mawt bi'l-irada)?
A) Literally dying before one's time

?

?

?

Repentance and Its Three Pillars
Dardeer defines tawbah and lists its three pillars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tawbah (repentance) and its three essential pillars: remorse, resolve, and immediate desistance.

Core Idea
Repentance is returning to Allah through remorse, resolve, and immediate desistance.

In this foundational passage, Imam Dardeer explains the first principle of the spiritual path: repentance. He defines tawbah as returning to Allah from sins and then enumerates its three pillars. Let's read his words carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the three pillars
Find the three pillars listed. Note the wording for each.
The first two are remorse and resolve; the third is immediate desistance.
3
Understand why these are essential
Consider why remorse and resolve are necessary in every repentance, and how the third applies to ongoing sins.
The passage explicitly says the first two are necessary in every repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأَوَّلُ: التَّوبةُ مِنْ كُلِّ ذنبٍ، ولو صغيرةً على التحقيق، وإليه أشار بقوله (وجدِّدُ) جمع وزر، وهو المعصية. وجوبًا (التوبة) أي: الرجوع إلى الله تعالى (للأوزارِ) أَيْ: مِنْ أَجْلِ ارتكاب الأوزار - وأركانها ثلاثة الندَمُ على ما وقع منه مِنَ المُخالفاتِ مُراعاة حقٌّ اللَّهِ سُبْحانَه وتعالى، والعزم على أن لا يعود لمثله، وهذان لا بُدَّ منهما في كُلِّ تَوبة. والثالِثُ الإقلاع عن الذنب في الحال، وهذا إنما يتأتى في ذنبٍ لم ينقَضِ، فيجب الكفُّ عن استمام الزنا وشُرب الخمر، وعن أذيّةِ أحَدٍ، ورَدُّ المظالم إلى أهلها، واستسماحُ المظلوم إِنْ أَمَكَنَ، والا استغْفَرَ لَه وتصَدَّقَ له بما يُمكِنه ، فإنَّ الله تعالى إذا علِمَ صِدقَ العبدِ أرضى الله عنه خُصماءه. وتصِحُ التَّوبةُ مِنْ ذنب دونَ آخَرَ بخِلافِ السَّيرِ إلى الله تعالى فإنه إنما يصِحُ بالتَّوبة عنِ الجميع وتجبُ المبادرة بها، فتأخيرُها ذَنْبُ آخَرُ . وتوبةُ الكافِرِ عنْ كُفْرِه بالإسلام مقبولةٌ قطعًا، والمؤمِنُ الْمُذْنِبُ مِنْ ذَنبِه مقبولةٌ ظنَّا، وقيل: قطعا.
The first: repentance (at-tawbah) from every sin, even a minor one, definitively. And to it he pointed by his saying (wa jaddid) plural of wizr, which is disobedience. Obligatorily, repentance meaning returning to Allah, may He be exalted, from the sins, i.e., because of committing sins. And its pillars are three: remorse over what occurred from him of violations, observing the right of Allah, may He be glorified and exalted, and the resolve not to return to the like of it; these two are necessary in every repentance. And the third: desisting from the sin immediately. This only applies to a sin that has not ended; so one must stop continuing fornication and drinking wine, and stop harming anyone, and return injustices to their people, and seek forgiveness from the one wronged if possible, otherwise ask forgiveness for him and give charity on his behalf as much as he can. For Allah, may He be exalted, when He knows the sincerity of the servant, will satisfy his opponents on his behalf. And repentance from one sin without another is valid, contrary to the journey to Allah, for it is only valid by repentance from all. And one must hasten to it, for delaying it is another sin. And the repentance of the disbeliever from his disbelief by Islam is accepted definitively; and the believing sinner's repentance from his sin is accepted probabilistically, and it is said: definitively.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage explains the first foundation of the spiritual path: repentance (tawbah). It begins by stating that repentance is required from every sin, even minor sins, as indicated by the poet's word 'jaddid' (renew). Repentance literally means returning to Allah from sins. The author then enumerates three essential pillars: (1) remorse for having violated Allah's rights, (2) firm resolve not to return to the sin, and (3) immediate desistance from the sin if it is ongoing. For sins that have already ended, immediate desistance is not applicable; instead, one needs to stop continuing if the sin is ongoing, such as ceasing adultery or drinking, and must make amends for wrongs done to others by returning stolen property or seeking forgiveness. If one cannot seek forgiveness directly, he should pray for the person and give charity on his behalf, as Allah will satisfy the opponents if He sees sincerity. Repentance from one sin without repenting from others is valid, unlike the spiritual journey (sayr) to Allah, which requires repentance from all sins. One must hasten to repentance because delaying it is itself a sin. Finally, the passage discusses the acceptance of repentance: the repentance of a disbeliever who accepts Islam is certainly accepted, while the repentance of a sinful believer is accepted with probability (or certainty according to some).

Key Takeaway
Repentance has three pillars: remorse, resolve, and immediate desistance; the first two are always required.

?

?
If you are currently engaged in a sin, why are you delaying desistance? What is holding you back from immediately stopping for Allah’s sake right now?

Repentance from Ongoing Sins and Making Amends
Dardeer explains the third pillar and the requirements for sins involving others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the application of the third pillar (immediate desistance) to ongoing sins and the requirement to make amends for wrongs done to others.

Core Idea
For ongoing sins, repentance demands immediate stop; for wrongs against others, restitution or seeking forgiveness is required.

This passage expands on the third pillar of repentance—immediate desistance—by explaining how it applies to sins that are still ongoing and what must be done when others' rights have been violated. It also gives hope that Allah can reconcile opponents if the servant is sincere.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage from the beginning of the third pillar explanation.
2
Identify the rule for ongoing sins
Note that for sins like fornication or drinking, one must stop immediately.
3
Understand the process for wrongs against others
Note the steps: return injustices, seek forgiveness, or if unable, pray and give charity on their behalf.
The passage ends with a promise that Allah will satisfy opponents if He sees sincerity.

Validity of Specific Repentance and Acceptance Conditions
Dardeer discusses the validity of specific repentance, the obligation to hasten, and the acceptance of repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that repentance from one sin without others is valid (except for the spiritual journey), the obligation to hasten repentance, and the certain vs. probable acceptance for disbelievers and believers.

Core Idea
Repentance is valid for specific sins, but delaying it is itself a sin; the disbeliever's repentance through Islam is certainly accepted.

This final part of the passage addresses several important details: repentance can be from a single sin, the spiritual journey requires full repentance, hastening is obligatory, and the acceptance of repentance differs between a disbeliever and a sinful believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage from 'و تصح التوبة' to the end.
2
Understand exception for the spiritual journey
Note that while repentance from one sin is generally valid, the journey to Allah requires repentance from all sins.
3
Note the rule about hastening
Delaying repentance is itself a sin; therefore one must hasten.
4
Distinguish between disbeliever and believer
The repentance of a disbeliever through Islam is accepted definitively; the believer's repentance is accepted probabilistically (or definitively according to some).
