Conditions for the Spiritual Seeker
Advice on seeking a shaykh and sincerity in the path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key conditions for a spiritual seeker on the path of tajrid and tawhid, including the role of a shaykh and the necessity of sincerity and self-sacrifice.

Core Idea
The seeker's sincerity is proven by complete submission to a qualified shaykh and sacrifice of self, not wealth.

This passage from Imam Dardir's commentary outlines essential guidance for anyone whose soul yearns to tread the path of spiritual abstraction (tajrid) and immerse in divine oneness (tawhid). The text emphasizes the prerequisites of piety, reliance on Allah, and seeking the Prophet's intercession, but highlights the critical role of a gnostic shaykh. Let's explore the conditions and the seeker's proper attitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic and its literal meaning. Notice the sequence of instructions from the author.
The passage is dense with spiritual terminology.
2
Identify the seeker's prerequisites
List the four key actions mentioned before finding a shaykh: taqwa, seeking refuge in Allah, tawassul through the Prophet, and asking for a shaykh.
3
Understand the shaykh's role
The shaykh is described as one who nurtures, brings out of darkness, purifies, and gives from the wine of love. Reflect on these functions.
The shaykh is a spiritual physician.
4
Recognize the seeker's attitude upon meeting
The text commands to grasp the shaykh's hand firmly and be like a corpse in his hands—complete submission. Also to praise Allah for guidance.
This metaphor emphasizes total trust and obedience.
5
Reflect on the final exhortation
The verses highlight giving one's self, not wealth. Consider why self-sacrifice is the ultimate expression of love.
The poet contrasts giving soul vs. giving world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى مَنْ تشوَّقتْ نفْسُه إلى سُلوكِ طريق التجريد، حتى يستغرق في بحار التوحيد، مُلازمةُ التَّقْوى والالتجاء إلى اللهِ والتَّوَسُّلُ إليه برسوله عليه الصلاةُ والسلامُ في أن يجمَعَه على شَيخِ عارفٍ يُربّيه، ويُخْرِجُه مِنَ الظُّلُماتِ النفْسِيةِ، ويُصَفِّيه ويسقيه مِنْ خَمرِ المحبّة ويُصافيه. فإذا علمَ اللهُ صِدقَكَ أطْلَعَكَ عليه، فإذا اجتمعت به فشُدَّ يدَكَ عليه، وكُنْ كالميت بين يديه، وقُلِ الْحَمْدُ للهِ الَّذِي هَدَانَا هَذَا وَمَا كُنَّا لِنَهْتَدِيَ لَوْلا أَنْ هَدَانَا اللَّهُ ﴾ ثُمَّ خُذْ في الجد والابتهال، وجُدْ بَنَفْسِكَ لا بالمالِ كما قالَ: فنافس ببذل النفس فيها أخا الهوى * فَإِنْ قبلتها مِنكَ يا حبَّذا البذْلُ ومَنْ لم يجد في حُبِّ نُعْمَ بنفسه * ولو جادَ بالدُّنْيا إليه انتهى البُخْلُ
So for one whose soul yearns to travel the path of abstraction (tajrid) until it immerses in the seas of divine oneness (tawhid), [the duty is] constant observance of piety (taqwa), seeking refuge in Allah, and seeking means (tawassul) to Him through His Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) that He gather him to a gnostic shaykh (shaykh 'arif) who will nurture him, bring him out of the darknesses of the soul, purify him, and give him to drink from the wine of love and be close to him. When Allah knows your sincerity, He will show you to him. When you meet him, grasp his hand firmly, and be like a corpse in his hands, and say: 'Praise be to Allah Who has guided us to this, and we would never have been guided had Allah not guided us.' Then take up seriousness and supplication, and sacrifice your own self, not wealth, as he said: 'Compete in giving your soul, O lover, for if you give it, how excellent is that gift. And whoever does not give his own soul in love, even if he gave the world, he would be considered miserly.'
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage gives instructions for the spiritual seeker (murid) who desires the path of abstraction and immersion in tawhid. The author states that such a person must: 1) adhere to taqwa, 2) seek refuge in Allah, 3) seek means through the Prophet, and 4) ask to be brought to a gnostic shaykh who will train him. The shaykh's role is to bring the seeker out of spiritual darknesses, purify him, and give him to drink from the wine of divine love. Then the author says that when Allah sees sincerity, He will cause the seeker to meet such a shaykh. Upon meeting, the seeker should hold firmly to him and be like a corpse in his hands—completely submissive. He should praise Allah for guidance. Then he should engage in serious effort and supplication, sacrificing his own self (not merely wealth) as the verses emphasize: giving one's soul is the true generosity.

Key Terms
التجريد
التوحيد
التقوى
التوسل
شيخ عارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage encourages abandoning teachers or that giving wealth is sufficient for spiritual progress.
Correction: The passage does not call for abandoning teachers; rather it emphasizes finding one qualified shaykh and fully submitting to him. It also stresses that sacrificing one's self (soul) is superior to offering wealth.

Checkpoint
What must the seeker do upon meeting the shaykh, according to the passage?
A) Question him thoroughly
B) Be like a corpse in his hands
C) Immediately debate him
D) Give him money

Key Takeaway
The spiritual seeker must combine outward piety, sincere reliance on Allah, prophetic intercession, and complete submission to a qualified guide, sacrificing his own self rather than wealth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage demands total submission to a shaykh: 'be like a corpse in his hands'. Is there any part of your ego that resists this level of surrender? Can you identify one area where you hold back and commit it to prayer?
?
Sacrificing your own self, not wealth—what does that actually require from you? Are there comforts, opinions, or habits you cling to that you know you must release for the sake of love? When will you begin?

The Practice of Hunger: Conditions and Effects
Definition and conditions of hunger as a spiritual practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual practice of hunger, its conditions (eating one light halal meal per day), and how the type of food influences one's deeds.

Core Idea
The food you eat determines the quality of your deeds.

In this passage, Imam Dardir explains the sixth spiritual practice: hunger. He details the conditions for practicing hunger—eating only one light halal meal per day—and emphasizes that this is achieved through frequent fasting. Most importantly, he establishes a profound link between the food we consume and the actions we produce, categorizing food into three types: halal, haram, and ambiguous, each with corresponding effects on our deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the key terms like 'halal', 'haram', and 'mutashabih'.
The passage is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the practice of hunger
Identify the condition: one light meal per day from halal food of unknown origin. Note the role of fasting as a means to achieve this.
Hunger here is a chosen discipline, not starvation.
3
Analyze the food-deed connection
Examine the three types of food and their effects: halal produces righteous deeds, haram produces evil deeds, and ambiguous produces mixed deeds tainted by hypocrisy and self-admiration.
This shows the spiritual importance of lawful sustenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: الجوع اختيار (۲)، بأن لا يأكُلَ أكثَرَ مِنْ أَكْلِةٍ خفيفةٍ فِي يَومِه ولَيلَتِه مِنَ الحلال، وهو ما جهل أصله، ولا يمكنه ذلك في ابتداء أمره إلا بكثرة الصوم، فإنه لجام السائرين. واعلم أنَّ العمل ثمرة المأكول، فالأكل الحرام لا ينشأ عنه إلا أعمال خبيثةٌ مُحَرَّمَةٌ، والحلال الصرف لا ينشأ عنه إلا الأعمال الصالحة، والمتشابه ينشأ عنه أعمال مختلطةٌ لا تخلو عن الرياء والعُجب والخواطر الرديئة.
The sixth: Hunger is a choice, that one should not eat more than one light meal in his day and night from the lawful (halal) – that is food whose origin is unknown – and he cannot do that at the beginning of his affair except with much fasting, for it is the bridle of the travelers. And know that the deed is the fruit of what is eaten; forbidden food (haram) produces nothing but evil, forbidden deeds; pure halal produces nothing but righteous deeds; and ambiguous food (mutashabih) produces mixed deeds that are not free from hypocrisy (riya), self-admiration ('ujb), and base thoughts.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth practice: hunger chosen voluntarily. The author specifies that one should eat no more than one light meal per day from halal food, specifically food whose origin is unknown (presumably avoiding doubtful matters). At the beginning, this can only be achieved through frequent fasting, which is the bridle (bridle) of the travelers on the spiritual path. Then the author draws a connection between food and actions: deeds are the fruit of what is eaten. Haram food only produces evil haram actions; pure halal food produces only righteous actions; and mutashabih (ambiguous) food produces mixed actions tainted by riya, 'ujb, and bad thoughts. This emphasizes the importance of lawful sustenance for spiritual purification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The practice is about starving oneself without any conditions on food.
Correction: It is about moderation (one light meal) and requires halal food, specifically of unknown origin, and is aided by fasting.

Checkpoint

A) Pure righteous deeds
B) Mixed deeds with hypocrisy and self-admiration
C) Only evil deeds
D) No effect on deeds

Key Takeaway
Hunger practiced with lawful food purifies the soul, while the type of food directly affects the quality of one's actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Seventh Practice: Solitude
Definition and necessity of solitude with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of solitude in Islamic spiritual practice, its exceptions, and the reasons for its importance.

Core Idea
Solitude protects the heart, but necessary interactions remain permitted.

Imam Dardir now turns to the seventh practice: solitude. He defines it as complete withdrawal from people, but immediately qualifies it with three important exceptions. This passage shows that Islamic spirituality does not demand extreme isolation, but rather a balanced approach that prioritizes spiritual growth while fulfilling necessities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the full meaning of the text.
Notice the exceptions listed first, then the reason.
2
Identify the exceptions
Note the three cases where mixing is allowed: shaykh, righteous brother, and necessity of buying/selling.
3
Understand the harm of mixing
Reflect on why even gatherings free of sin cause darkness in the heart.
The passage mentions backbiting and slander as common.
4
Reflect on the poetic advice
Consider how the quote reinforces the teaching that socializing often leads to idle talk.
5
Apply the final guideline
Recognize the two legitimate reasons for meeting people: acquiring knowledge or mending a situation.
This provides balance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : العزلة عن الناسِ قاطبةً إلا عنْ شَيخه المُرَبّي له، أو أخ صالح يُعينه على العزلة والطاعة والهمة، وإلا لضرورة بيع أو شراء، إذْ مُخالطة الناسِ تُكسِبُ القلب ظُلمة، لو أنها تخلو عن ارتكاب المحرَّماتِ، فَكَيْفَ ولا يخلو مجلِسٌ عنها مِنْ غِيبة ونميمةٍ وغيرها، ولبعضهم: لقاء الناسِ لَيسَ يُفيدُ شَيئًا * إلا الهذيان مِنْ قيل وقال فأقلل من لقاء الناس إلا . لأخذ العلم أو إصلاح حال
The seventh: Solitude from all people except his nurturing shaykh, or a righteous brother who helps him in solitude, obedience, and high ambition, and except for necessity of buying or selling. For mixing with people earns the heart darkness, even if it is free from committing forbidden acts. How then when no gathering is free from backbiting, slander, and other things? And one of them said: 'Meeting people benefits nothing except nonsense of saying and saying.' So reduce meeting people except for acquiring knowledge or mending a situation.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh practice: complete solitude from people with three exceptions: 1) one's nurturing shaykh, 2) a righteous brother who helps in solitude, obedience, and high ambition, 3) for the necessity of buying or selling. The reason given is that mixing with people earns the heart darkness, even if the gatherings are free from actual forbidden acts – yet gatherings are rarely free from backbiting, slander, etc. The author quotes a poet: meeting people gives nothing but idle talk. Finally, he advises to reduce meetings except for two purposes: taking knowledge or correcting a situation (i.e., fulfilling a need).

The Eighth Practice: Silence and Heart Concentration
Definition of silence and its purpose in spiritual concentration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual practice of silence as a tool for attaining heart concentration (jamiyya), including its exception for the remembrance of Allah.

Core Idea
Silence is not about muteness but about gathering the heart on Allah.

This passage introduces the eighth spiritual practice: silence. It clarifies that the goal is not absolute silence but a focused heart, achieved by avoiding unnecessary speech that causes dispersion. The exception for dhikr shows that speech in remembrance of Allah is beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the key terms: ṣamt, dhikr, tafarruq, jamiyya.
The passage is concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the exception and its reason
Notice that silence is not absolute—it is broken for dhikr. The reason is that speech causes tafarruq (dispersion), while the goal is jamiyya (togetherness).
This contrasts worldly speech with spiritual speech.
3
Understand the condition and derivation
The silence is prescribed when one must mix with people out of necessity. The concept is derived from the term 'jamiyya', which means gathering the heart on the Truth.
This shows the practice is grounded in a broader spiritual principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : الصمتُ (۱) إلا عَنْ ذِكرِ اللهِ تعالى، فإنَّ الكلامَ يوجِبُ التَّفَرُّق، والمطلوب الصمت معيّة (٢)، وهذا على تقدير مخالطة الناس لضرورة، وهذه مأخوذةٌ مِنْ قَولِنا الجمعية
The eighth: Silence, except for the remembrance of Allah Almighty, for speech necessitates dispersion (tafarruq), and the desired goal is silence of togetherness (jamiyya). This is on the assumption of mixing with people out of necessity, and this is taken from our saying: 'togetherness (jamiyya).'
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth practice: silence, with an exception for dhikr (remembrance of Allah). The reason given is that speech causes tafarruq (dispersion, disunity of focus), while the desired state is silence that brings jamiyya (togetherness, concentration of the heart). The author notes that this silence is observed when one must mix with people out of necessity. The concept is derived from the term 'jamiyya', which implies gathering the heart on the Truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Purifying the Heart through Striving and Vigilance
Explanation of a verse on purifying the heart from distractions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'purifying the heart from other than Allah' and the means of achieving it through effort and devotion, as taught by Imam Dardir.

Core Idea
True purification requires active struggle against the soul and vigilant prayer at dawn.

Imam Dardir explains a verse that encapsulates the spiritual path: cleansing the heart from everything that distracts from Allah. He defines 'other than Allah' broadly, including even knowledge and good deeds if they occupy the heart. Then he presents two essential means: sincere effort and the vigil of the pre-dawn prayer. Let's explore this foundational teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and explanation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its literal translation. Notice how the author expands on the meaning of 'other than Allah'.
The verse is a poetic line from al-Kharida.
2
Identify what 'other than Allah' includes
The author lists wealth, spouse, child, status, knowledge, action, and anything that distracts the heart. Consider why even good things are included.
This shows the comprehensive nature of tazkiya.
3
Understand the role of effort (jidd)
Effort is defined as ijtihad. The Quranic verse (29:69) is cited to show that striving leads to guidance. Reflect on the connection between effort and divine guidance.
4
Grasp the definition of struggle (mujahada)
The struggle is opposing the soul's desires out of fear of Allah after repentance. Note the sequence: repentance first, then ongoing opposition to the self.
5
Reflect on practical implications
Consider how this teaching applies to your own life. What distractions occupy your heart? How can you incorporate effort and pre-dawn prayer?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلص القلبَ مِنَ الأغيار * بالجد والقيام في الأسْحَارِ وخلص القلبَ مِنَ الأغيار ) أي : تِما سوى الله تعالى، مِنْ مالِ وزَوجةٍ وولد وجاه وعِلْمٍ وعَمَلٍ، وغَيْرِها مِنْ كُلِّ مُشغِل عنْ تعلَّقِ القَلْبِ بالرَّبِّ. (بالجد) - بكسر الجيم - أي: الاجتهاد، أي: بسببِه قال تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ جَاهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا ) (۳). والمجاهدة تكونُ بمُخالفة النفس في هَواها معَ الخَوفِ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى بعد التوبة،
And purify the heart from other than Allah (al-aghyar) with effort (jidd) and standing in the before-dawn hours (as-har). 'And purify the heart from other than Allah' means everything other than Allah Almighty: wealth, spouse, child, status, knowledge, action, and all other things that occupy the heart from attachment to the Lord. 'With effort' (bi al-jidd) – with a kasra on the jim – meaning exertion (ijtihad), i.e., by means of it. He said, the Exalted: 'And those who strive for Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways.' And the struggle (mujahada) consists of opposing the soul in its desires with fear of Allah Almighty after repentance.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage explains the verse: 'Purify the heart from other than Allah through effort and standing in the before-dawn hours.' 'Other than Allah' (al-aghyar) is glossed as everything besides Allah: wealth, spouse, child, status, knowledge, action, and anything that distracts the heart from attachment to the Lord. 'Effort' (al-jidd) is interpreted as exertion (ijtihad), and the author cites Quran 29:69: 'And those who strive for Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways.' Then he defines the struggle (mujahada) as opposing the soul's desires out of fear of Allah after repentance. This shows that purification requires active effort and vigilant night prayer as a means to detach the heart from worldly attachments.

الأغيار
الجِدّ
القيام في الأسحار
المجاهدة
مخالفة النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Purification is merely a passive hope or prayer, not requiring active striving.
Correction: Purification demands active jihad against the soul's desires and consistent pre-dawn worship, as stated in the passage: 'the struggle consists of opposing the soul in its desires with fear of Allah after repentance.'

Checkpoint
What is included in 'other than Allah' (al-aghyar) according to the passage?
A) Only wealth and status
B) Everything besides Allah, including knowledge and action
C) Only sinful things
D) Only people

Key Takeaway
Purification of the heart is achieved through diligent effort and the pre-dawn worship, by opposing the soul's whims after repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

You may have repented, but the source says the struggle continues after repentance. What specific desire or habit have you not yet opposed because it's too comfortable?

The Practice of Silence and Purification of the Heart
The author discusses the eighth practice: silence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual practice of silence (except for dhikr) and its role in purifying the heart from distractions.

Core Idea
Silence leads to closeness with Allah; speech causes dispersion.

This passage presents the eighth practice in a series of spiritual disciplines: silence. The author emphasizes that silence is not absolute but is prescribed except for the remembrance of Allah. The goal is to achieve a state of inner intimacy with Allah, free from the distractions of excessive talk. The lesson also connects this practice to the broader theme of purifying the heart from everything other than Allah through sincere striving.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of ideas: the importance of silence, the exception for dhikr, and the connection to purifying the heart.
Notice the inclusion of a couplet advising minimal socializing.
2
Understand the rule of silence
The eighth practice is silence except for dhikr. Recognize why speech causes 'tafarruq' (dispersion) and why the goal is 'ma'iyya' (intimacy with Allah). This is a spiritual discipline for those who must interact with people out of necessity.
The author is addressing those who mix with people for valid reasons.
3
Connect to purification of the heart
The passage links silence to the broader concept of purifying the heart from all distractions (other than Allah). The verse 'And purify the heart from other-than-Allah' is explained to include wealth, family, status, knowledge, etc.
Purification requires striving (jihad against the soul) and standing in the dawn hours.

Commonly, people think silence means complete muteness. The author clarifies it is only from useless talk, and that remembrance of Allah is always allowed and encouraged.

The Ninth Practice: Staying Awake at Night (Sahar)
The author explains the ninth practice: staying awake in the last third of the night.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth recommended practice: staying awake during the last third of the night for worship, and its special virtue.

Core Idea
The last third of the night is a precious time for dhikr and dua.

This lesson presents the ninth practice from the poem: staying awake during the last third of the night (sahar). The author explains its purpose—tahajjud, istighfar, and dhikr—and supports it with Quranic verses. He also highlights that dhikr at this time has a greater spiritual impact. Let's examine the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text slowly, then the literal translation. Notice how the author defines the practice and lists its purposes.
The term 'sahar' refers specifically to the last portion of the night before dawn.
2
Identify the three acts of worship
The passage mentions three acts: night prayer (tahajjud), seeking forgiveness (istighfar), and remembrance (dhikr). Observe that dhikr is singled out for emphasis.
Although dhikr is included in previous practices, the author reiterates it due to its importance at this time.
3
Reflect on the Quranic support
The author quotes Quran 51:17-18 about those who sleep little and seek forgiveness at dawn. Consider how this verse reinforces the practice.
4
Understand the special effect of dawn dhikr
The passage concludes that remembrance at dawn has a greater effect than at other times. Think about why this might be so from a spiritual perspective.
This is a key takeaway: timing matters in worship.

He supports this with the Quranic verses praising those who wake at night. Finally, he states that dhikr at this time has a greater spiritual effect.

The Tenth Practice: Contemplation and Constant Remembrance
Description of the tenth practice: contemplation and remembrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complementary roles of contemplation and remembrance in the spiritual path, and why remembrance is considered the greatest pillar.

Core Idea
Remembrance of Allah is the heart’s path to purity.

This passage from Imam Dardir's commentary introduces the tenth spiritual practice, which combines contemplation (fikr) and constant remembrance (dhikr). Contemplation of Allah's creation leads to knowledge and modesty, while remembrance—done in all states—purifies the heart and causes the remembrance of Allah to dominate. The author emphasizes that dhikr is the greatest pillar of the spiritual path.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get a sense of the author's flow and the key concepts introduced.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the clear statements about contemplation and remembrance.
2
Identify the two practices
Find where the author describes contemplation and remembrance. Notice the specific benefits mentioned for each.
Contemplation increases knowledge and modesty; remembrance is to be performed in all states.
3
Reflect on why remembrance is greatest
Consider the author's reason: because the purpose is to purify the heart from everything other than Allah, and abundance of dhikr causes Allah to dominate the heart.
This highlights the centrality of dhikr in spiritual purification.

The Tenth Step: Contemplation and Constant Remembrance
Dardeer defines the tenth step as constant contemplation and dhikr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the tenth spiritual step combines contemplation of Allah's creation and continuous remembrance, with dhikr as the greatest pillar.

Core Idea
Contemplation illuminates wisdom, but dhikr dominates the heart.

Imam Dardeer now explains the tenth and final step of spiritual purification: contemplation and constant remembrance. This step is the culmination of the path, merging intellectual wonder with heartfelt devotion. The passage not only defines the step but also elevates dhikr as the most important practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
Notice how Dardeer moves from defining the step to emphasizing dhikr.
2
Identify the two components
The step has two parts: contemplation (tafakkur) and remembrance (dhikr). Note how each is described.
Contemplation is of Allah's wonderful creation; dhikr is continuous in all postures.
3
Understand the primacy of dhikr
Dardeer declares dhikr as the greatest pillar. Ask why it is given such status.
The explanation follows in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر: التفكر في بديع صنع الله؛ لإدراك دقائق الحكم لتزداد علما وحياء، والذكر قياما وقعودا واضطجاعا على سبيل الدوام، وإليه أشار بقوله: (والفكر والذكر على الدوام). واعلم: أن الذكر أعظم أركان الطريق؛ لأن المقصود منها تخليص القلوب عما سوى الله تعالى، وهو أعظمها في ذلك؛ لأن كثرته توجب استيلاء المذكور على القلب، حتى لا يكون فيه سواه، بل جميع الأركان تنشأ عنه؛ لأنه يورث القلب نورا ساطعا، به يزهد الدنيا التي حبها رأس كل خطيئة، ولذا قالوا: من أعطي الذكر فقد أعطي منشور الولاية، فالمداومة عليه دليل ولاية المشتغل به.
The tenth: contemplation (tafakkur) of the wonderful creation of Allah, to perceive subtle wisdoms so that one may increase in knowledge and modesty; and remembrance (dhikr) while standing, sitting, and lying down, continuously. And to this he pointed with his saying: (Contemplation and remembrance continuously). And know: that remembrance (dhikr) is the greatest of the pillars of the path, because the purpose of them is to purify the hearts from other than Allah Almighty, and it is the greatest of them in that; because its abundance necessitates the dominance of the Remembered (al-Madhkur) over the heart, so that there is no other than Him in it; rather, all the pillars originate from it, because it bestows upon the heart a shining light, by which one becomes ascetic in this world, the love of which is the head of every sin. Hence they said: 'Whoever is given dhikr has been given the certificate of sainthood (manshur al-wilaya).' So perseverance upon it is evidence of the sainthood of the one engaged in it.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage explains the tenth step: contemplation (تفكر) of Allah's magnificent creation and constant remembrance (ذكر) in all postures. The author emphasizes that dhikr is the greatest of the spiritual pillars because its purpose is to purify the heart from everything except Allah. The abundance of dhikr causes the remembrance of the Remembered (Allah) to dominate the heart until nothing else remains. All other pillars stem from dhikr, as it gives the heart a brilliant light that leads to detachment from worldly desires—love of which is the root of all sins. The author quotes the saying: 'Whoever is given dhikr has been given the certificate of sainthood,' meaning that persistent dhikr is evidence of one's closeness to Allah.

الذكر
الطريق
منشور الولاية

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage emphasizes dhikr as the greatest pillar and the source of all others.

Key Takeaway
The tenth step requires both contemplation of Allah's creation and perpetual remembrance, with dhikr being the most important pillar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If dhikr is the certificate of sainthood, then every moment you spend in remembrance is an investment in your eternal rank. Are you treating your dhikr as a casual habit or as the most important act of your day? What would change if you truly believed that your next 'SubhanAllah' could transform your heart?

The First Type of Dhikr and the Stages of Presence
Dardeer describes the first type of dhikr (tongue) and the progression from heedlessness to absorption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of dhikr (verbal remembrance) and the spiritual progression from heedlessness to absorption, according to Imam Dardeer.

Core Idea
Even imperfect dhikr with heedlessness can elevate you to perfect presence and absorption.

Imam Dardeer begins his discussion of remembrance (dhikr) by classifying it into two types, but he focuses here on the first: remembrance with the tongue. This is the practice of beginners, and he gives crucial advice about persistence despite heedlessness. This passage teaches that the path to spiritual presence starts with consistent verbal dhikr, even when the heart is distracted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall idea of the classification and the stages mentioned.
Notice that only the first type of dhikr is described here; the second type is not included.
2
Identify the three stages of dhikr
From the passage, extract the three stages: dhikr with heedlessness, dhikr with presence of heart, and dhikr with absence from everything except Allah. Understand their progression.
These stages represent a spiritual ladder from beginner to advanced.
3
Reflect on persistence despite heedlessness
Consider the key principle: do not abandon dhikr even if you are heedless. The passage gives a hope that even imperfect dhikr can lead to higher states.
This counters the misconception that dhikr must be perfect to be beneficial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذكر نوعان الأول: الذكر باللسان، وهو شأن أصحاب البدايات، فيجب عليهم موالاة الذكر باللسان مع تكلف الحضور بالقلب، حتى يصير الحضور طبيعة له. ولا يترك الذكر لوجود الغفلة فيه، فلرب ذكر مع غفلة يرفعه إلى الذكر مع الحضور، ولرب ذكر مع الحضور، يرفعه إلى الذكر مع الغيبة عما سوى المذكور.
And remembrance (dhikr) is of two types. The first: remembrance with the tongue, and it is the concern of beginners. So they must continue remembrance with the tongue while taking pains to have presence of heart, until presence becomes natural for them. And he should not abandon remembrance due to the existence of heedlessness in it, for perhaps remembrance with heedlessness will raise him to remembrance with presence, and perhaps remembrance with presence will raise him to remembrance with absence from other than the Remembered.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage classifies dhikr into two types but explicitly describes only the first: dhikr with the tongue (الذكر باللسان). This is the practice of beginners (أصحاب البدايات). They are obligated to persist in verbal dhikr while making an effort to have presence of heart (حضور بالقلب), until such presence becomes second nature. The author then gives an important spiritual principle: one should not abandon dhikr even if it is accompanied by heedlessness (غفلة). He explains the gradual elevation: dhikr with heedlessness may lead to dhikr with presence, and dhikr with presence may lead to dhikr with absence from everything other than the Remembered (الغيبة عما سوى المذكور). This shows the transformative power of persistent dhikr.

Key Terms
الذكر باللسان
الحضور بالقلب
الغفلة
الغيبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dhikr without concentration is useless and should be stopped.
Correction: The passage teaches that even dhikr with heedlessness can elevate one to higher stages; never abandon dhikr.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Persistent verbal dhikr, even with heedlessness, is the beginner's path to spiritual presence and eventual absorption in Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Spiritual States: Murāqaba, Mushāhada, and Mu‘āyana
Defines murāqaba, mushāhada, mu‘āyana and explains their progression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of murāqaba, mushāhada, and mu‘āyana, and how they form a progressive path to witnessing the unity of acts and essence.

Core Idea
Three spiritual states lead from watchfulness to direct vision of God's sole agency.

In this passage, Imam Dardīr outlines the journey of the spiritual seeker through three stages: watchfulness (murāqaba), witnessing (mushāhada), and direct vision (mu‘āyana). Using the example of eating, he illustrates how observing God in every detail leads to the realization that there is no agent but God—a profound shift in perception that culminates in the unity of essence. This lesson explores these states and clarifies common misunderstandings about their meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the poetic opening and the detailed example of eating.
The passage uses a verse line before prose commentary.
2
Identify the three states
Find where murāqaba, mushāhada, and mu‘āyana are defined. Note the progression: by murāqaba you ascend to mushāhada, and by mushāhada to mu‘āyana.
3
Examine the eating example
The author uses eating to explain murāqaba. Trace how he breaks down the process—from food arriving to digestion—to show that God is the sole agent.
This leads to the concept of waḥdat al-af‘āl (unity of acts).
4
Understand waḥdat al-af‘āl and waḥdat al-dhāt
Notice when these terms appear: after strong murāqaba comes waḥdat al-af‘āl; after stronger mushāhada comes waḥdat al-dhāt (mu‘āyana). The passage then mentions a further stage: witnessing God before everything.
These are experiential, not intellectual, realizations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُرَاقِبَا الله في الأحوالِ * لترتقي معالم الكمال
مُرَاقِبَا الله في (الأحوالِ أي جميع أحوالك، فإنك بالمراقبة ترتقي إلى وبالمشاهدة ترتقي إلى المعاينة.
المشاهدة،
والمراقبةُ : مُلاحظةُ الحقِّ تعالى عِندَ كُلِّ شَيءٍ  َمثَلًا إذا لاحظته حالَ قصدِ النفْسِ الوُقوعَ في المعصية وجدته تعالى مُطلعا عليك، فترجع عنها؛ حياءً منه، وإذا لاحظته حالَ أكْلِكَ وجَدته تعالى هو الذي ساقَ إليك ذلك الطَّعامَ مِنْ غَير حَولِ مِنكَ ولا قوة لك، ثُمَّ وجَدته حرَّكَ يدك إلى تناوله، وجعَلَ فيك القدرة على رفْعِه لفَمِكَ، ثُمَّ حرَّكَ فمَكَ وأجرى فيه الرّيق، ثُمَّ خَلَقَ فيكَ قُوَّةَ اللَّذَّةِ فَسَاقَه إِلى المَعِدِةِ، ثُمَّ رَتَّبَ على ذلك قوّةً في جسمِكَ وربّاكَ، فجعَلَ مِنه للخم نصيبا وللعظم نصيبا وللعصَبِ نصيبا، وما فضل مما لا منفعة فيه أخرَجَه، فتعلَمُ بذلك أنه لا فاعلَ سِواه، فإذا قَوِيَ هذا المعنى فيك سُمّي وَحدة الأفعال»، وصرتَ مُشاهِدًا الله في كُلِّ شَيءٍ.
فإذا قويتْ هذه المشاهدة حتى غِبتَ عما سِوى اللهِ سُمِّيتُ المُعاينةَ ووَحْدَةَ
الذات.
فإذا زاد التمكين شاهدتَ بعد ذلك أنه خالقٌ  ِلعَبْدِه وما عَمِلَ، وهذا معنى قولهم «مُشاهدةُ الله قبلَ كُلِّ شَيءٍ »
وهذه أمورٌ ذَوقيةٌ مِنْ وراء طورِ العقْلِ لَا يَعْرِفُهَا إِلا أَهْلُ العِنايَاتِ والنُّفوسِ
القدسيّة رَضِي اللَّه عنهم وعنا .
بهم
Be watchful of God in states * so that you may ascend to the levels of perfection. Be watchful of God in (states) meaning all your states, for by watchfulness (murāqaba) you ascend to witnessing (mushāhada), and by witnessing you ascend to direct vision (mu‘āyana). Witnessing. And watchfulness (murāqaba) is: observing the Truth, Exalted, at every thing. For example, if you observe Him when the soul intends to fall into disobedience, you find Him, Exalted, aware of you, so you desist out of shame before Him. And if you observe Him while eating, you find Him, Exalted, the One who brought that food to you without any capacity or power from you, then you found Him moving your hand to take it, and placing in you the ability to raise it to your mouth, then moving your mouth and causing saliva to flow, then creating in you the power of pleasure and conveying it to the stomach, then arranging thereby strength in your body and nourishing you, making from it a portion for flesh, a portion for bone, a portion for nerve, and what remains that has no benefit He expels. So you come to know thereby that there is no agent but Him. When this meaning becomes strong in you, it is called 'the unity of acts' (waḥdat al-af‘āl), and you become one who witnesses God in everything. Then when this witnessing becomes strong until you are absent from other than God, it is called direct vision (mu‘āyana) and the unity of essence (waḥdat al-dhāt). Then when establishment increases, you witness after that that He is the Creator of His servant and what he did, and this is the meaning of their saying: 'Witnessing God before everything.' And these are matters of taste (dhawqiyya) beyond the realm of intellect; none knows them except the people of divine attentions and sanctified souls, may God be pleased with them and with us by them.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines three progressive spiritual states: murāqaba (watchfulness), mushāhada (witnessing), and mu‘āyana (direct vision). It begins by stating that through murāqaba one ascends to mushāhada, and through mushāhada to mu‘āyana. Then it defines murāqaba as observing the Divine in all things, giving two examples: when tempted to sin, one observes God's awareness and refrains out of shame; when eating, one observes God's actions—providing food, moving the hand, enabling chewing, digestion, nourishment, and expulsion of waste. This leads to the realization that no agent exists but God, called waḥdat al-af‘āl (unity of acts). When this witnessing intensifies and one is absent from all besides God, it becomes mu‘āyana (direct vision), also called waḥdat al-dhāt (unity of essence). Further establishment brings witnessing that God is the Creator of both the servant and his actions, known as 'witnessing God before everything.' The author concludes that these are experiential matters beyond reason, known only to the elite sanctified souls.

Key Terms
مراقبة
مشاهدة
معاينة
وحدة الأفعال
وحدة الذات
أمور ذوقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That waḥdat al-af‘āl or waḥdat al-dhāt implies pantheism (God is identical with creation).
Correction: The passage maintains that God is the sole agent and creator, not that He is identical with creation. It affirms God's transcendence while recognizing His exclusive causality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the outcome of strong murāqaba regarding acts?
A) A feeling of independence in one's actions.
B) The realization that there is no agent but God (waḥdat al-af‘āl).
C) Direct vision of God (mu‘āyana).
D) The ability to perform miracles.

Key Takeaway
The spiritual seeker progresses from watchfulness (murāqaba) to witnessing (mushāhada) to direct vision (mu‘āyana), culminating in the experiential realization of God's sole agency and unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you eat your next meal, will you truly observe God's detailed involvement as described, or will you take it for granted? What would change in your heart if you did?
?
Have you ever stopped yourself from a sin because you felt God's awareness? If not, what is holding you back from cultivating that awareness starting today?
?
The passage says these states are 'beyond the realm of intellect.' Are you relying too much on your own reasoning in your spiritual life, neglecting the experiential taste that only comes through sincere practice?

Outward Etiquettes: Purity, Modesty, and Respect
Sanusi lists the first four etiquettes of the spiritual path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational etiquettes of physical purity, modesty, and respect for elders and scholars that a seeker must adopt.

Core Idea
Perfection begins with outward purity and respect for God, angels, and teachers.

The Sufi path is not only about inner states; it begins with outward conduct. These etiquettes establish the baseline for a seeker's character, covering purity, modesty, and proper manners toward others, especially scholars and shaykhs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on physical and moral etiquettes
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read its literal translation. Let the author's words set the foundation.
Notice that the passage starts with 'mulāzamah al-ṭahārah' – adherence to purity.
2
Identify the four etiquettes
Find the four distinct etiquettes listed: purity, covering private parts, respecting elders, and etiquette with scholars. Write them down.
Each is introduced by 'wa-minhā' – 'and among them'.
3
Reflect on the reasons given
For each etiquette, note the reason provided (e.g., shame before God and angels, inheritors of prophets). Ask why these reasons are important.
This step deepens understanding of the wisdom behind the rules.
4
Connect to the goal of perfection
Consider how these outward practices contribute to spiritual perfection, the stated aim of the etiquettes.
The passage opens by saying these etiquettes lead to kamāl (perfection).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ آداب هذه الطائفة التي يحصُلُ بها الكمالُ: مُلازَمةُ الطهارة، والنَّومُ عليها . وعدَمُ كشْفِ العَورة المُغلَّظة في الخلَواتِ؛ حياءً مِنَ اللهِ ومِنَ الملائكة. ومنها : توقيرُ الكبير والشفقة على الصغير والأرامل والمساكين، بل على جميع الخلق. ومنها: الأدب معَ أَهْلِ العِلْم خُصوصًا خَدَمَةَ الشريعة ومشايخ الطريق؛ فإنهم ورثة الأنبياء. ومنها: أن لا يزورَ أَحَدًا مِنَ الصالحين ما دامَ تحتَ التربية قبل الكمال؛ خوفًا مِنْ أن يرى كرامةً أو خُلُقًا في أحَدِهم لم يره في شَيخه، فيعتَقِدُ في شَيخه النقْصَ فيُحرَمُ مدَدَه. ومنها: سوء الظن بنفسه وحُسْنُه بغيره، حتى يرى أنَّ كُلَّ أَحَدٍ أَحسَنُ منه حالا. ومنها: أنْ لا ينتصر لنفْسِه في أمر. ومنها: أن يرى عبادته دائما قد دخلها الخللُ مِنَ الرياءِ والخواطر الردية، ومِثْلُها يستحقُ عليها العقاب لولا مُسامحة الله تعالى له، فيستغفرَ مِنْ عِبادتِه ومِنَ اسْتِغْفارِه. ومنها: أنْ لا يتكَلَّمَ بكلام العارفين مِنَ الفرْقِ والجمع، والبقاء والفناء ما لم يَكْمُلْ، على أنَّ الأولى للكاملِ تَركُ ذلك، إلا لحاجة تقتضي ذلك. ومنها: مُحاسبة النفس على ما ارتكبته مِنَ المُحَرَّماتِ والمكروهات وفضول المباحات، وعلى ما وقع في نفْسِهِ مِنَ الخواطر النفسانيةِ والشَّيطانية، والاستغفار منها. والفرقُ بَينَ الخاطر النفساني والشَّيطاني: أنَّ الأَولَ: يكون بإلحاح على المعصية أو الشهوة، كالطفل الذي يُلح على أمه حتى تغطيه ما يُريدُ، فيجب قمعها عن ذلك بمُلازمةِ الذِّكْرِ وبيانِ عاقبة هذا الأمرِ والتَّوجه إلى الشَّيخ والثاني: يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِ إلحاح، بل يأمُرُ بالمعصية ويُزَيَّنها، فإن طاوَعَه الشخص، وإلا انتقَلَ لأُخرى؛ لأنَّ قصده الغواية على أي حالة تكون، لا معصية بخصوصها.
And among the etiquettes of this group by which perfection is attained: adherence to purity and sleeping in a state of purity. And not uncovering the severe private parts in solitude out of shame before God and the angels. And among them: respecting the elder and compassion for the young, widows, and the poor, indeed for all creatures. And among them: etiquette with the people of knowledge, especially the servants of the Sacred Law and the shaykhs of the path; for they are the inheritors of the prophets. And among them: that one should not visit any of the righteous while he is under spiritual training before perfection, for fear that he might see a miracle or a character trait in one of them that he has not seen in his shaykh, and thus believe his shaykh to be deficient and so be deprived of his spiritual flow (madad). And among them: thinking ill of oneself and thinking well of others, so that he sees that everyone is in a better state than he. And among them: not defending oneself in any matter. And among them: that he should always see his worship as having defectiveness from ostentation and evil thoughts, and that such worship deserves punishment were it not for God's forgiveness, so he asks forgiveness for his worship and for his seeking forgiveness. And among them: that he should not speak the speech of the knowers (‘ārifīn) concerning separation (farq) and union (jam‘), and subsistence (baqā’) and annihilation (fanā’), until he becomes perfect; indeed, it is preferable even for the perfect one to leave that except for a necessary need. And among them: self-accounting (muḥāsaba) for what he has committed of forbidden things, disliked things, and excess of permissible things, and for what has occurred in his soul of psychological and satanic thoughts, and seeking forgiveness from them. And the difference between the psychological thought and the satanic thought is that the first comes with insistence on sin or lust, like a child who insists on his mother until she gives him what he wants; so it must be suppressed by constant remembrance (dhikr), explaining the consequence of this matter, and turning to the shaykh. The second comes without insistence; rather it commands sin and makes it appear attractive. If the person obeys, fine; otherwise it moves to another, because its purpose is misguidance in any form, not a particular sin.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage enumerates the essential etiquettes (ādāb) of the Sufi path that lead to perfection. The first set deals with physical and moral conduct: maintaining ritual purity even while sleeping, covering one's private parts even in solitude out of modesty before God and angels, respecting elders and showing compassion to all creatures, and especially showing proper etiquette toward scholars, particularly the guardians of the Law and the shaykhs of the path, as they are the inheritors of prophets. The second set addresses spiritual discipline: a disciple should not visit other righteous people during his training lest he see in them a virtue absent in his own shaykh, which could cause him to lose the spiritual flow (madad) from the shaykh. One should have a low opinion of oneself and think well of others, never defend oneself, and always consider one's worship as tainted by ostentation and evil thoughts, thus requiring constant repentance—even from one's repentance. One should not speak about advanced concepts like farq (separation) and jam‘ (union), baqā’ (subsistence) and fanā’ (annihilation) until fully perfected, and even then it is better to refrain unless necessary. Finally, one must practice self-accounting (muḥāsaba) for all actions, including forbidden, disliked, and excessive permissible acts, and for psychological and satanic thoughts. A key distinction is given: psychological thoughts pressure relentlessly like a demanding child, requiring suppression through dhikr, reflection, and turning to the shaykh; satanic thoughts are whimsical, tempting different sins, aiming simply at misguidance.

Key Terms
آداب
طهارة
توقير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inner purification is sufficient without outward etiquettes.
Correction: The passage shows that outward purity, modesty, respect, and proper conduct are essential foundations for spiritual perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why should one not uncover the severe private parts in solitude?
A) Because of social norms
B) Out of shame before God and the angels
C) Because it is summer
D) To test one's willpower

Key Takeaway
Perfection on the path begins with outward etiquette: purity, modesty, and respect for scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are alone at night, do you maintain your wudu or even sleep in a state of purity? If not, what does that reveal about your awareness that God sees you at every moment?
?
Think of a time you were in a private space and uncovered your private parts carelessly. Did you remember that the angels are with you, recording your actions? How can you train yourself to feel that divine presence even in solitude?
?
Consider how you treat the scholars and teachers in your life—do you honor them as inheritors of the prophets, or do you take them for granted? What is one concrete change you can make today to show them the adab Sanusi describes?

Spiritual Discipline: Shaykh Loyalty and Self-Criticism
Sanusi lists etiquettes concerning the shaykh and self-perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of unwavering loyalty to one's shaykh, self-criticism, and humility in the spiritual journey.

Core Idea
Loyalty to one's shaykh and self-blame are essential for spiritual growth.

The disciple's relationship with his shaykh is delicate. This lesson warns against actions that can break that connection, such as visiting other saints during training, and emphasizes thinking ill of oneself, not defending oneself, and seeing one's worship as defective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on inner discipline
Read the Arabic and translation for this lesson, focusing on the etiquettes related to the shaykh and self-perception.
Notice the warning about visiting other righteous people.
2
Understand the danger of visiting others
Grasp why a disciple should not visit other saints during training: it can lead to comparing and losing the shaykh's spiritual flow (madad).
The passage says: 'خوفًا مِنْ أن يرى كرامةً أو خُلُقًا في أحَدِهم لم يره في شَيخه'.
3
Learn about self-criticism
Identify the three practices: thinking ill of oneself, not defending oneself, and seeing worship as defective. Understand how each cultivates humility.
Especially the last point: even one's repentance needs forgiveness.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider how these practices protect the disciple's relationship with the shaykh and foster genuine humility.
They prevent pride and ensure constant turning to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ آداب هذه الطائفة التي يحصُلُ بها الكمالُ: مُلازَمةُ الطهارة، والنَّومُ عليها . وعدَمُ كشْفِ العَورة المُغلَّظة في الخلَواتِ؛ حياءً مِنَ اللهِ ومِنَ الملائكة. ومنها : توقيرُ الكبير والشفقة على الصغير والأرامل والمساكين، بل على جميع الخلق. ومنها: الأدب معَ أَهْلِ العِلْم خُصوصًا خَدَمَةَ الشريعة ومشايخ الطريق؛ فإنهم ورثة الأنبياء. ومنها: أن لا يزورَ أَحَدًا مِنَ الصالحين ما دامَ تحتَ التربية قبل الكمال؛ خوفًا مِنْ أن يرى كرامةً أو خُلُقًا في أحَدِهم لم يره في شَيخه، فيعتَقِدُ في شَيخه النقْصَ فيُحرَمُ مدَدَه. ومنها: سوء الظن بنفسه وحُسْنُه بغيره، حتى يرى أنَّ كُلَّ أَحَدٍ أَحسَنُ منه حالا. ومنها: أنْ لا ينتصر لنفْسِه في أمر. ومنها: أن يرى عبادته دائما قد دخلها الخللُ مِنَ الرياءِ والخواطر الردية، ومِثْلُها يستحقُ عليها العقاب لولا مُسامحة الله تعالى له، فيستغفرَ مِنْ عِبادتِه ومِنَ اسْتِغْفارِه. ومنها: أنْ لا يتكَلَّمَ بكلام العارفين مِنَ الفرْقِ والجمع، والبقاء والفناء ما لم يَكْمُلْ، على أنَّ الأولى للكاملِ تَركُ ذلك، إلا لحاجة تقتضي ذلك. ومنها: مُحاسبة النفس على ما ارتكبته مِنَ المُحَرَّماتِ والمكروهات وفضول المباحات، وعلى ما وقع في نفْسِهِ مِنَ الخواطر النفسانيةِ والشَّيطانية، والاستغفار منها. والفرقُ بَينَ الخاطر النفساني والشَّيطاني: أنَّ الأَولَ: يكون بإلحاح على المعصية أو الشهوة، كالطفل الذي يُلح على أمه حتى تغطيه ما يُريدُ، فيجب قمعها عن ذلك بمُلازمةِ الذِّكْرِ وبيانِ عاقبة هذا الأمرِ والتَّوجه إلى الشَّيخ والثاني: يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِ إلحاح، بل يأمُرُ بالمعصية ويُزَيَّنها، فإن طاوَعَه الشخص، وإلا انتقَلَ لأُخرى؛ لأنَّ قصده الغواية على أي حالة تكون، لا معصية بخصوصها.
And among the etiquettes of this group by which perfection is attained: adherence to purity and sleeping in a state of purity. And not uncovering the severe private parts in solitude out of shame before God and the angels. And among them: respecting the elder and compassion for the young, widows, and the poor, indeed for all creatures. And among them: that he should not speak the speech of the knowers (‘ārifīn) concerning separation (farq) and union (jam‘), and subsistence (baqā’) and annihilation (fanā’), until he becomes perfect; indeed, it is preferable even for the perfect one to leave that except for a necessary need. The second comes without insistence; rather it commands sin and makes it appear attractive. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visiting many saints is always beneficial for spiritual growth.
Correction: The passage warns that during training, visiting other righteous people can cause one to see a virtue absent in one's shaykh, leading to a break in the spiritual flow (madad).

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if a disciple sees a miracle in another saint not present in his shaykh?
A) He becomes inspired
B) He may think his shaykh is deficient and lose his spiritual flow
C) He should leave the shaykh
D) It has no effect

Key Takeaway
Loyalty to one's shaykh and constant self-criticism protect the disciple's spiritual progress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed yourself to compare your teacher or spiritual guide to others, secretly thinking someone else is better? How did that feeling affect your learning and connection? What concrete step will you take today to renew your loyalty and protect that bond?
?
Do you genuinely believe that every person you meet is in a better spiritual state than you? When was the last time you felt superior to someone? Ask yourself: is your self-opinion protecting an ego that needs to be shattered? What will you do the next time you catch yourself thinking you are better?
?
When you finish a prayer or act of worship, do you assume it is accepted and feel satisfied? Based on this lesson, how will you change your attitude after every prayer? Will you now ask forgiveness not only for your sins but also for the act of worship itself? Make a decision right now to do that tonight.

Speech, Self-Accounting, and Thought Discernment
Sanusi lists etiquettes on speech, self-accounting, and thought discernment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dangers of discussing advanced mystical concepts prematurely and how to practice self-accounting, as well as the difference between psychological and satanic whispers.

Core Idea
Self-accounting and discernment of thoughts are keys to spiritual purification.

The path requires constant vigilance over one's thoughts and silence about high states until one is perfected. This lesson explains how to differentiate and deal with inner whispers, and emphasizes the practice of self-accounting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on speech and self-accounting
Read the Arabic and translation for this lesson, which covers restrictions on speech and the practice of muhasaba.
Notice the distinction between psychological and satanic thoughts at the end.
2
Understand the restriction on advanced speech
Grasp why one should not speak about farq/jam‘ and baqā’/fanā’ before perfection, and even then only when necessary.
These are high mystical states that can be misunderstood.
3
Learn the practice of self-accounting
Muhasaba involves examining one's actions for forbidden, disliked, and excessive permissible deeds, and also examining one's thoughts.
It includes seeking forgiveness for both actions and thoughts.
4
Distinguish between two types of thoughts
Analyze the difference: psychological thoughts (insistent, like a child) vs. satanic thoughts (non-insistent, shifting). Identify the remedies for each.
The passage gives specific remedies for psychological thoughts: dhikr, reflection, and turning to shaykh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ آداب هذه الطائفة التي يحصُلُ بها الكمالُ: مُلازَمةُ الطهارة، والنَّومُ عليها . وعدَمُ كشْفِ العَورة المُغلَّظة في الخلَواتِ؛ حياءً مِنَ اللهِ ومِنَ الملائكة. ومنها : توقيرُ الكبير والشفقة على الصغير والأرامل والمساكين، بل على جميع الخلق. ومنها: الأدب معَ أَهْلِ العِلْم خُصوصًا خَدَمَةَ الشريعة ومشايخ الطريق؛ فإنهم ورثة الأنبياء. ومنها: أن لا يزورَ أَحَدًا مِنَ الصالحين ما دامَ تحتَ التربية قبل الكمال؛ خوفًا مِنْ أن يرى كرامةً أو خُلُقًا في أحَدِهم لم يره في شَيخه، فيعتَقِدُ في شَيخه النقْصَ فيُحرَمُ مدَدَه. ومنها: سوء الظن بنفسه وحُسْنُه بغيره، حتى يرى أنَّ كُلَّ أَحَدٍ أَحسَنُ منه حالا. ومنها: أنْ لا ينتصر لنفْسِه في أمر. ومنها: أن يرى عبادته دائما قد دخلها الخللُ مِنَ الرياءِ والخواطر الردية، ومِثْلُها يستحقُ عليها العقاب لولا مُسامحة الله تعالى له، فيستغفرَ مِنْ عِبادتِه ومِنَ اسْتِغْفارِه. ومنها: أنْ لا يتكَلَّمَ بكلام العارفين مِنَ الفرْقِ والجمع، والبقاء والفناء ما لم يَكْمُلْ، على أنَّ الأولى للكاملِ تَركُ ذلك، إلا لحاجة تقتضي ذلك. ومنها: مُحاسبة النفس على ما ارتكبته مِنَ المُحَرَّماتِ والمكروهات وفضول المباحات، وعلى ما وقع في نفْسِهِ مِنَ الخواطر النفسانيةِ والشَّيطانية، والاستغفار منها. والفرقُ بَينَ الخاطر النفساني والشَّيطاني: أنَّ الأَولَ: يكون بإلحاح على المعصية أو الشهوة، كالطفل الذي يُلح على أمه حتى تغطيه ما يُريدُ، فيجب قمعها عن ذلك بمُلازمةِ الذِّكْرِ وبيانِ عاقبة هذا الأمرِ والتَّوجه إلى الشَّيخ والثاني: يكونُ مِنْ غَيْرِ إلحاح، بل يأمُرُ بالمعصية ويُزَيَّنها، فإن طاوَعَه الشخص، وإلا انتقَلَ لأُخرى؛ لأنَّ قصده الغواية على أي حالة تكون، لا معصية بخصوصها.
And among the etiquettes of this group by which perfection is attained: adherence to purity and sleeping in a state of purity. And not uncovering the severe private parts in solitude out of shame before God and the angels. And among them: respecting the elder and compassion for the young, widows, and the poor, indeed for all creatures. And among them: etiquette with the people of knowledge, especially the servants of the Sacred Law and the shaykhs of the path; for they are the inheritors of the prophets. And among them: that one should not visit any of the righteous while he is under spiritual training before perfection, for fear that he might see a miracle or a character trait in one of them that he has not seen in his shaykh, and thus believe his shaykh to be deficient and so be deprived of his spiritual flow (madad). And among them: thinking ill of oneself and thinking well of others, so that he sees that everyone is in a better state than he. And among them: not defending oneself in any matter. And among them: that he should always see his worship as having defectiveness from ostentation and evil thoughts, and that such worship deserves punishment were it not for God's forgiveness, so he asks forgiveness for his worship and for his seeking forgiveness. And among them: that he should not speak the speech of the knowers (‘ārifīn) concerning separation (farq) and union (jam‘), and subsistence (baqā’) and annihilation (fanā’), until he becomes perfect; indeed, it is preferable even for the perfect one to leave that except for a necessary need. And among them: self-accounting (muḥāsaba) for what he has committed of forbidden things, disliked things, and excess of permissible things, and for what has occurred in his soul of psychological and satanic thoughts, and seeking forgiveness from them. And the difference between the psychological thought and the satanic thought is that the first comes with insistence on sin or lust, like a child who insists on his mother until she gives him what he wants; so it must be suppressed by constant remembrance (dhikr), explaining the consequence of this matter, and turning to the shaykh. The second comes without insistence; rather it commands sin and makes it appear attractive. If the person obeys, fine; otherwise it moves to another, because its purpose is misguidance in any form, not a particular sin.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage enumerates the essential etiquettes (ādāb) of the Sufi path that lead to perfection. The first set deals with physical and moral conduct: maintaining ritual purity even while sleeping, covering one's private parts even in solitude out of modesty before God and angels, respecting elders and showing compassion to all creatures, and especially showing proper etiquette toward scholars, particularly the guardians of the Law and the shaykhs of the path, as they are the inheritors of prophets. The second set addresses spiritual discipline: a disciple should not visit other righteous people during his training lest he see in them a virtue absent in his own shaykh, which could cause him to lose the spiritual flow (madad) from the shaykh. One should have a low opinion of oneself and think well of others, never defend oneself, and always consider one's worship as tainted by ostentation and evil thoughts, thus requiring constant repentance—even from one's repentance. One should not speak about advanced concepts like farq (separation) and jam‘ (union), baqā’ (subsistence) and fanā’ (annihilation) until fully perfected, and even then it is better to refrain unless necessary. Finally, one must practice self-accounting (muḥāsaba) for all actions, including forbidden, disliked, and excessive permissible acts, and for psychological and satanic thoughts. A key distinction is given: psychological thoughts pressure relentlessly like a demanding child, requiring suppression through dhikr, reflection, and turning to the shaykh; satanic thoughts are whimsical, tempting different sins, aiming simply at misguidance.

Key Terms
محاسبة النفس
خاطر نفساني
خاطر شيطاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Satanic thoughts are more dangerous because they insist on a specific sin.
Correction: The passage says psychological thoughts are insistent, while satanic thoughts are non-insistent and quickly move to another sin if not obeyed.

Checkpoint
How does a psychological thought (khāṭir nafsānī) differ from a satanic thought (khāṭir shayṭānī)?
A) Psychological thoughts are weaker
B) Psychological thoughts insist; satanic thoughts shift
C) Satanic thoughts are from the ego
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
Discerning between psychological and satanic thoughts and practicing self-accounting are essential for inner purification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your thoughts today, can you recall a thought that was insistent like a child demanding something? How did you handle it? Will you now apply the remedy of constant remembrance and turning to a shaykh?
?
Have you ever assumed that satanic thoughts are more dangerous because they are persistent? After this lesson, how does realizing that satanic thoughts shift and are less persistent change your strategy to resist them starting today?
?
What permissible excesses (fudūl al-mubāḥāt) have you engaged in without accounting for them? Will you commit to a daily self-accounting practice tonight before sleep, asking forgiveness for even these excesses?

Conditions for the Acceptance of Du'a
Enumeration of conditions for du'a acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key conditions that make a supplication acceptable to Allah, including avoiding impossibility, adopting humility, choosing virtuous times, and not constraining Allah's will.

Core Idea
Du'a must be possible, humble, timely, and unconstraining.

When we call upon Allah, our du'a must meet certain conditions to be proper and acceptable. In this passage, the author outlines the essential criteria: it must not be impossible by reason, law, or custom; it should be offered with humility and at blessed times; and it must not impose a specific demand on Allah except in dire need. Let's explore these conditions in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'يجب' and 'ينبغي' to denote different levels of obligation.
2
Identify the conditions
The passage lists several conditions for du'a. Find each one: impossibility (three types), humility, timing, and no constriction.
3
Understand the three types of impossibility
The author explains that du'a must not be impossible by reason (e.g., making three even), by law (e.g., forgiveness for a polytheist), or by custom (e.g., a child without a spouse).
These are examples; the principle is general.
4
Grasp the etiquette of humility and timing
Du'a should be accompanied by humility (dhull) and brokenness (inkisār), and said at virtuous times like pre-dawn and after prayers.
These reflect the proper attitude and optimal moments.
5
Learn the prohibition of constriction (taḥjīr)
Do not demand that Allah fulfill a specific need at a specific time, unless in intense distress like escaping an oppressor.
This respects Allah's absolute freedom and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب أن لا يكونَ بمُمتنع عقلا، أو شرعًا، أو عادةً. وينبغي أنْ يكونَ مُصاحبًا للذَّلَّ والانكسار وأن يكون في الأوقات الشريفة كالأسحار وعقب الصلواتِ. وأن لا يكون فيه تحجير على الله تعالى، كأن يسْأَلَ كَأَنْ قضاء حاجة بخصوصِها في هذا الوَقتِ بعينه مثلا، ما لم يشتَدَّ الكربُ كالخلاص مِنْ ظالم مثَلًا. (٢) يعني أن لا يكون الدعاء بممتنع أي مستحيل عقلاً، كأن تصير الثلاثة زوجا مثلا، أو شرعا كالمغفرة للمشرك مثلا، أو عادة كطلب الولد بغير زوجة مثلا.
And it is necessary that it (du'a) not be impossible by reason, or law, or custom. And it is fitting that it be accompanied by humility and brokenness, and that it be at virtuous times such as the pre-dawn hours and after prayers. And that there be no constriction upon Allah the Exalted, such as asking for the fulfillment of a need specifically in this precise time, for example, unless the distress is intense, like deliverance from an oppressor, for example. (2) Meaning that du'a not be impossible, i.e., intrinsically impossible by reason, like the three becoming an even number for example; or by law, like forgiveness for a polytheist for example; or by custom, like seeking a child without a wife for example.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a list of conditions for du'a. The author first states that du'a must not be impossible (mumtaniʿ) according to three criteria: reason (ʿaql), law (sharʿ), or custom (ʿāda). He then advises that du'a be accompanied by humility (dhull) and brokenness (inkisār), and performed at virtuous times such as the last part of the night (asḥār) and after prayers. Additionally, du'a should not place a constriction (taḥjīr) on Allah, such as demanding a specific need at a specific time unless there is intense distress, like deliverance from an oppressor. The footnote (2) elaborates on the three types of impossibility: by reason (e.g., making three an even number), by law (e.g., asking forgiveness for a polytheist), and by custom (e.g., asking for a child without a spouse). These conditions ensure that du'a is proper and compatible with divine wisdom.

Key Terms
الممتنع (impossible)
الذل (humility)
الانكسار (brokenness)
الأسحار (pre-dawn)
تحجير (constriction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think du'a can ask for anything without restriction.
Correction: The author clarifies that du'a must not be impossible by reason, law, or custom, nor constrain Allah's will.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'impossible by custom' in the conditions of du'a?
A) Asking for something that cannot happen according to natural laws.
B) Asking for something that is forbidden by Islamic law.
C) Asking for something that is logically contradictory.
D) Asking for a child without a spouse.

Key Takeaway
For du'a to be acceptable, it must be possible, offered with humility at virtuous times, and not impose specific demands on Allah except in dire need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you check whether your request is impossible by reason, law, or custom? How often have you asked for something that violates these conditions without realizing it? Will you now pause and reflect before each du'a?
?
Your du'a – is it accompanied by genuine humility and brokenness, or do you approach Allah with a sense of entitlement? What will you change to ensure your heart is in the right state when you raise your hands?
?
Have you ever tried to force a specific outcome from Allah within a rigid timeframe, as if He must answer now? Are you willing to trust His wisdom and timing, even in intense distress, while still asking with urgency?

Concluding Section on Tasawwuf and Closing Praise
The author ends the book with spiritual counsel and final praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author concludes his theological work with spiritual counsel, self-critique, and praise, integrating hadith and poetry.

Core Idea
The author ends the book by turning from doctrine to heartfelt supplication and praise.

This final passage shows the author's concern for spiritual sincerity alongside theological precision. After covering complex theological topics, he concludes with a chapter on tasawwuf, emphasizing that knowledge must lead to action and reliance on Allah. He includes a hadith on mercy, verses from al-Burda confessing his own shortcomings, and a closing praise to Allah for enabling the completion of the book.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening supplication and hadith
Begin by reading the Arabic supplication and its translation. Notice the author's plea for Allah to seize their souls, replace sins with good deeds, and help at stumbles. Then examine the hadith on mercy that follows, which connects the attribute 'O Most Compassionate' to the Prophet's teaching.
The hadith is from al-Tirmidhi and is provided in the footnote.
2
Reflect on the al-Burda verses and self-critique
Read the two poetic lines from al-Burda. The author imitates al-Busiri to express regret for preaching without practicing. Consider why a scholar would include such a personal confession in a book's conclusion.
This shows the importance of sincerity (ikhlas) and humility in Islamic scholarship.
3
Understand the closing praise as gratitude
Read the final supplication and praise. The author explains that completing the book is a favor from Allah, so gratitude is obligatory. Notice how the praise (al-hamd) and salawat on the Prophet and his family serve as a fitting end.
The concluding rhyme (والحمد لله على الإتمام) mirrors the opening of many Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في التصوف
.. واقبض أرواحنا بيدك، وبدل سيئاتِنا حسَناتٍ، وخُذْ بأيدينا عِندَ العَثَراتِ، رَبَّنَا آمنا بها أنزَلَتَ واتَّبَعْنا الرسولَ فاكتبنا مع الشاهدين.
(يا رحيم) أي: يا أرحم الرحماء، فيه إشارة وتلميح إلى قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (الراحمون يرحمهم الرحمنُ تبارك وتعالى، ارحموا مَنْ فِي الْأَرْضِ يَرْحمكم مَنْ فِي السماءِ). ولا يخفى ما في الكلامِ مِنْ حُسنِ الاختتام، هذا وأقولُ متمثلا بقول صاحب البردة:
أستغْفِرُ اللهَ مِنْ قَول بلا عمَل * لقدْ نَسَبتُ به نسْلًا لذي عُقم
أَمَرْتُكَ الخَيْرَ، لَكِنْ مَا ابْتَمَرتُ بِهِ * وما اسْتَقَمتُ، فما قولي لكَ اسْتَقِم؟!
نعوذ بالله مِنْ عِلْم لا ينفع، وقلب لا يخشع، ومن الطمع في غير مطمع. وجهنا إليك مطايا الآمال، فلا تحرمنا لذة الوِصالِ، واحِمِلْنا على مطايا التوفيق، واسلك بنا أنفَعَ طريقِ، إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الجوّاد الكريمُ، الرؤوف الرحيم.
والحمد لله على الإتمام * وأفضل الصلاةِ والسلامِ
على النبي الهاشمي الخاتم * وآلِهِ وصحْبِهِ الأَكارِمِ
ولما كان تأليفُ هذا الكتاب، والإقدارُ عليه مِنْ نِعَم الله تعالى، وكان شُكرُ المُنعِم واجبًا ختمَ كِتابَهُ بِحَمْدِ اللهِ تعالى بقوله (والحمد لله على الإتمام) لهذا الكتاب.
(۱) رواه الترمذي في سننه، كتاب البر والصلة عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، باب لا تنزع الرحمة إلى من شقي، بسنده عن سيدنا عبد الله بن عمر رَضِي الله عنهما.
A concluding chapter on tasawwuf. ... And seize our souls in Your hand, and replace our evil deeds with good deeds, and take our hands at the stumbles. Our Lord, we believe in what You have revealed and we follow the messenger, so write us among the witnesses. (O Most Compassionate) meaning: O Most Merciful of the merciful. In it is an indication and allusion to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The compassionate ones are shown mercy by the Most Compassionate, blessed and exalted is He. Show mercy to those on earth, He will show you mercy who is in the heavens.' And it is not hidden what is in the speech of beauty of conclusion. This, and I say, imitating the words of the author of al-Burda: 'I seek forgiveness from Allah for speech without action; I have thereby attributed lineage to one who is barren. I commanded you good, but I did not follow it; I did not remain upright, so what is my saying to you: be upright?!' We seek refuge in Allah from knowledge that does not benefit, a heart that does not fear, and from greed in that which is not to be desired. We have directed to You the mounts of hopes, so do not deprive us of the pleasure of union, and carry us on the mounts of success, and lead us on the most beneficial path. Indeed, You are the Generous, the Kind, the Compassionate, the Merciful. And praise be to Allah upon completion, and the best of prayers and peace be upon the Hashemite prophet, the seal, and his family and his noble companions. And since the authorship of this book and the ability to do it are among the favors of Allah the Exalted, and thanks to the Benefactor is obligatory, he concluded his book with praise of Allah the Exalted by saying 'And praise be to Allah upon completion' of this book. (1) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi in his Sunan, the book of righteousness and kinship, from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, the chapter on 'Do not remove mercy from the wretched', with his chain from our master Abdullah ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is the concluding section (khātima) of the book, titled 'On Tasawwuf'. It begins with a supplication asking Allah to seize our souls, replace our sins with good deeds, and help us at times of stumbling. It then invokes the attribute of mercy, explaining that 'O Merciful' means 'O Most Merciful of the merciful', linking to a hadith about the merciful. The author notes the beauty of this conclusion. He then recites verses imitating al-Busiri's al-Burda, expressing regret for preaching without practicing. He continues with a series of supplications seeking beneficial knowledge, a humble heart, and spiritual success. Finally, he offers praise to Allah for completing the book, along with salawat upon the Prophet and his family, and explains that he ended with praise because gratitude for divine favors is obligatory. A footnote provides the hadith source.

Key Terms
التصوف (tasawwuf)
الخاتمة (conclusion)
البردة (al-Burda)
الحمد لله (praise be to Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author's self-criticism in the al-Burda verses is false modesty or mere poetic convention.
Correction: The author genuinely intends to remind himself and the reader that knowledge must be accompanied by action, and that even scholars are fallible. His confession is a sincere call to humility and repentance.

Checkpoint
How does the author conclude his book?
A) With a poem of his own composition.
B) With a chapter on tasawwuf and praise to Allah.
C) With a refutation of the Mu'tazila.
D) With a list of his teachers.

Key Takeaway
The author ends his theological treatise with a turn to spiritual practice, showing that right belief must be accompanied by sincere action and gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author ends his book by quoting a line from al-Burda: 'I commanded you good, but I did not follow it; I did not remain upright, so what is my saying to you: be upright?!' Think of one command or advice you frequently give to others—whether in teaching, parenting, or friendship. Where are you personally falling short in that area? What specific step will you take this week to close the gap between your words and your actions?
?
The author supplicates: 'And replace our evil deeds with good deeds.' Is there a particular evil deed or habit you have been struggling with that you have not sincerely asked Allah to replace? Write a short, personal dua right now, using this exact phrase, and resolve to take a tangible action towards abandoning that sin today.
?
The author concludes with praise to Allah for enabling him to complete the book. Recall a recent completion in your own life—a task, a project, or even a difficult day. Have you genuinely thanked Allah for it, or did you attribute it solely to your own effort? Right now, offer a spontaneous prayer of gratitude, naming the specific blessing, and commit to making gratitude a more constant practice.
